THE RED PAGE.
The Red-Headed Girl.
(WITH SOME ALLUSION TO SPHING.)
ISPE AK of life —of love. I sing the Spring
which yet, though dimly seen, stands
smiling at us over drifts of Winter
snow. The Spring, the gentle Spring,
the precious Spring, forever shrined within
the heart of man—its purest heritage from
heaven —of that I sing. Whjr. should we
dream of Death, why fear his sting, when on
the steely sky the rose-bloom glows, and on the
chilly bosom of the Winter’s snow bright, rosy
steps of Dawn appear ?
If the world, as the Kabbalists say, be but an
emanation from the Infinite, anatom in the Adam
Kadmon of the Visible, shall there not be in every
being—-yea, in every atom in the frame of every
being, in the microcosm as in the macrocosm—a
metaphor of Spring ? As Spirit is the Apotheosis
of Matter, so shall not Matter be also Incarnation
of the Spirit? And shall not the red-haired girl
gladden us with her peach-blow cheeks, and
writhing ringlets ravishing her neck of snow ?
She is indeed a metaphor of Dawn and early
Spring, for the hot blood that burns beneath her
snowy skin mantles upon her rounded lips and
throbbing bosom, and blazes there, like the fire of
a polished ruby hidden in its deepest heart, and
flashing forth its lambent radiance to command
the dazzled eye.
Red hair and black, dark eyes and blue, the
universe hath radiance fo’- them all; but some-
where in the heart of AStna burns a fire that
crimsons all the heavens when it chooses. The
azure sky bends down, the violet answers it in
kind ; both are pure and gentle and celestial
of their sort; but on the sky the deep red glows
when morning comes, and underneath the violet
are fires without which all the tendrils of its roots
would wither in a chill and frozen earth. Ye
know it not, oh foolish ones ! the red ray quickens
all the germs of life, and Aphrodite could not rise
above the foamy Wave till warm Aurora, with her
glowing finger, .stirred the surge. The heat of
morning dwelt within the pines of Mount Meru,
else had the D ivas never found the lotus growing
on the fluctuations of the wave. Sweeps, like the
sword of Seraph, through this inert universe of
clods, the red fire of fecundity ; and grasses grow,
and lichens flowers in the Alps, and blood-like
snow lies on Spitsbergen plains, and Iceland flings
the breath of blooms upon the passing wind—all,
all, because the red ray of the sun demands its
homage from the sympathetic worlds.
“ Rosae cordon in ore tua invenio sings the
soul of man—-“in thy mouth the rose’s heart I
A BOOKPLATE, by Norman Lindsay.
find.” Yea, in thy mouth, oh red heart of the
sun ! the world finds fragrance of the rose, and
breath of orange flower.
* * *
Ad-am was “ red-earth,” so the Rabbins say ;
but before Eve came, (the He-va, mother, whom
the Yod had not yet made a fruitful one), there
was Lilith, the golden-haired, and round the apple
on the tree her rosy locks were twined. Oh, fair
Lilith ! red-haired Lilith ! thou broughtest first
to man that passion which is his uplifting—and
his bane ! The serpent snared thee for his own,
and on his high uplifted head, there shone the red
sheen of thy burning, locks. Lilith ! Lilith !
sweet banished one, one strand of thy red hair
was eaten with the apple which our parents stole !
The Devil knew the sweet, the bitter, of that
tasting, oh Lilith, red-haired and warm and
amorous ariiong the virgin buds of Eden’s shady
groves! The Devil knew it, and he knows it yet
—alas ! I know it too; for round my heart, and
round the heart of every man, there is that single
golden hair, burning and chafing and embracing it
with fire. The flaming swords of seraphim point
every way, out from the innocence of Eden:
theirs is the white light, of perfection that only
Brahrn himself can face ; we, poor mortal men and
women of the dusk, must stumble on with Lilith’s
hair about our hearts, and red blood in our veins.
‘ Heard you ever of “golden ” hair, my reader-
gentle or ungentle ? Dreamed you ever of Cleo-
patra on her barge, or Sappho on her rock above
the sounding surf? Have you considered Zenobia
on her throne, or Semiramis the fierce, or Boadicea
the terrible in fight ? And the poisoners, from
Poppsea to her of the Medici, have they ever
becomes visible pictures in yoiir mind ? These
were not dark-haired languishes by still waters,
as you may have thought. They bore the red
crest of the Devil on their heads, and the red hair
of Lilith twined about their hearts ; and the red
nipples which gave suck to their young burnt the
lips of the babes whose red gums bit them.
4- The color of the Blessed Virgin is blue, because
her chaste bosom suckled a God-child; but
Babylon was clothed in scarlet,
and her throne was upon many
waters, where the red sun kissed
the reeds as they bent before the hot
wind from the- desert. There is
philosophy in this. . . .
Lilith was Passion—created when
Adam was ; ethereal, elusive, beauti-
ful. Eve was of Adam’s flesh—his
sexual complement. Eve -first felt
de§ire —touched, as she was, by the
invisible fingers of Lilith —and to
Adam she communicated that sweet
impulse which- makes us all to sin.
The hair—-the red hair—of Lilith
was eaten with the forbidden fruit;
and tnat invisible gossamer of desire
is coiled about the heart of every
man and woman—yes, of-every living
creature, from the protoplasmic
monad to the Russian countess.
And the highest type of Passion, the
most beautiful incarnation of Desire,
was, among the gray-beards of the
schools, as it is among the unlearned
gray-beards now, the Red-haired
Woman.
* ♦ !■ » -
The stern surroundings of all life
demand a complement. Cold calls
for heat; heat makes the zephyr
necessary. In the frozen North, the
red-haired woman burns amid tlie
stiows ; the blue-black tresses of the
tropic maid are tangled by the wafted
winds from purple seas. A red-
haired Venus matches best the
heather of the Highlands, while an
ox-eyed Juno dallies by the wimp-
ling waters of the South. So does
sweet Nature cater to our needs*.
The black hair in the chilling
North would speak of darkness more intense than
that which comes when, from the Arctic rim, the
sun descends to death. The Scalds, the Runes,
the Kalevala and the Eddas tell us how the frozen
world was still and speechless—in the dusk of
death—until the red tongue from the mouth of
Hell was licked across the expanse, and Friga
shook her flaming tresses loose among the clouds.
But the ideal of a languid poet, writing verse in
languid lands, is thus embodied in his glowing
words: “ Her eyes resembled the full-blown
Nymphiea ; her arms the charming stalk of the
Lotus; her flowing tresses the thick darkness of
the Night.”—To paraphrase his verse :
Her eyes the purple Nymphasa approve
Within whose heart a dew-drop sparkles bright :
Her arms the Lotus—metaphor of Love ;
Her flowing tr ;wi. like the robes of Night.
They say that there are few
red-haired women in Boston,
and none at all in Cambridge.
The blue of their stockings
strikes in, and they have a sort
of cerulean satisfaction in their
supremacy over all the dis-
quietudes of life. The Boston
woman’s soul is a sea of
serenity, barely ruffled by the
emotions of the clammy cod-
fish ball and the continual
dropping into it of the baked
bean. She would philosophise
over a lover as if he were a
new combination of Peruvian
bark with calomel and May
apple, and she would micro-
scope the microbes on his
moustache before she would
let him kiss her. The heart
of a Boston virgin is a prehis-
toric trilobite shrined in a
glacial bosom ; and if Death
on his white horse rode through
the back-bay districts he would
meet no evidences of life more
vivid than a brickdust-colored
Spitz poodle. Even gray mules
die therein despair. So Chicago
—although in iniquity, they
say, it almost rivals Boston—
can show up very few of the
red haired girls. The frog-
pond was the nucleus of Bos-
ton, and the foundation of
Chicago is mud; so their
wqmen are sallow-hued and
droopy and web-footed. In
Cleveland and Kansas City the
red-haired woman blossoms as
the rose. She has snap and
vim, vigour and victory, and
can raise the devil when she
will.
* sft *
A red-haired woman can
raise the devil anywhere, for
when she becomes red-headed
hell gets too cold for old Satan
and he comes to her vicinity
for warmth. But it is in Cleveland that the
softest snap i;j the way of a red-haired woman is
to be found. The lake breezes and the Kelly
Island grapes koep her complexion clear, and the
red of her cheeks is like sunlight on the snow. In
all the North there is no other city where the
women are so beautiful as in this Forest City by
the lake. And, except in Kansas City, there is no
place where red-haired women are so plentiful.
But in Cleveland their hair is softer, their necks
more swanlike, their step more springy than else-
where on earth. They are the Liliths of this
modern time, and it is no wonder that the coal-oil
women bring a better price in foreign markets
than the prudes of Boston or the lank Lydias of
New York. Standard Oil is much more lubricious
than watered railroad stocks or the indigo-tainted
suds in which Puritanic stockings have been
washed. The Cleveland women are more plac-
able, they walk better, they sit, more gracefully,
they are softer in the eye-glance than their Kansas
City sisters ; and then they have a twist to the
hips, as they perambulate in the Spring sunshine,
which makes an old man wish he were young
again. The red-haired Cleveland woman is a
strawberry who furnishes her own cream, and 9an
sweeten with her kisses all the bitter things we
have to take in life.
But what shall I say of the red-haired jay-
hawker of Kansas City ? To her, white mules
bow down in reverence, and bald-headed men con-
gregate upon the street corners to snuff the atmo-
sphere as she passes. There are more of the class
ill this town than in any other place I ever visited ;
but, as I intimated before, they lack lubricity.
Their voices are grating, and tlieir hair is harsk
to the touch —it has, in fact, the feel of the brick-
dust which it resembles in color. But they do not
allow themselves to be slighted, by any means.
They have high cheek-bones and prominent chins,
and when one of them fixes a fellow with her
glittering eye he has to surrender or take the
next train for Topeka, where the women have
hair as dark as the Kaw river mud and com-
plexions the color of a Sorosister’s stocking. The
EX. LIBRIS:
A BOOKPLATE, by Norman Lindsay.
Kansas City woman is shrfewd, sharp and progres-
sive ; breezy in her ways as the hill-top town she
lives in ; but she would rather trade corner lots
wit a stranger than play the badger game on
him. Therefore, her red-haired sister never
tangles the wayfarer with a loop of Lilith’s hair,'
but lassoos him with a rawhide lariat. If she
wants him she is going to have him, even if he
does come high. He must surrender or leave town.
The red-haired Kansas City woman, when she
dies and becomes somewhat sublimated and
etherealised, goes to Colorado—which is her Pur-
fatory and the Paradise of the red-headed man.
he sojourns there until until she is sufficiently
spiritualised to be reborn in Cleveland—the
heaven of red-haired women. I spoke of
Colorado ; yes, Colorado is the Paradise of the
red-haired man and the Purgatory of the red-
haired woman. At Denver, at Colorado Springs,
at Manitou, the red-haired woman is much in evi-
dence. The ordinary mail, at those places, is in-
capable ; but the red-headed man is a daisy and a
darling. Somehow, to those of us whose* hearts
are dreamy and whose minds are mystically bent,
the great Rockies, with their snowy breasts and
their libations of the morning sun, speak of
eternity and purity. The man whose hair is
tangled with the sunbeams knows not this ; and
the passionate throbbings of the woman whose
still heart is quivering with the electric voltage
of the air appeal to him. Alas ! there are too
few red-headed men in that land of deceptive-
distances. It is the, Purgatory of the red-haired
woman—the Paradise of the red-haired man.
* * * *
Butin ordinary climates and among ordinary
people the red-hfeaded woman has her. innings,
and is not to be despised. I wish I could quote
what some of the gentle poets of old England say
about her, but I dare not. These sterilised old
virgins and unsatisfied matrons of the Women’s
Clubs would prosecute The Gatling Gun if I did.
They discard me as an “ abandoned old repro-
bate,” while they maunder over Chaucer and Walt
Whitman and Zola and Stinkingwitz, and listen
to cub-preachers’ talk about Marlowe and Ben Jen-
son. I could quote from Marlowe or Robert
Greene or Ben Jonson what would put the printers
of this highly moral engine in the penitentiary if
I chose. And yet, these dessicated people of the
Women’s Clubs who every night press to their
withered breasts, in which the milk of human
kindness has long since curdled, the salacious
Song of Solomon, prate about virtue and sing
their song of “social purity.” The red-haired
girl is quick of temper, and just as prone to kind-
ness. I think Lilith must have been the mother
of Cain, for red-haired women have raised Cain
ever since. But they are forgiving, if you treat
them right; and my_ friend Scott—not Walter
Scott, but one who, if his merits were known,
would be poet laureate of Kentucky—voices my
sentiments in his exquisite but little known
poem:
“ She was angry at me, and the Devil’s own glare
Gave a glint to her eyes and a curl to her hair ;
But I looked at the white of her throat down below,
And I pillowed my head on her bosom of snow
Till the love-light was burning within her soft breast,
And we sank into slumber with lip to lip pressed.”
In other words, when a red-headed woman—or
any other woman, maybe—scolds you, the best
thing to do is to cuddle up to her and quiet her
nerves. The red-haired woman never sulks, and
rarely plays the badger game. The hair of Lilith
which twines about the heart is a bond of Love.
“Lillith, there come sweet symphonies from thee,
Softer than foam from off the surging sea ;
Sweet symphonies that set the heart afire—
Lilith ! Lilith ! grant me my heart’s desire ! ”
J. Soule Smith, in The Gatling Gun (Amer).
For the imaginative illustrator of books there is
in Australia at present the least possible en-
couragement. One sees with surprise, therefore,
the set of pen-drawing to illustrate Boccaccio’s
“Decameron” which ™lr. Norman Lindsay has
executed, and which remain for the present
at N.S.W. Society of Artists’ rooms in
Sydney. The young artist (he is only
21) is a native of Creswick (V.), resident
in Melbourne, and practically self-taught.
Hi 3 incomplete series of some 30 or more draw-
ings contains the finest examples of pen-draughts-
manship of their kind yet produced in this coun-
try. Indeed, one might almost venture to say
that, in its kind, no better work than Lindsay’s
best is being done anywhere. The series is un-
equal ; but some of its pages are masterly.
There are two things which • pen-drawing
can do ; it can imitate, the attitude of life, and
it can interpret the artist’s expression of
life—can state and create. At present Lindsay’s
clever draughtsmanship takes precedence of his
imaginative results. For the workmanship of
them, his best drawings are beautiful. The way
in which he realises the texture of stuffs, the
rotundity of flesh, the contour and poise of bodies
seen nude or under a robe, is a delight to
the eye. Where several figures enter his picture,
as a rule he groups them admirably against a
background no less admirable. He seems to have
steeped himself in the lore of .Boccaccio’s time.
He succeeds least in his faces, which are usually
too posed, too stiff, too monotonous in expression ;
and in his total result, which does not seem to
disengage sufficient spirit, sufficient life. With
rare exceptions, his brilliant technique has not an
equal illustrative value. But twice or thrice—in
the figure of a fencer defending himself, or the
very fine figure of a woman turning in abandon
to her lover (Lidia and Pyrrus) he triumphs.
Mr. Lindsay shows also a number of clever
book-plates, of which two are by grace repro-
duced on this page. The prices of these and
others are 4 or figs.—which includes drawing,
plate, and 500 book-plates.

Q.G., issues a poetical pamphlet—“ The Human
Comedy, or Faust Up to Date ” (Hussey and
Gillingham)—which was written at Parkside in
1808, and (truth to tell) reads like it.

with electricity, which generates heat bypassing through
a resisting atmosphere on its way to a negatively-charged
Earth. Thus the temperature' becomes colder as you
ascend through the atmosphere, because the higher you
go there’s less heat-generating resistance. And the sun-
corona “ flames ” may be anode Or cathode rays of different
intensities, marking various points of charge or discharge.

Charges on the Sale of Wool by Reducing same, and also Abandoning the CONSOLI-
DATED COMMISSION CHARGE, which, we have always contended, unnecessarily
Penalises the Woolgrower, as the BETTER the character of the Wool Grown and the
PRICE REALISED the HIGHER the CHARGE.

Chills
Ever become
chilled through and
through ?
The acute sen-
sation of cold is
followed by; fever-
ishness and aches in
all parts of the body.
Finally the whole
trouble settles in the
throat and lungs in
the shape of a cough.
There is nothing
so bad for a cough
as coughing.
cherry
Pectoral
■will cure these chills and coughs. A
few doses are sufficient at first. More
time is required later on.
Stop coughing and you will get well.
Keep coughing and you invite bronchi-
tis, pueumonia, or even consumption
itself. Better stop your cough at once
and thus end the whole matter.

Drivin’.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
The world has many kinds of trade—
Some hard and others easy ;
An’ some are clean an’ nobly paid,
An’ others cheap and greasy.
The world has many sorts of toil
An’ many people strivin’ —
Some use their brains, some till the soil ;
But I exist by drivin’!
Not drivin’ sheep or lazy cows,
Nor even starvin’ cattle,
Nor yet with foamin’, roarin’ bows
And engine-room a-rattle ;
Not drivin’ four tough, ill-mouthed blades
Of horse-flesh, young an’ borin’ —
But drivin’ sobbin’ up the grades
An’ down with brake-shoes snorin’.
Oh, it’s drivin ’ — drivin’ —
There’s many millions strivin ’ l
Some men sleep aft,
But most men graft,
An’ I am drivin ’ — drivin ’ /
The “ Tank ” is but a poor machine ;
The “Fairlie’s” almost slower ;
The “Fell ”requires some keepin’ clean,
But this machine’s a goer !
The cars may swing or backward fling
Their weight, but still she’ll shift ’em ;
A six-wheel coupled Yankee rig
Is b ailt, I guess, to lift ’em.
It’s not the softest kind of thing—
This population-movin’;
You’ve got to keep one optic aft,
An’ one ahead an’ rovin’;
There’s semyfores to dodge an’ duck
Both goin’ an’ arrivin’,
But every man must trust to luck—
Which same applies to drivin’.
Oh, it’s drivin’ — drivin ’ —
Both goin ’ an’ arrivin’;
I dodge an’ duck
The worst of luck
An’ get there somehow, drivin ’ /
I’ve never heard a loco, sing
Up-grade with black smoke liftin’,
Nor down with lazy, rollin’ swing,
Nor round the bright curves driftin’;
I don’t suppose she’s learnt the way
(Well oiled in every bearin’)
But often, on a greasy day,
I’ve heard some healthy swearin’.
An engine is a thing of steel,
So what’s the use of sayin’
She sings in every mighty wheel ?
As like as not she’s prayin’
For luck to cross the trestle that
Is some darned fool’s contrivin’ . . .
I’ll bet a sovereign to a hat
She’s only drivin’ —drivin’.
Oh, she’s drivin’ —drivin’ —
By reck’nin’ an’ contrivin’
I get her there
With time to spare,
An’ bring her back —still drivin’ I
It’s nice to lose your sleep at night
An’ keep awake all day-time,
To watch the starin’ head-lamp’s light
An’ keep the blessed way-time ;
It’s cheerful-like to risk your bones
Because her belchin’ funnel'
Knocks slack an’ lime an’ bricks an’ stones
From every rotten thnnel.
It’s grand to stand half-roasted and
The other half at zero,
To hold the lever in your hand —
A poet’s bloomin’ hero ;
It’s nice to watch the crowd that’s met
Both leavin’ and arrivin’,
Who rush their friends an’ quite forget
The chap ahead that’s drivin’.
Oh, Pm drivin’ — drivin’ —
There’s many thousan’s strivin ’;
Som.e men drive cores,
Some foamin’ boivs —
I set six wheels a-drivin’!
Now hear the words my loco, speaks—
Slow, rhythmic, yet not singin’—
As by the peaks an’ tumblin’ creeks
Her eighty tons goes ringin’ . . .
“ Man’s life is just two far-stretched rails
With many a curve an’ crossin’;
The levels change, the wide curves range
Up grades an’ down a-tossin’ ;
An’ men must live, an’ men must die,
So what’s the use of growlin’ ?
The “ danger” falls, the whistle calls :
Full Steam ! —an’ take it howlin’.”
These words are cheerin’-like to those
Whose lives are full of strivin’—
The smooth valves gape, the smooth valves
close,
An’ I am drivin’, drivin’.
Oh, it’s drivin’ — drivin’ —
The ivorld’s a place to strive in ;
When cold an’ numb
My hour has come,
Perhaps I’ll go there drivin ’ /
M. L.
QOILP N.
Lady Roberts is very simple in manner,
while her appearance is that of a nice buxom
boarding-house landlady. Before she left to join
the Field-Marshal in South Africa she visited
Kilmainham Hospital. Extensive additions had
been made to the premises, and a big new range
erected. To inspect this latter Laly Roberts,
Iressed in a plain black frock and big white apron
with a bib, tripped out to the kitchen. The work-
men were just preparing to leave when Lady R.
laid to the foreman, “I’m sure you have put
it too far forward, and all the work will have
to be done over again.” Replied the genial work-
man, patting the lady affectionately on the
shoulder, “Oh, no, cookie, just wait till you’ve
cooked a dinner with it and you’ll find it all right,
my dear 1 ”

Special Notice.
The whole contents of this paper are copyright by the
publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
article, tale, sketch, poem, paragraph, illustration, or
any other part of the contents of this number, is hereby
expressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
“ Correspondence ” column all contributions submitted,
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in length,
or, say, 3000 words, are specially acceptable), Paragraphs,
Letters, or Newspaper clippings. All communications
will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when the
address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we
desire those sending MS. to distinctly understand . that we
are not responsible for its preservation or transmission.

or other topical drawings in line or wash, of sufficient

any interesting photograph illustrative of a current event,
will be paid for same on acceptance. Any picture con-
taining a good idea, but unfitted for direct reproduction
— i.e., any picture which has to be re-drawn —will be paid
for as a suggestion only. The conditions under which
such artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.

postage on Interprovincial Newspapers, the annual sub-
scription outside N.S. Wales (except Queensland) is now
£l, with 2s. 2d. added for postage.
No newspaper can be sent unless the subscription is
prepaid. There will be no exception to this rule.

annum, payable in advance.

exacted by the Queensland Post Office, a special Thynne-
paper edition of The Bulletin is how sent to Queensland.
SATURDAY, AUGUST 18. 1900.
The Federal Railway Problem.
The Parliament shall, subject to this Constitution, have
power to make laws for the peace, order, and good
government of the Commonwealth with respect to :
XXXII. The control of railways with respect to trans-
port for the naval and military purposes of the Common-
wealth.
XXXIII. The acquisition, with the consent of a State,
of any railways of the State on terms arranged between
the Commonwealth and the State.
XXXIV. Railway construction and extension in any
State with the consent of that State.—Commonwealth
Constitution, clause 51.
The railway problem was one of the ques-
tions which the Federal Convention shirked
by a half-hearted compromise. At first the
mass of the delegates viewed the idea of
“ handing over our railways ” as sacrilege
and something akin to throwing mud at the
Ark of the Covenant. Later, as they grew
more familiar with it and with each other,
the bogey shrank considerably. The original
idea that all the other five provinces were
bent on robbing “ our province ” of some-
thing or other, and that each State was the
victim of a conspiracy of the five other States
directed against its goods and chattels, and
was itself a member of five separate con-
spiracies, one against each of its five neigh-
bours, faded away as time went on. And in
t he latter days of the Convention the idea of
handing over to the Commonwealth not only
the existing railways, but also the work of
future railway construction, began to be
viewed rather favorably. But the delegates
were still doubtful, and while it was easy to
decide that the railways should be federated,
it was difficult to decide how to do it, so in
the end the Convention compromised by
giving authority to the Federal Parliament
to deal with the matter if the States were
willing. And the problem is so very urgent
that the Federal Parliament will unques-
tionably have to deal with it at an early date.
♦ m • *
The question really resolves itself into
three, each large enough for any ordinary
politician to wreck a reputation upon. They
ire as follows :
(1) Should the Federal Government take over the
existing railway systems and the work of future railway
construction ? It can’t well do one without the other.
For the Federal Government to take over and combine
all the existing lines into one great system, and yet leave
the work of future construction to the States, would
mean that six new separate provincial systems would
immediately start, and instead of the dire confusion of
six railway systems there would be the worse confusion
of seven.
(2) If the Federal Government takes over the existing
railway systems, on what basis should their value be
estimated, and on what terms should the purchase be
effected? s
(3) On what system, if it is possible to devise any sys-
tem, should the work of future construction be carried
out so as to remove any serious imputation of unfair
dealing between States and and portions of States ?
It is impossible to deal with all these ques-
tions in one article, but they may be taken
by instalments.
• • • •
The reasons why the Commonwealth
should take over the railway systems are
overwhelming. There are at present three
distinct gauges among the Australian State
railways—and even this is not inclusive of
sundry toy branches built on a baby-gauge
of their own. Victoria has the sft. 3in.
gauge. N.S.W. lines are on the 4ft. B|in.
gauge. South Australia’s railways are
partly on the sft. Sin. gauge and partly on
one of 3ft. 6in. Queensland and Westralia
have the 3ft. 6in. gauge. So has Tasmania,
but as that province can’t be connected
with the mainland lines its discrepancy
makes no difference. The results are ex-
pensive and remarkable. A train can run
right through from Melbourne to Adelaide,
but not from Melbourne to Sydney, nor
from Sydney to Brisbane. There is a slow,
laborious and expensive process of trans-
ferring passengers and goods at the borders,
and when border tariffs are abolished and
trade is greatly increased in consequence,
this expense and aggravation will be vastly
increased. When railways multiply twenty-
fold, as they will some day, and there are
twenty border crossings for one now exist-
ing, the aggravation and delay and waste of
money will again be vastly increased.
• • • •
The various gauges, too, cause a grievous
waste of rolling-stock and engine-power. If
there was one gauge and one management
for all Australia, then when the harvest had
failed in Victoria and there was little wheat
to carry, the superfluous rolling-stock could
do duty in Westralia, or the Northern Terri-
tory, or Queensland, or wherever there was
a superfluity of work. The harvests of the
various provinces don’t all fall at exactly the
same period, and with one gauge and one
management the rolling-stock which hauls
wool at one season in N.S.W. could haul
grain or sugar elsewhere at other times.
When each province’s rolling-stock is prac-
tically imprisoned in that province, each
province has to keep enough to supply all its
requirements in its own busy season, and
leave much of it to moulder on sidings or in
sheds for the rest of the year. There is a
melancholy lack of convenience and economy
about this system, and yet this is only a
small part of the evil.
* * * *
The diverse gauges also make Australia
very helpless in case of attack. The
great purpose of railways in war-time is to
take troops and supplies as swiftly as pos-
sible to the scene of trouble, to fetch non-
combatants away, and also, if the defence
fails, to fetch the defeated troops away and
with them whatever may be useful to the
enemy. Assuming that Queensland was in-
vaded it may safely be assumed that every
man that S.A., Victoria and N.S.W. could
spare would be at her service, Federation or
no Federation. But under existing condi-
tions there would be more goodwill than
practical aid. The S.A. and Victorian rein-
forcements could get along safely as far as
Wodonga, for there is a continuous sft. 3in.
gauge from Adelaide to that town. Then
much valuable time would be lost unloading
men and properties, and reloading them on
to N.S.W. trains, the Victorian and S.A.
rolling-stock being useless beyond that point.
If the N.S.W. trains were not there—if the
province’s rolling-stock was all employed
carrying its own reinforcements northwards,
and shifting non-combatants away from Syd-
ney and Newcastle to the interior, and doing
other work incidental to the defence of its
own coastal towns, the much-needed rein-
forcements from the South would simply
have to moulder on the Victorian border and
drink beer, while Brisbane took care of itself.
If they and the N.S.W. Contingent did get
as far as the Queensland border, they could
get no further (except very gradually and on
foot) unless Queensland trains were there to
meet them, N.S.W. rolling-stock being of no
further use. If Queensland’s southern line
had fallen into the hands of the enemy, that
province might meanwhile have been com-
pelled to make a bonfire of so much of its
rolling-stock as was penned up on that line.
With a uniform gauge, its cars and locomo-
tives could retreat over the border and help
to bring up men and stores from the south,
but under the present wonderful system it
might be necessary instead to set them on
fire or blow them up at the border to prevent
them falling into the hands of the enemy.
In case of invasion, the three separate rail-
way gauges would paralyse every serious
attempt at defence, by cutting off the pro-
vinces from one another, and blocking every
possibility of rapid movement. To all intents
and purposes they would cut off half or two-
thirds of Australia’s defenders, who are few
enough already. An enemy moving round
the coast in swift vessels could strike at one
place and another, generally keeping ahead
of a defence force which had to connect with
three separate railway systems—all more or
less disorganised by sudden pressure— on its
passage from Melbourne to Brisbane, or vice
versa. A uniform railway gauge is probably
worth a force of 100,000 men for defence
purposes.
• * * *
There is no visible hope or possibility of
agreeing on a uniform gauge except by
putting the railways under one control. If
the N.S.W. one of 4ft. BJin. was accepted
as the standard, then the Victorian lines
would have to be narrowed —a comparatively
cheap matter; and the S.A., Queensland
and Westralian lines would require to be
widened—a matter of some expense. It
would be impossible for each province to
pay its own bill—in that case N.S.W. would
get all the benefits of uniformity gratis ;
Victoria would get them at a cheap rate;
the other provinces would pay heavily. The
bill could not be assessed at so much per
mile of line ; in that case Queensland and
S.A. would pay twice as much per inhabitant
as N.S.W., and yet they would be paying
for a work equally beneficial to the whole
continent. Yet, if it were proposed to
divide the bill according to population, and
the N.S.W. gauge was adopted all round, the
complaint would promptly arise that N.S.W.
paid more for work done in S A. and Queens-
land than those provinces paid them-
selves. Considering that the provinces have
never managed to agree on any important
point involving the outlay of money,
the chances of an agreement, on this one
could find accommodation and to spare on
the point of a needle. A Federal manage-
ment would have none of these exasperating
problems to consider. It would merely have
to decide which gauge should be adopted
throughout Australia, to adopt that gauge,
and to charge the expense of alteration to
the railway revenue of Australia, without
allotting it among the provinces at all.
♦ * * *
This is not a question to be hung up, on
the ground that the time is not ripe, or on
the excuse that public opinion is not ready
for the change. The necessity for a uniform
railway gauge may not be urgently felt now,
but, in years to come, when there are a
dozen lines crossing the N.S.W.-Queensland
border, and as many crossing the Victoria-
N.S.W. border, and N.S.W. connects in two
or three places with S.A., and there are 30
separate places, instead of only two, where
the break of gauge wastes time and labor and
money daily, it is certain that the necessity
will be felt. And it is better to make the
reform cheaply when there are, perhaps,
10,000 miles of line to be altered, even
though the time may not be technically
“ ripe,” than to do it at ten times the cost
when public opinion is ready for the change
and there are, perhaps, 100,000 miles of rail-
way to be dealt with.
* * » •
But however important it is to put existing
railways under Federal control, it is at least
equally important to place the work of
future construction in the hands of the
Commonwealth. In railway construc-
tion matters separate provincial manage-
ment is a burden too great to be borne.
The difference can be expressed in a sen-
tence. The object of an Australian railway
management would naturally be to con-
nect every place which seemed to promise
a profitable railway business with the nearest
seaport city in Australia —the one with
which it could be connected at least cost,
and to which its products could be carried at
least expense. A really Australian manage-
ment would naturally do this to keep down
its own construction bill, and also to
develope trade for its own benefit; trade
can’t be developed by dragging a ton of oats
500 miles, at great expense, when it could
find as good a market 250 miles away, at
half the outlay for freight. The object of
separate provincial management is to give
every place which seems worth a railway
access to the nearest seaport city in that
proving That city may be 500 miles away,
and there may be an equally good one in
another province only 250 miles distant, but
the cast-iron rule holds all the same. Many-
places are denied a line, to the great loss of
the country, because a railway won’t pay by
reason of the distance to “our ” capital, and
though it would pay to connect them with
another and a nearer capital such a thing is
contrary to provincial policy. Othersget aline
twice as long as is necessary in order to bring
their trade to “our ” capital; and by reason
of the heavy freights involved through need-
less miles of haulage it does them little
good, and the line being a failure through
the overwhelming expense of this needless
haulage it involves a heavy loss to the State,
and thus the system is a disaster all round.
How many millions of good Australian bor-
rowed money have been fooled away by this
parochial policy it is difficult to guess now,
and how many places have lost their chance
of prosperity through it is not pleasant to
think of.
• * * •
Furthermore, if railway control is left in
the hands of the States they will have to
separately borrow the money for construc-
tion, and as their credit will be inferior to
that of the Federal Government they will
have to pay more for the money. There-
fore the railways will cost more—a very
small difference in the interest rate, accu-
mulated for a century or two, would easily
end by making the new railway built under
State control cost twice as much as the same
line built under Federal management.
Again, if State management is perpetuated
the Federal capital and territory will be in
the ridiculous position of depending on the
goodwill of some adjacent State or States
for communication with Australia at large ;
if it is surrounded by N.S.W. territory,
N.S.W. can refuse it any direct connection
with Brisbane, Melbourne or Adelaide,
and give it a line to Sydney only,
thus securing all the trade. This
would indeed be a curious situation for any
supreme Government to occupy. And, lastly
and above all, there can be no real
Federation of interest and sentiment if the
internal commerce of the Commonwealth is
divided by five conflicting interests, and five
hostile railway construction policies maintain
a state of civil war in the interior of the
Union —or the Commercial Disunion, as it
should be called under such circumstances.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Faying the Bill; an Address to the
Cornstalk Jingo.
The New South Wales finance committee, in the
course of its report on the lamentable monetary
affairs of the province with the biggest deficiency
in Australasia, made one suggestion which The
Bulletin prognosticated several months ago. It
didn’t prognosticate that the finance committee
would make the suggestion, or that it would be
made by anyone in particular—simply that it was
bound to arise from some source or other. The
finance committee proposes that the cost of the
Transvaal Contingent up to the end of the finan-
cial year just closed shall not be charged to the
current year’s revenue, and, presumably, that the
rest of the bill shall not be charged to next
year’s revenue, but that it shall be put quietly
away into the floating debt and left there to be
liquidated on some future occasion. On the
strength of this precedent it seems fairly probable
some one will also rise to suggest that the cost of
the Chinese Contingentshall likewise go peacefully
into the floating debt, to be paid on some future-
occasion. Even if the finance committee’s pro-
posal to raise the annual appropriation towards,
paying off the Cornstalk deficit from £150,000 t0£400,000
£400,000 a year is adopted, and providing there
are no more deficits, this means postponing pay-
ment for 10 or 12 years, by which time many of
the old Jingoes who hollered for the war will be
dead and gone, and a new generation will have
grown up to find the money. So far, not one
manly Jingo has arisen to object to this proposi-
tion, and unless they do arise every Jingo of the-
crowd deserves the contempt of whatever honest
men there are in the community. A few months
ago Cornstalk Land was ablaze with the
red flags and red streamers and waving umbrellas
of the people who wanted to “ make sacrifices for
the Empire.” Well, a small body of men went to-
fight for the Empire, and so far as their pay and
sundries were a fair remuneration for their
risks they didn’t make any sacrifices. But,,
leaving them out of the question, the
great bulk of the people didn’t go to-
fight for the Empire, and the only way they
can do anything for the Empire is by manfully
paying the bill themselves —not leaving it to their
children or to chance. And there should be no
doubt about the personal character of the
payment. If they meet the bill by econom-
ising on the road vote and leaving a bigger bill for
the repair of the highways to be paid hereafter,
or by saving on some other ordinary vote and
making the difference good out of loans, they are
shirking it just as meanly as if they didn’t pay it
at all. If they pay it by cutting down civil ser-
vice salaries, or not granting promised increases;
or by starving the education vote and thus injuring
the chances and prospects of the coming genera-
tion ; or by paring down the charitable vote and
thus making the sick and poor and aged stand the
cost of their .Jingoism ; or by postponing old-
age pensions when these might otherwise have'
been granted, and thus again screwing the cost of
their “patriotism” out of the misery of the poor
and aged; if they do any of these things, then;
they are again meanly shirking their responsibility..
In view of the fact that the ordinary N.S.W..
revenue is admittedly barely sufficient for ordinary
expenditure, the only manly and honest way
to. “make sacrifices for the Empire” is to
levy a temporary war-tax and make the sacrifices
right off. The Bulletin, much as it opposed the
war, advocates this, and is ready to pay its share
of such a tax, but in the absence of any signs of
support it seems that this wholly non-Jingo paper
is the only honest Jingo of the lot. It isn’t a case-
now of subscribing to a Patriotic Fund (which,
at the best, didn’t amount to more than a frac-

London, July 26.
The Kaiser, in bidding farewell at Bremenhaven yester-
day to the German troops ordered to China, said : “ Your
mission is to avenge thedeathof the Ambassadors and Euro-
peans who have been murdered in Pekin and elsewhere.
When close to the enemy remember that no quarter is to
be given, and that no prisoners are to be taken. Use
your weapons so that for a thousand .years to come no
Chinaman will dare to look at a German askance. Pre-
pare the way for a Christian civilisation. Good-bye, com-
rades.”—Recent Cable.
Lord God of hosts, the heathen rage,
And bring to scorn the sacred page.
But, tho’ they will not bow to Thee,
By My mailed fist! they shall to Me.
It often makes Me feel quite riled,
To see Thee treating them so mild.
And how the heathenist Chinee
Gets over Thee, gets over Me.
I know in our affairs of State
We rulers see some things too late ;
But sin Thou passest, or don’t see,
Will never pass below with Me.
So now I’ve girded up my loins,
Sent out My men, My ships, My coins,
With guns beyond the Yellow Sea
To civilise for Thee and Me.
Thou soon shalt see the heathen die,
No living Hung or Chang or Li
Will there be left to disagree
With what is taught by Me and Thee.
A few may climb the golden stair,
But most of them will go elsewhere,
And bow to Nick the stubborn knee
• That would not bow to Me or Thee.
When by the gun sweet peace is won,
I’ll ship them Bibles by the ton,
And take them out in silk and tea
For Christian folk in Germany.
I’ve told My men to see Thee through,
They know just what they have to do ;
But when the nations all praise Me
I’ll alter it to We, Thou’lt see.
MILLS WOOD, S.A.

tion, comparatively speaking); that was a matter
of advertisement and notoriety, and the value of
the subscriptions came back in advertisement. It
is a case of meeting the real expense—meeting it
as a matter of duty, and getting no more kudos
out of it than by settling for the income-tax or
the dog-tax. As regards 99 per cent, of the
prominent Jingoes—they didn’t fight themselves,
and if they are not willing to pay promptly and
fully then their alleged British patriotism is a lie,
and their alleged enthusiasm for the empire is
shoddy, and their alleged loyalty is sham,
and they are things of gas and bunkum.
The Jingoes who kept their own skins in safety,
and who are now apparently quite prepared to
keep their cash in safety also by leaving posterity
to stand the expense, deserve the unutterable con-
tempt of every decent white man of whatever way
of thinking. They enjoyed the excitement, the
drink, the banquets, the flag-flapping, and the
heroics, and whatever was to be got out of the
business. They secured the profits on the sale of
horses and provisions and shoddy saddlery. They
made money exporting victuals to South Africa,
and they talked at appalling length about their
own love for the Mother-land, and their readiness
to make sacrifices for it, and their eagerness to
help bear the burden of empire. Yet it now
begins to look as if what they really meant was
that they loved the Mother-land and were quite
willing that their children should make sacrifices
for it. British Jingoism was at least respectable.
It commenced honestly by levying a big war-tax
on itself with hardly a dissentient voice. It
couldn’t all fight, but the part that didn’t fight
was at least prepared to shoulder the dreary, in-
glorious burden of footing the invoice, and byway
of showing its sincerity it commenced at once.
N.S.W. Jingoism is in danger of proving mere
beer-froth.

The Foolish Victorian Licensing' Law.
Nathaniel Levi, M.L.C., is trying to do Victoria
a good turn with a Bill providing that a license
may be transferred from one public-house to
another in the same district, provided the new
house is much better than the old. Under the
foolish law fixed on Victoria in the days when a
teetotal Ministry lay like a keg of cold tea on that
province’s chest, a license can’t be transferred to
any place more than a certain very small number
of feet distant from the old original pub. It was
in the wealthy days that this was enacted. Just
when the silly law was passed, dozens of owners
of old, mouldered cockroach-haunted wooden pubs,
in country towns were building fine new stone
or brick premises with a view to shifting their
businesses and pulling down the superannuated
shanties. The law shut down like a steel-trap on
any transfer of the license, except when the new
place happened to be just alongside the old one,
so in many cases the new hotel is a sly groggery,
while the licensed business is carried on in a
wooden wreck which has ceased to be an hotel
because no boarder vyill stay there, and is only a
dirty shed used for drinking purposes. The best
example of how the new stroke of genius worked
that the writer ever met was at a little town
on a branch line in the N.W. district. A fine
large, luxurious brick hotel was being built when
the intelligent statute stepped in and prevented
the owner getting a transfer of his license from
his old wooden crate down the road. The new
place was l|ft. too far away from the old one, or
something to that effect. So he kept on the old
Elace to save his license, and the new one because
e couldn’t sell it for a butter-factory or a lunatic
asylum, and people stay at the new place, and
walk down to the old one for a drink, and carry
their spare drink back with them in their pockets.
Nobody stays in the old place except the drink-
seller, and a dog, and some cockroaches and rats,
and a gopher or two, and a large centipede, and
three worms and a blow-fly. The owner would
gladly put a fire-stick into it and remove a long-
standing eyesore if only he could remove his
license to the new establishment ; but the sapient
law says he mustn’t. And all over Victoria simi-
lar places are to be found. Nat Levi, M.L.C., as
a member of a House that understands good liquor
and likes to have its drink comfortably, has struck
a valuable idea.
The “Aged and Infirm Worker.”
A hunk from a Maoriland paper—about the five-
billionth similar hunk that the minimum-wage
idea has evolved from the Tory press of Austra-
lasia :
Mr. G. W. Russell told the House some unpalatable
truths the other night concerning the effect of our labor
legislation, and not the least effective of these home-
thrusts was the statement that while we have fixed the
minimum wage we are making it more difficult for
middle-aged and elderlv men to earn a living, for surely
it stands to reason, as Mr. Russell pointed out with some
force, i hat an employer, being compelled to pay a certain
wage, will invariably give the preference to strong and
energetic young men most capable of earning it, while
middle-aged and elderly men will be passed over and left
in idleness. This was certain to be, the effe t of the
wage law, just as certain as the day ’follows the night.
There is no use closing our eyes to the ugly fact t at old-
fashioned sentiment is gradually being squeezed out of
the relations between employers and employes by the
legislation of the hour, and who can blame the em-
ployer if he takes the trades-unionist at his word and
reduces the whole matter to the sordid considera-
tion of the maximum quantity of work for the legal
wages ? This is what he is doing now, and what the law
is compelling him to do. The wage in every trade is
being assessed upon the quantity of work that a young
and vigorous mechanic can do in a given time. No one
contends that the middle-aged or elderly man can do the
same, and, being unable to do it, does it not follow that
he will invariably be passed over wherever the employer
has a choice, and the work given to the younger man ?
The most cruel and tyrannical part of the wage law is its
effect in preventing elderly men from taking work at the
wage they can earn, and thus sacrificing them and their
livelihood to younger and more active workers. It is too
soon yet for this inevitable consequence of the maximum
or minimum wage to make itself felt. But the day is
close at hand when the hardship it entails will compel a
demand from the elderly workers for the relaxation of
this law, in order that they may honestly get bread to
eat.
It is curious how many Tories are grieved to their
inmost soul over the minimum wage—not lest
it should make them pay a decent price for labor,
but lest it should lead to the elderly and infirm
person being deprived of his employment. And
that elderly and infirm person is mostly a foolish
lie. He never got any great show even in the
worst of the sweating days when his boss could
get a younger and stronger man. He has a better
show in Maoriland now than he ever had before,
for these reasons : (1) The number of persons em-
ployed in the province’s industries has increased
so very greatly under the influence of Protection
and enlightened Labor legislation that there is a
far smaller margin of unemployed, and the elderly
worker has to be engaged to a greater extent to fill
the demand ; the writer can remember a time in
Maoriland, before the Protection and Labour legis-
lation era, when labor was so plentiful that em-
ployers could reject every man above 45 and still
find more men than they required, and in those days
the elderly one got employment only as a semi-
charitable matter and at any wages which the
employer liked to name. (2) Before he becomes
elderly (no man is born elderly) he now gets
sufficient employment and at wages which enable
him to make some provision for his old age;
formerly he didn’t. (3) When he becomes elderly
if he chances to get employment he now gets it at
a living wage, which he formerly didn’t. (4) He
has an Old Age Pension to fall back upon which
wasn’t the case in the good old Tory days, and
Maoriland has prospered so under Protection and
Labour legislation that it is able to pay these
pensions without extra taxation! Also the old-
fashioned sentiment between employers and em-
ployed never did exist to any extent worth
mentioning, so the loss of it isn’t worth
grieving over. Nowadays the aged person
is certainly more in evidence than he used
to be, but that is not because he was better
paid or better fed or better employed under
Tory Government in (say) 1886 than he was
under Liberal government in 1896. There was
simply less of him. The Maoriland people aged
55 and upwards numbered 30,869 in 1886 and
60,387 in 1896. It is the same in every province.
And because more aged inhabitants are now
visible in the streets through their being more of
them in existence, the impression arises either
that Liberal legislation makes people older, or
else that the old man in the times when the Tory
party bossed the show was concealed in factories
where he was too busy earning huge wages to
come out and show himself. And there is. simply
more of him—partly because that is the natural
state of things and partly, it may be, because he
now gets enough food to keep him alive longer.
A Land Resumption Question.
Sydney Sunday Times raises a very pertinent
question :
One point in connection with the wharfage resumption
scheme is very necessary to bear in mind, which is that
every care must be taken that the citizens are not com-
pelled to pay twice over for the improvements effected by
the Government.
What is meant by this will readily be perceived when it
is recollected to what an enormous extent the value of the
adjacent property will be improved, and that unless
adequate steps are taken by the Government to protect
the public, the landowners will not only be paid directly
for the resumed property, but will be placed in a position
to extort from future occupiers perhaps twice as much as
the original value in consequence of the enhanced rentals
which will be obtainable for premises in proximity to the
resumed area.
An English publication recently gave some striking in-
stances of the way this has worked in the case of several
towns and cities where large public improvement works
have been carried out. Liverpool affords one striking
example. The ground upon which some of the docks
there were built was but a few decades ago merely a
piece of waste sand, in which rabbits burrowed, but when
the new docks had been erected at public expense the
value of the adjoining land was so greatly enhanced
that its rental value is calculated at £40,000 a
year. Another instance is that of the town of Bootle,
which has now a rateable value of £9 per head of the
population, the highest in England or Wales. This is
simply due to the fact that the town has raised £330,000
to carry out public improvements, and the single land-
lord who owns it has raised his rent exactly in proportion
as the ratepayers paid to increase the value of his pro-
perty. Thus while in 1879 the amount of rates in Bootle
was £9OOO, the ground rents were only £IO,OOO. In 1398
the rates amounted to £94,000 and the ground rents to
£IOO,OOO.
The meaning of this latter example is that, if the
State or tne municipality had owned Bootle
instead of it being owned by a private landlord,
the huge sum spent on improving the town would
all have been paid off in a few years by the in-
creased rentals which the expenditure of this
money created. It is for just this reason that
The Bulletin advocates, seeing that N.S.W. has
gone into the Sydney resumption business, the
resumption of all Sydney and suburbs by the
State, the payment of the landlords in State
securities, and an enactment that the State shall
never again sell the land, but shall be the sole
owner of the site in perpetuity. Then it could set
about the gradual reconstruction of the crooked
metropolis—all of it, not merely a small fraction—
and the increased ground rentals it created would
gradually pay for the reconstruction. Also, for
the same reason, The Bulletin argues that the
Federal Government should be sole ground-land-
lord of the Federal capital and the adjacent terri-
tory for ever—so that the land-values created by
the expenditure of the people’s coin will be the
people’s property. And by exactly the same
process of reasoning it holds that the Federal ter-
ritory should consist of 5000 square miles instead
of 100—that the unearned increment created by
the expenditure of the people’s money may not
slop over a too-narrow boundary on to the private
owner’s possessions.

Alfred Deakin’s Proposal.
Alfred Deakin said something that wanted say-
ing very badly indeed when he told the Victorian
Assembly last week that a Convention should be
elected by the people to draft a new State Consti-
tution for Victoria, and that the new Constitution,
when drafted, should be submitted to the people
for acceptance or rejection by Referendum. Hig-
gtns, of Geelong, started the debate by moving
that the Upper House needed reform ; but Hig-
gins, of Geelong, came forward with no definite
proposal for bringing the necessary pressure to
bear on the 48 Rich Landlords. Df.akin struck
the true note when he said that the.whole Consti-
tution needed remodelling on thoroughly demo-
cratic principles, and on a smaller scale to suit the
new conditions arising through Federation ; and
that the people, in view of the great success they
had made over the Federal Constitution, should
be entrusted with the work. And then, when
only four members had spoken, the subject
was dropped for rather over a month to allow
Bennett, M.L.A., to talk about the neces-
sity for a committee to inquire into the
dismissal of a railway-porter. It was a pitiable
falling of the biggest and most urgent subject in
Victorian politics. Just now, if there was only a
Kingston at the head of the Ministry, there is a
great and unique opportunity to rouse the people in
the matter of Council reform. An Enabling Bill
authorising the election of the proposed conven-
tion and the taking of the Referendum could be
drafted in a few hours, and passed by the As-
sembly in a few more, and thrown out by the
Council in less than 30 minutes. Then the
Assembly could go to work by itself/ as S.A.
Assembly did under Kingston’s guidance over
the S.A. Council Reform scheme. The Ministry
could announce that the proposed Convention was
going to be called without an Enabling Bill—
there is no law against the people electing any
Convention they please. Nominations could be
called for ; the ballot for the election of delegates
to the Convention could be taken at the As-
sembly’s approaching General Election, and the
Convention could meet and draft the new
Victorian Constitution. And when it was
drafted it could be submitted to the people by
Referendum. The only illegality would lie in
paying the expenses of these proceedings out of
the public revenue. If the Council would not
authorise that, which it probably wouldn’t, the
delegates would have to be unpaid and to receive
no allowance for expenses, and the cost of ballot-
papers, &c., would nave to be defrayed by passing
round the hat. By taking the election of dele-
gates and the election of Assembly members
together, and taking the Referendum and the
election of Federal members together, the ex-
pense could be kept down to a minimum. The
best Liberals in Victoria could be got to sit on
such a Convention, and give it their assistance.
And when a new Constitution was drafted, and
accepted by an overwhelming majority of the
people, and passed by the Assembly, then if the
Council rejected it there would be material for a
storm which would blow the Rich Landlords off
their decrepit legs when the next instalment of
them went before their constituents for the usual
walk-over. Providing the thing was done as
Kingston would do it, it would rouse a burst of
enthusiasm that would be the beginning of the
end for the 48 Rich Landlords. But if it was
done in a perfunctory way by a cold and unbeliev-
ing cattle-salesman, it would be such a farce that
it were better not attempted.

Emancipating the Publican.
The combined brewery interest of New South
Wales will doubtless fight hard against the propo-
sal of Watson, M.L.A., to abolish “tied ” public-
houses, and the public-house interest will probably
fight as openly as it dare in favor of the abolition.
That is, it will fight when it thinks the sleep-
less eye of the brewery isn’t upon it. In a coun-
try which objects, in theory at least, to slavery,
the proposed abolition of the tied-hotel system
should be greeted with enthusiasm. It is a
monstrous doctrine that because one publican
borrows money from a brewer, therefore, in
addition to being personally bound to pay in-
terest and principal in full, not only he but
every other publican who comes after him in
that house, so long as it exists, should be com-
pelled to buy his liquor off that brewer only, no
matter how exorbitant the price or how un-
speakable the quality. A system under which a
publican can sell not only himself but a long
row of publicans who came after him into com-
mercial serfdom is an anomaly on the edge of
the 20th century. It is also monstrous that the
publican should be legally punishable for selling
bad liquor when the law well knows that he
is compelled to take any liquor, however
bad it may be, that the brewery slave-
owner likes to send him. It would be
equally monstrous if he wasn’t punishable for
selling bad liquor. It is a grievous wrong to the
public stomach that, in many successive streets of
public-houses, it may not be able to get a drink of
good beer, because, when whole streets of pubs,
are tied to one or two big breweries, these breweries
are in no way compelled to take the trouble to
make decent liquor. The average man will drink
bad beer rather than walk a mile or two for better,
and if the law is so fixed that a few brewers of in-
ferior liquor can shut out all concoctions that are
better than their own, they have no motive for
trying to improve their brew. Everything is com-
pirative; and however bad beer may be, it will
sell if the vendor can prohibit the supply of a
better article. Further, the law professes to pro-
hibit any man holding more than one public-house
license, and the legal decisions which ignored and
over-rode this law by letting a brewer hold 150
licenses, if he could get them, on the dummy
system, is a thing that calls for all the dis-
respect the community has about it. In the
interests of law and freedom and good beer,
and of the national stomach and the public liver,
kidneys, and brain, the tied-house system calls
loudly to be abolished, with contumely and with-
out compensation. When the brewers invented
it they didn’t pay any_ one for the use of their
new money-making device, and there is no reason
why any one should pay them for the loss of it.

The Sympathy of the 48 Rich Landlords.
Apparently the Victorian Tory is taking steps
to square himself with the electors, in view of the
Federal elections. Sargood, Wrixon and Agar
Wynne are now sponsoring a Compensation for
Accidents to Workmen Bill before the House of
41 Rich Landlords. It is not very large or violent
or revolutionary in itself, but it is wildly revolu-
tionary compared with some previous attitudes of
the Upper House. It applies to railway, factory,
mine, quarry and engineering employes, and those
engaged on buildings over 30ft. in height. What
the workers in other branches of employment have
done (including those on buildings of or less)
that they should be refused compensation lor
damage is one of the subtle mysteries known only
to the 48 Large Proprietors. The employer shall
not be liable unless the injury causes disablement
for at least two weeks. Contracting-out is per-
mitted, but only provided the Registrar of Friendly
Societies is satisfied that accident is provided
against by a scheme not less favorable than that of
the Act. The scale of compensation is not lavishly
generous, though it might easily be much worse.
For death—the workman leaving others wholly
dependent on him—compensation is to equal three
years’ wages, but may not be less than £l5O nor
more than £3OO. For total disablement, 50 per
cent, of this amount —which is wonderfully illogi-
cal, for a totally disabled man can earn no more
than a dead one, yet he has to be fed, clothed,
housed and attended, while the dead one hasn’t.
Also, the dead man’s life insurance (if he has any)
is received by his family ; the disabled one’s pre-
miums have to be paid. Total permanent disable-
ment should mean more compensation than death—
not less. Then for partial disablement 50 per cent,
of the victim’s wages during a certain varying
period. If he leaves no dependents—in the case
of death —£10 is to be the limit allowed for
medical and funeral expenses; so the bill of the
man of sorrows and weepers will have to be some-
what brief. It is a respectable little Bill, which
might be much larger, yet which might be ex-
pected, considering whence it comes, to be a great
deal smaller.
Gentleman One.
Judge Hood spread himself largely in Melbourne
last week in the matter of a Gent. One. Said his
Honor: “I am satisfied that many years ago
(before 1890, when the Court ordered him to pay
up), the solicitor misappropriated his client’s
money, and has evaded payment ever since, and
now sets up a sham defence. . . . Morally, it is
theft. . . . The Court is bound to be severe when
such improper conduct is detected.” The solicitor
was thereupon suspended, but at the expiration of
five years, provided he has meanwhile settled with
the complaining clients, he is given permission to
apply to be reinstated. It will be observed that
this misappropriating solicitor had for 10 years
disregarded and flouted the Court’s previous order
to pay up, and the Court took no notice of it
until the defrauded client moved in the matter.
The defrauded client couldn’t move earlier, being
a minor and in the hands of a trustee. Judge
Hood, being in talkative humor, also observed :
“I have seen it stated that a lawyer who uses his
client’s money practically escapes where another
man would go to gaol. The very reverse is the
truth. Where the conduct complained of is not
criminal, the layman would escape while the
lawyer would be punished. And where the con-
duct is criminal, lawyer and layman are alike
enmeshed, while the former is, in addition, de-
prived of his means of living.” So the lawyer is
enmeshed, is he? Well, The Bulletin has a notion
that any layman whom his judge was “satisfied
had appropriated another person’s money, en-
trusted to him,” would very suddenly find himself
inside a Victorian gaol (he wouldn’t get ten years’
grace), and would also be deprived of his then
means of living—his billet, or situation, or prac-
tice. Further, the police would make it very
difficult for him to find another billet when he
came out of gaol (though that is by the way). The
fact is that if a man be a gentleman by Act of
Parliament he can misappropriate trust-moneys
and go practically unpunished. In many cases,
when he comes to that, he has no practice worth
speaking of to lose, and even being “struck off”
is a mere formality. The defaulter in ques-
tion was admitted by Judge Hood to be practi-
cally penniless ; his practice having left him, his
profession is, for the time being, of no use for
making a living, and he loses nothing by losing it.
He is deprived of a shadow. If he pay up he will
be reinstated in due course, but if his practice is
worthless will he pay up to get it back ? And
think of a defaulting cashier being simply ordered
to cease being a cashier, and getting 15 years in
which to refund the shortage, with no prospect of
punishment if he doesn’t refund except that he
will still have to cease being a cashier ! And ex-
lawyer Hoop has the cool assurance to say that
Gent. One is treated more harshly than the
common, ordinary person !

Local Jingo politicians are beginning to slowly
realise what asses they showed themselves over
the Transvaal contingent business ; but the word
is “Keep it quiet! Don’t say anything!” The
net result of tne war frenzy is that we have ex-
ported to Africa at enormous expense several
thousand first-rate settlers—of whom “ a very
large number ” (says the Sydney Telegraph cor-
respondent, Aug. 9) “ have already volunteered to
remain as permanent constabulary,” with the
bribe of 10s per day and upwards. Lond. Times
correspondent at Pretoria (June 20) backs this up—
A great number of Australians have been greatly struck
by the possibilities of the country for stock-raising and
fruit-growing, and many have determined to settle.
Meanwhile, the sick and crippled are coming back
to Australia. If the Lyne Govt., the Philp
Govt, and the rest, had deliberately set
themselves to injure this country for the bene-
fit of British Africa, they could have devised
no better means than “the Transvaal contingents.”
And it seems the exportation of able-bodied men,
the bone and sinew of Australia, is not yet
finished. The Bendigo Miners’ Association has
received a letter from a S’African engineering
firm, asking for 3000 miners to work in Rhodesia
and elsewhere at from 20s. per day upwards.
Mechanics and engine-drivers are required at 30s.
to 40s. per day, and it is said that 10,000 men are
wanted altogether. The worth of the guarantee be-
hind this wages-bait does not appear ; and there is
apparently no guarantee of permanent work ; but
the money looks tempting. If they go, it will be a
big blow to the prosperity of Australia —a fine
result of the doctrine of “ Australia a recruiting-
ground for the Empire.” The Bulletin is glad
to see that the Bendigo A.M.A. counsels caution.
Rhodesian promises are very thin pie-crust.
Though the Law mendaciously boasts that it
holds all men innocent till they are proved guilty
it takes all manner of care that, once convicted,
the subsequent paths of rectitude are well pro-
vided with thorns. A marine-stores collector,
summoned to North Melb. Court recently to show
cause why his license should not be cancelled on
account of his having been convicted of larceny,
urged that he had held the said license for the last
three years with credit, and that the only convic-
tion against him was away back in ’93. The Bench
postponed the matter for two months in the hope
that it would be carefully forgotten meanwhile.
The law, be it observed, says that a man convioted
of larceny cannot hold a marine-store license.
That seems to mean that when a man who holds
such a license is convicted he shall thereby lose the
license—be struck off the rolls, so to speak. But
in this case the man was convicted of larceny in
1893. In 1897 the law decided that he was a good
enough person to be a rag-and-bone dealer and
johnny-all-sorts. He ran his business honestly till
1900. And then the law suddenly rose up in great
arrears of wrath and demanded that his license
should be forfeited for something done four years
before the license existed. Why the law’s memory
was so short in 1897 and so long in 1900 is not ex-
plained. If the rag-and-bone dealer could afford
to go to the Full Court and hire Isaac Isaacs to
conduct the case, he could probably be able to
drive his horse and scrap-iron and rabbit-skin
waggon through the Act. It surely was
meant to be so retrospective as all this ?

The plague, as predicted, has shown itself again
in Sydney with the spring, and, unless people
keep on energetically cleansing their premises and
exterminating rats, the second visitation is likely
to be a much more serious matter than the first.
Instead of springing nftw from one solitary source
of infection, the disease may at any time develop
from numerous different centres, with the dire
result that, by repeated reinforcement of virulence,
the malady may assume that horrible and rapidly-
fatal pneumonic form—wherein a patient’s lungs
literally rot within an hour or two—which is the
Black Death indeed. That gross relaxation of
sanitary vigilance has already taken place is evi-
dent from the condition of the public conveniences
of certain leading Sydney hotels —places which,
during the height of the scare, simply reeked of
disinfectants. In view of the mad panic which
any considerable recrudescence of the plague would
certainly produce, it has become the urgent duty
of every citizen promptly to advise the authorities
of any observed neglect of hygienic precautions.
For the Federal elections (Lower House) the Q.
Govt, is dividing the province into electorates—
on the whole, perhaps, the best system, under the
circumstances. A total of 101,269 voters on roll
gives 11,252 for each of nine divisions. Wide
Bay, the largest, will number 12,736 votes ; War-
rego, the smallest (in voting strength) will include
9102. Southern Q. is to have five members ; the
Centre one, and the North two. The fractional
representations over the one and two members
due to Centre and North are to be combined,
giving one extra representative whose district will
be partly in the North and partly in the Centre.
The South has a little the worst of the bargain
this time, for its voting strength of 67,095
names carries five members and a fraction;
if it possessed 67,512 it would be entitled
to another member. Some divisions include
five of the present electorates; Warrego, the
largest, has no less than eight. Had the province
voted as a whole, the North and Centre would
probably have been left out in the cold altogether,
the South securing the whole of the representa-
tion. As it is, the North and Centre (Democratic
Queensland) have a decent chance—to the gain of

Town Hall Scherek's Orchestra (Saturday
afternoon).
Not a new show, nor the gleam or vestige
of one, rose oil Sydney’s theatrical horizon last
week. The nearest approach to one consists of
the addition to the Biograph show at the Palace
of certain pictures revealing, or purporting to re-
veal, the daily life of Pope Leo X1 [f. Leo X1II.
is an elderly unmarried gentleman residing in
Rome. He is a grand old kindly vestige of :i past
era, and wears skirts. Also, he leads a patient,
grey existence in a large building, with a lot of
elderly unmarried men around him, and doesn’t
eat much, and never drinks, and never goes to the
theatre, and reads mournful, pious books in Latin,
and gets no joy out of his money whatsoever. But
he does an awful amount of work, and occasion-
ally has half a boiled egg and goes to bed early.
To be a good, honest, respectable Pope is
probably the most lonesome billet in Christendom.
It is doubtful if any other elderly, unmarried
gentleman eats half a boiled egg occasionally in a
palace of 11,000 apartments, and never quits his
tremendous residence, and never has any dissipa-
tion except when another single man of advanced
years reads to him from the life of some saint, and
leads so absolutely harmless an existence. The
Pope of the day is the extreme end of a long
peninsula of survival sticking out from the conti-
nent of the dead past into the ocean of the more
or less live future. The views given of the daily
life of this grand, but rather desolate, institution
further convince the writer that it is a good and
honorable and very dreary thing to be a Pope,
and that the billet is a sacred one with a great
lack of healthy exercise about it. Judging by
what can be seen, the Vatican is a fine property,
which would be somewhat improved if it possessed
a water-frontage, so that the Sacred College could
fish for bream over the front wall ; and there is
about it an atmosphere of silence—the accumulated
silence of 750 years—that would get on this
scribe’s nerves if he lived there long. But as any
respectable Pope would probably object to him
living there at all, he isn’t likely to be affected
by it.
* * * *
The British soldier who blackens his
miserably thin and hungry wife’s eye, and then
goes to the South African war and is shocked at
the stage-Boer’s treatment of certain women —the
stage Boer doesn’t hit his women captives in the
eye, but it is understood that he is going to do
something wrong —continues to rouse the fury of a
right-minded gallery at Sydney Royal. Curiously
enough, the gallery only laughs at the wrongs of
the pathetic English wife with the black eye, and
though that lady has obviously not had a square
meal for many weeks, and is lamentably short of
clothes and boots, and has been faithful to
her Bill while he was in gaol, only to be
spanked and deserted when he came out, she gets
no sympathy whatever. She drifts out of the
story in her broken boots and her torn skirt, and
her slept-on back-hair, and starts back alone to
her attic, and there is no reason to believe that
her Bill ever returns to her, or even gives her
another black eye by telegraph. But Bill comes
across another man’s wife, who is prettier, and
wears nice clothes, and after proudly stating that
it is the duty of every British Bill to defend a
woman in distress, he plugs a Boer with such
energy that the Boer wishes he had never been
born, and there is great applause. The other
man’s wife is rescued by an armoured train, but
not even a go-cart comes to the rescue of Bill’s
own battered “missus.” Judging by these differ-
ences, it would seem that the Transvaal was a
much safer place than London for a woman in
Bill’s time.

“ Rose of Persia ” has lost one of its lead-
ing characters. Miss Dorothy Vane has
quitted the _ tijneful Asiatic vegetable on
account of her approaching marriage, and
the part of Hassan’s stormy wife is now
taken by Miss Fyvie Dench. Thk Bulle-
tin proposes to form its opinion of the new lady
by close and intense observation during the week,
and express it in next isjue. Meanwhile, it regrets
Miss Dorothy’s departure, and wishes that charm-
ing lady every happiness. The fact that, in her
last stage-character, she was a discontented wife,
who offered a priest the loan of a number of
Gardening implements with which to attack her
usband, is one of these curiously-irrelevant
things which will happen in this very irrelevant
world.

On Wednesday next Mr. Dampier gives a
matinee performance of the “ Merchant of
Venice ” at Sydney Criterion in aid of the Prince
Alfred Hospital, and as there is no reason to
believe this will be anything like that recent
fashionable charity performance where everything
except £3 14s. Id. went in expenses, The Bul-
letin feels justified in urging the public to attend.
This show is for the hospital, and not chiefly for
the landlord or largely to buy decorations for
the vice-regal box. His X. and Lady Mary
Lygon, also the Chief Justice and wife, and many-
other notabilities, are lending their countenance,
and when one considers the countenance of one of
the patrons it seems probable that he, at all
events, won’t mind if the loan is never repaid.
“ Hamlet ” is to be repeated on Friday night, but
otherwise the “ Green Lanes of England ” will be
desecrated by the boot-marks of the well-dressed
villain and the stousher and the fallen gentleman
in the red suit as usual.

* its # *
Potts’ Point and co. rolled down in its
joint and several carriage drawn by its fatted
horse to a theatrical performance done by itself at
Sydney Lyceum, for the benefit of the battered
veteransfrotn Africa and their belongings. Takings
were £194 odd, and out of this a magnificent
balance of £3 14s. Id. was left for the
good cause. “Expenses” rose up like an
armed Boer in the night, and swallowed the other
£194 odd. All fashionable Sydney attended, the
vice-regals being present in a box decorated at a
cost of £11 3s. 7d. The ladies who minuetted did
so in wigs hired for £5 16s., which were paid for
out of the proceeds. According to the balance-
sheet, published in S.M.H., everything—stamps,
telegrams, cab hire (who arrived in that cab ?) came
from the same source. The theatre cost £50 for
the night, and £13 for i j at rehearsals, and the
properties and gauze drop ran into £23 8s. fid.
Nobody did anything for nothing appa-
rently, so that only a microscopic amount
resulted. However, the Miss P.P’s. fan-danced
to their own enjoyment, and nobody can say the
wounded soldiers at the front are any the worse
for it, and the lessee of the theatre is £03 better.
But it was a terribly tight fit. If Only £4 more
had been spent on hanging green stuff round the
Governor’s box it would have been necessary to
stop 5s. lid. out of the pay of the sick and
wounded soldiers, to make good the loss. Surely
a more thrifty management might have made a
trifle for the fund by the re-sale of the £11 3s. 7d.
worth of furnishings put into the gubernatorial
place of honour. Who has got these furnishings
now? His X. did not take them away in his
pocket.
