This week’s novelty at Sydney Tivoli con-
sists of two flying Patterson brothers, who fly as
extensively as can be expected. The fooling of
the funny brother is probably useful as giving the
other brother time to rest; but it should be re-
membered that the audience wants a rest, too. It
is a passing commentary on the slump in the war
business, and the growing tendency to pay the war
bill out of loan funds and say no more about it, that
one Bell gives a grotesque parody of war-corres-
pondent Macdonald’s lecture, and isn’t murdered ;
in fact, he is received by the audience with delight.
The Century Animatographe is frequently
crowded out, owing to the extreme alacrity with
which performers of small ability accept small,
perfunctory encores.
* * * *
“The Man of Forty” was introduced to
Australia on Saturday night at Melbourne Prin-
cess’s, but the acquaintance is not likely to ever
ripen into intimacy. The work of a new author,
Walter Frith, it has many fine points, several
glaring defects, and only a little originality. All
the same, Frith’s patchy play, with its strong
suggestions of Pinero, Jones and Grundy, pro-
mises an interesting future for the new workman.
“The Man of Forty” is a debonair Tanqueray, a
widower who adores another and a less strictly
immoral sort of Paula, and whose daughter is
much the same girl as the Ellean in Pinero’s
famous story. The daughter, whilst travelling
under the wing of a chaperone, falls in love (like
Ellean aforesaid) with a bounder—a gaudy actor
johnnie—and this undesirable mash turns out
to be the long-lost husband of Papa’s
grass-widow. But the grass-widow doesn’t con-
fess the truth to her Tanqueray, as Paula did in
the matter of Captain Ardale. She permits the
bounder to produce bogus evidence of his own
decease, so that he can marry Tanqueray’s daugh-
ter as soon as the 40-year-old parent has married
his (the bounder’s) wife. A hard-up burlesque
actress from S’Africa tells the necessary lies to
oblige the bounder (and for the sake of £20), after
which she gets troubled in her conscience, or per-
haps the grass-widow is overtaken by remorse.
Anyhow, the two women come and confess the
swindle, whereupon Tanqueray grabs the bounder’s
windpipe, with the result that he dies suddenly,
yet convincingly, of pains in the head. No trou-
ble comes of this strange catastrophe. The
bounder appears to get buried from natural
causes, and things end pleasantly enough.
* * * *
For no sufficient reason author Frith
added to the improbabilities of the plot by
“doubling” the character of the bad bounder
with that of a virtuous brother who acts secretary
to the Man of Forty. The alleged brethren are
supposed to be so much alike that the same actor
(W. T. Lovell) plays the pair of them in different
suits of clothes. Lovell as the bounder positively
asks to be confronted by Lovell as secretary, and
in a general way each Lovell speaks ill of his
other self. This confusion of Lovells is injurious
to the play. Lots of things are equally unsatis-
factory ; nevertheless, it is an effective play up to
the end of the third act, the fourth being washy.
Four characters outside of the plot ai’e well
drawn, and they give an air of reality to
the melodrama. The bounder’s death is a
strong finish to a stirring act, and the dia-
logue ranges from good to brilliant all the time.
Nine people get good acting chances, but the four
principals suffer from attacks of unreality all
round. Lovell’s bounder would be a very difficult
person to believe in even if the actor were built
for the part, and Miss Noble seems too wise a
girl to have been captivated by his thin wiles.
Mrs. Brough’s character doesn’t hang together,
and Brough, in the name-part, has to shut his
eyes when in the natural course of things lie would
be bound to keep a sharp look-out. Miss Bessie
Thompson, Miss Temple, Leslie Victor, Miss
Martheze, and Reginald Dartrey are bright suc-
cesses as far as they go, and the staging bears the
Brough brand of merit. Next week is the last of
the season, so it will probably be full of revivals.
** * *
At Melbourne Royal the fraudulent tiger
continues to fix its glass gaze upon the Orphan
Heiress, and the head of the sinful animal rolls
ominously in its socket as before. Also, a vision
of the Heiress’s angel mother, who has a gigantic
pair of 22-carat gold wings protruding from her
spiritual shoulder-blades, breaks out at the back
of a cage in the menagerie when the young lady
hesitates between marriage with a nice, shiny
villain and mastication by the tiger. The purpose
of this apparition is to influence the Heiress in
favor of the big Chew, but se ting that she is about
to be saved by the low comeUian the young woman
could very well dispense with mother’s services.
** * *
“ Tess of the D’Urbervilles,” at Her
Majesty’s (Melb.), is the same doleful tale of an
unnatural dairymaid that was staged at Sydney
Royal in January last. The cast of characters is
different in most places, but the false situations,
and the artificial morality, or immorality, of the
plot remain unchanged. Plays adapted from
novels have to go on their bare merits. Few
people pay for the privilege of being able to com-
pare the stage story with the book story. The
majority of the audience know little or nothing
about the novel. Present scribe has not read
Hardy’s “ Tess,” and doesn’t care whether Nance
o‘Neil’s impersonation (of Nance) gives a fair
i,dea, or no idea whatever, of the original heroine.
The play’s the thing. Present writer gathers from
the play that a tall fair daughter reared by rather
despicable parents on a rather squalid farm gets
rid of her agricultural roughness through losing
her Virtue. Her fall from grace has “ lifted her
above her station ” and made of the unsophistic-
ated milkmaid a stagey young woman with a
tendency to stretch out her arms like Sarah
Bernhardt and turn her back abruptly on the
world. The author of Tess’s highly improving
“shame” is the gilded youth of the district,
and he would be glad to do everything handsome
by the girl—except marry her. In fact he loves
Tess with a practical love. She believed in him
three years ago and he wants her to renew her
faith and make the best of him and of her “ fall.”
But Tess prefers to marry a sickly sentimentalist
named Clare —Angel Clare —who is adored by
three loud, vulgar dairymaids, one of whom takes
to drink when she finds she can’t get the prize.
Tess presumes that gentle Angel has learned of
her early giddiness from a letter which never
reached him. Tess’s mistake on this point arises
from Angel’s acceptance of a mere rose as being
something very important which she had to say
to him, and is the first big absurdity in the plot.
• * * *
When it happens that Angel’s bride, in
white wedding dress and fixings, tells the mawkish
prig of her Past, after he has made a brief snuffle-
some confession of his own silly Past, he just
walks out into the adjacent thunderstorm and
leaves Mrs. Angel to her fate. It served her
right, of course. Considering the natural deceit-
fulness of her Sex, any woman who makes a
particular viitue of disclosing her Past to a
husband named Angel is an unaccountable fool.
Tess’s folly results in another Fall. Having been
persuaded that husband Angel is dead, she
takes up with the other fellow on his own
terms, and proceeds to persecute him with her
precious meekness and resignation. Her habit
of sitting bolt-upright at the breakfast-table
soon drives her broad-minded lover to drink,
whereat she considers herself more aggrieved
than ever, so when the wandering Angel turns
out to be alive, Tess goes into the next room,
accompanied by a table-knife, and comes back
with a lot of red paint on the knife and white
chalk on her face. She had murdered the other
fellow, she says. Then Angel takes her away to
Stonehenge, so that she may be arrested among the
Druidical monuments at dawn of day. Present
writer thinks he can see the possibilities of some
powerful acting in this unconvincing melodrama.
He is certain about the quality of the perform-
ance given by Nance O’Neil and Co. It is, on
the whole, poor to middling, but Nance’s per-
sonality retains a strong hold on the multitude,
and the show should flourish for a fortnight.
** * *
At “ Rickards’,” otherwise the Bijou, the
Martinettians produced “A Duel in the Snow”
last Saturday night. The duel is a pretty constant
joke, terminating in one of Paul Martinetti’s
brilliant deaths, and to see Paul the mummer go
through a form of decease is to understand the
difference between real tragic acting and other
acting that passes for tragic in newspaper notices.
The latest infant songster at Rickards’s is a suc-
cess ; also Irene Franklin’s “trade mark,” and
Ferry the Frog, and several other matters are
graciously received.

Scherek’s admirable Orchestra will have to
miss a Saturday at Melb. Town Hall (owing to a
previous engagement on the part of the hall), and
the next concert is fixed for the 25th.
At time of writing the pursuit of the
Breelong murderers is approaching the farcical.
Jimmy Governor and his mate (the family name,
it seems, was originally “ Grosvenor,” but was
changed to “Governor” on a certain historical
occasion when Gov. Carrington did the head of
the clan the honor of a shake-hands) appear to be
thoroughly wretched ; they commit no more out-
rages, and ask for tucker and newspapers (!) at
huts on their way like any ordinary swagmen.
Every day two or three people meet them ; even
the police meet them ; but though upwards of 200
police and civilians have been tracking them for a
fortnight they remain uncaught up to date. And
now they are crossing a belt of sandy, open
country near Narrabri on their way to good cover.
If they get there !

The second re-arrangement in 1895 cost £3960 in miscel-
laneous expenses, but by forfeiting £19,330 worth of
capital for unpaid calls the bank covered this liability
and wiped out its old losses, and it has now an accumu-
lated profit of £9901. The earnings this half-year are
equal to Os. Bd. per cent, per annum on the nominal paid-
up capita of £905,098.
The most promising feature about the Aus-
tralian Deposit and Mortgage Bank is that it contrives
to hold its own despite the steady increase in the average
rate of interest on its reconstructed deposits. Thearrange-
ment arrived at in 1896 was that 1:70 out of every £lOO
deposits was to be repaid in 1010, bearing interest mean-
while at 3j per cent. The other £3O was repayable by
three instalments in 1890, 1899 and 1901 without interest.
Therefore the av erage rate of interest at first was £2 9s.
per cent, all round. The payment of the first instal-
ment of non-interest-bearing deposits raised it to £2 14s.
6d. The payment of the second instalment last year raised
the average rate -that now paid—to £3 Is. 3d. per cent.
When the third instalment is paid on Ist January next
the rate will rise to i 3 10s. per cent, and remain there.
The money for the third instalment is already' almost all
available, and after it is disposed of, if the A.D. & M. Bank
can keep on improving while paying 3£ per cent., there
should be a prospect before it. On present appearances it
seems fairly probable (assuming that on real values a
solvent bank should be able to earn even the very humble
dividend of 4 per cent.) that of its £905,698 alleged capital
about £829,000 is lost, and there is a real surviving capital
of about £76,000. But with a small profit coming in, and
no more deposits to meet till 1910, and a patient crowd of
preference shareholders (they would gain nothing by
being otherwise than patient) the A.D. & M. Bank can
afford to nurse its assets and wait for an improvement in
values. The share-market values the institution at about
£150,000, which seems remarkably high on present
appearances.

At the end of 1891 the Equity Trustees, Executors and
Agency Co. had no reserve fund, and an undivided profit
balance of £2509. During 8| years it has progressed to
the extent of adding £1920 to its net assets, and it has
now £1250 reserve fund and £3185 of undivided profit.
The £1250 is an utterly inadequate line of defence, and if
the board would transfer the bulk of the profit balance
to reserve fund as a guarantee that it doesn’t intend to
dissipate this amount in dividends and that it really- in-
tends building up its reserves to a safe and respectable
figure, it would be much better for the company’s credit.
A trustee co. needs some solid resources behind it if it is
ever to inspire public confidence, and the conduct of the
management in keeping-up the 74- per cent, dividend
even when it isn’t fully earned (this is the sixth time in 17
half-years that the dividend has exceeded the earnings)
isn’t the kind of advertisement that any trustee co.
should hunger after. The best feature about the report
is that the principal item in the balance-sheet (£11,500)
consists of Government securities. There is also a sum of
£4559 invested on mortgage. If interest receipts were
stated separately from mortgages (as they should be), and
especially if mortgage interest was stated by itself (as it
should be), the soundness of these would probably be so
apparent that it would speak for itself. But the Equity
Trustees lumps its whole income together in one item,
which saves trouble and reduces information to a mini-
mum.

The Castlemaine Brewery Co. has thus, in nine years, got
through its reserve fund of £BO,OOO and £69,096* of profits
written off, making a disappearance of £149,096. Its total
reserves at Ist July, 1891 were £101,244 ; they now consist
solely of an undivided profit balance of £3922. The dis-
appearance of the reserve fund is not quite so great a loss
as it looks; £50,000 out of the £80,009 was made by
writing up the paper value of the co.’s land in 1888, and
when this item was written down again in 1896 it was
merely a return to the old valuation, and the vanishment
of a temporary paper value. How much remains to be
written off to bring values down to bedrock is abso-
lutely unknown —the balance-sheet gives no informa-
tion—but things look reasonably promising. Iri 1890
the co. floated £lOO,OOO worth of debentures to pay
off its overdraft, and the loss made on this transaction
(£8596) has all been written off out of the last five years’
earnings. With this burden removed, provided business
doesn’t drop again, things should lie much better next
year, and Seeing that the co. is carrying the heavy load
of £133,000 owing on mortgages and debentures it may
perhaps then see its way to start S3'stematically reducing
this bulky burden, *

The quantity of sulphides now treated per week
at the “ Barrier” compares favorably with the output of
any silver-lead mining field in the world. The difficulty
in treatment of sulphides is being successfully grappled
with mainly owing to improved systems of mechanical
“dressing.” In this the Luhrig Vanner plays a very
prominent part. A large number of these machines are
now in use there, and fresh orders are still being given to
the agents, Messrs. W. and J. Lempriere, Melbourne. The
South Mine people are remodelling their mill on up-to-
date lines, and will install twelve or more Luhrig Vanners
of the 1900 pattern. The British Co. is also erecting
more of these superior machines.*

In view of the copper-finds in Northern S.A.,
and the early starting of Govt, smelters at Port Augusta,
some prospectors up that way still keep their eyes open
for coal and shale. So far the only deposit developed to
any- extent has been the Leigh’s Creek show, which
has been a millstone about the necks of the
half-dozen syndicates and co.’s that have handled it;
but lately shale was found near Sliding Rock of fair
quality—a bit better than- Briggs and the other prospec-
tors of Leigh’s Creek first met. But S.A. is. so firmly
convinced that she has no workable coal deposits that the
prospector who goes aftet the mineral will have to either
go on his absolute own or give away 19-20ths of what he
gets to some daring speculator.,

in all to £523,140. One of these funds (£466,536) is
made up out of premiums on shares sold, and this is
apparently kept sacred and treated as a real reserve. The
other (£56,604), accumulated out of profits, is used in a
casual sort of way for improvements to the works, and
should hardly be rallied a reserve fund at all. At the
end of 1892 the two reserves amounted to £492,143,

one has increased, despite many fluctuations, by the net
addition of 13342 out of earnings. This can't be called
anything very large : in fact, the Melbourne Gas Co.,
despite its long and prosperous career, has saved hardly
anything worth mentioning. The £857,000 borrowed in
debentures is a tremendous burden, which swallows up
over £41,000 a year in interest. There was a chance in
boom-time to pay off this load and yet give the share-
holders steady 8 or 10 per cent, dividends, but the Mel-
bourne Gas Co. went mad then, like most other Melb. in-
stitutions. The dividend paid in 1885 was 15 per cent.,
arid with a burning desire to scatter all the profits
with a shovel, and leave the liabilities and the re-
serve-fund to take care of themselves, it was run
up steadily till it reached 21J per cent, in 1891.
It was 174 in 1892 ; 13 per cent, in 1893 ; 10 per cent, in
1894 ; 9 per cent, in 1895 ; and 8 per cent, since then.
Between 1880 and 1893 there was a great opportunity to
clear off the indebtedness—in fact, there is still some-
thing of an opportunity, but it could only be done by
cutting down the dividend to 5 per cent. If the co. had
shown a little more moderation in the days when all Vic-
toria was painted red it would be a steady 12 per shent.
div. -paying concern now, with a big reserve and a half-
yearly surplus and no debts. /

The Universal Land and Deposit Bank has no reserve
fund, but as it has managed to pay an occasional divi-
dend on its little capital of £6825, and its undivided profit
balance has increased by £l2l in the past 51 years, and
now stands at £3887, it has done, on the whole, much
better than most institutions of its kind, especially when
it is remembered that it never suspended or recon-
structed, even in the daj’s when land banks were going
down so unanimously. In the very worst times it always
more than met expenses, but the constant writing-down
of property values made serious trouble in ’96, ’97, and ’9B.

Amid the troubles and embarrassments of the
big Victorian breweries which re-floated themselves in
the boom-time with a huge paper capital and an immense
debenture liability, it is interesting to note the prosperity
of the few concerns which kept sane during that wild
hair-raising time, and didn’t take to wild financing, but
just went on brewing beer, in the old, plain, business-like
way. As an example, Fitzgerald’s Brewing and Malting
Go., of Castlemaine (Vic.,), shows a profit of £6353 for the
year ended 30th June, 1900 This is equal to £l5 17s. Bd.
per cent per annum on the paid-up capital of £40,000.
Including £4839 brought forward from last year the
amount available is £11,192. AlO per cent div. absorbs
£4OOO ; £5OOO is added to reserve, raising it to £12,000 ;
and £2192 is carried forward. Fitzgerald’s Brewing and
Malting Co, paid 124 per cent div. in 1894, 11J per cent in
1895, and 10 per cent since. It struck some losses, like
most other institutions, through the slump in property
values, still, despite the large dividends paid and
considerable sums written off, the total reserves (£14,192)
are £lOOO more than they were in 1891. Even in the
worst days Victoria was a good enough place for a brewery
that hadn’t lost its head during the boom, and had
attended strictly to its own business.

The Australian Brewery and Wine and Spirit
Co. (Sydney) has had varied luck in recent years—or, to
be more strictly accurate, it hasn't had varied luck. The
following is a brief epitome of its recent doings :
Carried to various
Year to accounts to pro- p- •, a

This is not an obstrusively cheerful retrospect for
shareholders. In spite of the large provision made for
losses, depreciation, wear and tear, &q., the gross profits
have a general tendency downwards. Last year, after de-
ducting £lOOO for wear and tear of plant, and making
no allowance for bad debts or values that require writing
down, the net earnings were £s9Bs—equal to only
£6 4s. Bd. per cent, per annum on the paid-up capital of
£96,000. There is no reserve fund, but £4500 stands to
credit of a special reserve for bad debts, £22,000 to credit
of adjustment account, and there is an undivided profit
balance of £7B2o—making £34,320 in all. As the Austra-
lian Brewery and Wine and Spirit Co. is a comparatively
small affair, total assets being £207,679, it should seem
reasonable to hope that it has nearly finished making pro-
vision for past troubles, though there is, as usual, a great
reticence as to the exact size of the trouble, and conse-
quently the prospects of the shareholders are mostly a
matter of conjecture.
* * * *
The Adelaide Steamship Co. celebrated its 25th
birthday recently. It has been a steadily prosperous
affair, the shareholders having received average dividends
of 6j per cent, for 20 years past. The last two years have
shown results as follows :
Added to
Various
Profits. Reserves. Dividend.
May, 1899 £29,550 £10,793 10 per cent. = £20,287
May, 1900 £64,658 £30,U00 16 per cent. = £32,459
The Adelaide Steamship Co. has £202,870 paid-up capital
and £248,107 of reserves (insurance reserve, £157,993;
renewal reserve, £79,942 ; and undivided profit, £10,172).
Shipping is a more or less precarious business, as a rule,
and very few Australasian co.'s in that line have ever
approached the results shown by the Adelaide S.S. Co.
• • • *
Just such a weighing difficulty as is chronic on
the coalfields cropped up for the first time at B. Ilill
lately and threw the Sulphide Corporation’s Central Mine
idle for 24 hours. The truckers work on contract ; but
as there isn’t a weighbridge suitable for weighing the
single skips of ore, the latter are emptied into a railway-
truck, which, when filled, is weighed, and the average skip-
load is then arrived at. Lately themanagementsaid the ore
had been lighter, so that the loads weighed less (and
payment went by weight), while the truckers swore they
had never shoved such heavy stuff in their lives ; and so
they “chucked it up.” But the trouble was fixed up
same day ; the men go on wages—7s. 6d. a shift ; and
don’t men who have to hitch themselves on to IScvvt.
trucks in hot places earn it! —with the promise of a bridge
to weigh every single truck as soon as one can be got.
Broken Hill truckers are the only class of work-
men at the silver-mines who ever strike nowadays ; and
they do it pretty often. But that is easily accounted for.
Most of them are young men, unmarried chiefly,
who are graduating as miners ; and if they aren’t satisfied
they kick first and think afterwards. The curious thing
is that, though a fair number of them are members of the
local Miners’ Association, they don’t take the latter into
their confidence. If they did they would, of course, have
to go through a rather formidable process before reaching
the strike stage.
• • • *
B.H. South, now under new management, gives
for the first time for many years a fair amount of informa-
tion about its doings. The half-year’s results have been
excellent. The net profits may fie set at about £29,300.
Charging against the half-year £1045 for accumulated
income-tax (the Privy Council appeal in the Block 10 case
has upset calculations a little), and writing-off £3062 for
depreciation, the profit is £28,615. The financial position
is fairly strong ; liquid assets amount to £30,982, liabilities
to £12,252, leaving a surplus of £18,730. Of this, £5009
forms a reserve fund, invested in Melb, Board of Works
3J per cents. A heavy outlay for new and enlarged plant
is contemplated ; but the funds for this—" in part,” the
directors, by the way, say—are to be raised by “ drawing
on the reserve which the uncalled capital of the company
constitutes,” which is a neat way of putting it.
• • * *
In its half-year to -Tune 30, B.H. South raised
and treated 48,004 tons sulphides, assaying 21 percent,
lead, 8"8oz. silver, and 17 9 per cent.' zinc. Of these
metals, 67'39 per cent, of the lead, 50-51 per cent, of the
silver, and 13 02 per cent, of the zinc were recovered ; as
to the zinc, the object of the treatment so far is to
eliminate it. This result is, for the times, first-class ; for
instance, in the previous half-year only 61'89 per cent, of
the lead and 47'60 per cent, of the silver were saved. The
improvement is mainly due to re-treatment of the slimes.
But the cost was 4s. 9d. per ton, which is sjd. per ton
more; and that is due to the increasing hardness of the
ore. The milling and other surface plant is now under
control of W. E. Wainwright, a young Australian-trained
metallurgist, who learned the theoretical part of his busi-
ness at Adelaide School of Mines. Plain Sam Mavne,
the underground manager, who has pulled himself up
from the position of wages miner in this one mine, savs
in his report—“ Never since my 10 years’ connection with
this property commenced have the underground pros-
pects looked so promising.’
» » * *
Railway Commissioner Fehon visited B. Hill
lately to enquil j into the proposed State line to compete
with the Silverton tramway, but to run through Pinnacles
and Thackaringa instead of through Silverton. The B.H.
town council had invited people to meet the Commissioner
and urge the construction of the line ; but nobodv turned
up—not even an odd alderman. B. Hilldid look like kicking
a few months back at its huge monoply ; but all the in-
clination has left it again. The field pa vs a private co.'
close upon £lOO,OOO a year, without turning a hair ; but
it yells itself hoarse over the S.A. Gov.’s ninepence per
ton increase, which means no more than 10 or 12 thousand
a year.
• • * «
Wertago copperfield, far-west N.S.W., has got
the results from its first trial shipment of ore, 5 tons alto-
gether. Hand-picked, it gave an average of 25 per cent.
Ore which can be hand-dressed to close on 30 per cent, is
being got from as deep as 104 ft.; below that the water
comes in too heavily for present working.
* • • *
A sensational gold-find was made on Friday
last in the mine of Messrs. Robinson and Rice at Long
Flat, near Gundagai, N.S.W. The gold is described as
sticking out like a mass of coral from a soft calcite forma-
tion, and is almost pure. About £3OOO worth was taken
out in two days.
* * * -tf
The sanguine investors who bought Turon River
dredging shares a year ago at a price which gave a capital
value to the show of 1160,000. and who have been waiting
patiently for the huge returns promised “when Golden
Point is reached ” —it was to have been reached six
months ago—must be pleased with the last two returns
of lOoz. and 12oz. respectively.
* * • •
Girilambone, N.S.W. Consequent on street-
corner whisperings shares in this, the Brokers’Joy. took a
big jump in Sydney one day last week. ’Twassaid a good
report had come from the new manager, and scrip at
once doubled in value. Next day the report was pub-
lished, and shares went back to 3s. 6d. The new boss
simply said that the old boss had gone through the lode
and didn't know it, so what sort of lode must it he !
This mine has a mortgage mill-stone (£lO,OOO of deben-
tures) around its neck, has gutted all the known good
bunches of ore; development has proved it weak in the
lower levels. It takes heaps of cash to run a copper-
mine, and as no part of brokers’ commissions go to that
end the public might do worse than leave Girilambone
religiously alone. Ditto North Nymagee.
* * * *
Mt. Lyell amalgamation scheme. Of course
the S. Tharsis directors have approached the parent mine,
wanting something more than £45.000 for it, and the
manager has given an estimate of the quantity of ore in
the mine and its enormous value. The fact of its being
worked at a loss for nearly a year is quite another matter,
also the fact that before this purchase was proposed the
shares were less than half their present price. The
request was declined ; and the sale is as good as accom-
plished. The others from which ores can be mined to
take the place of barren flux are the Lyell Tharsis and
Royal Tharsis ; the offers made by the big mine for these
are based upon Sticht’s calculations, and are not likely
to be departed from in any respect. Tjie big co. has an
unwritten mortagage on every mine in the field, and is to
be commended for the sporting offers it has made,
instead of pursuing a waiting policy, by which it would
certainly have got the mines for less than half the price,
but would have lost more than the difference meanwhile
in mining siliceous flux containing no copper. Its adver-
sity in this respect has been the other mines’ great
opportunity, and they will he fools if they don’t avail
themselves of it. The Mt. Lyell has worked and can go
on working on its own—none of the others can.
* • • *
It is more than likely that the Tarcoola field,
S. Australia’s very latest far-northern gold-rush, is better
than most of its predecessors. Prospectors report the
geological conditions favourable, at all. events. The
formation is said to resemble that characteristic of
Cobar.
• • • *
S.A. is experiencing a red-hot gold-boom over
the big talk re Tarcoola field. Shares in several shows
have risen 200 per cent., although not a spade has been
put in the ground in most cases. Hardly a single co. has
as much as £5OOO to spend. S.A. has never had a pay-
able gold mine, and now, when the cautious Geologist
Brown advises “ systematic development,” the same old
game of floating cats is being again played.

Westralian scrip took a turn for the worse last
week. Associateds dropped from 70s. to 605., represent-
ing a quarter of a million fall. At half their present price
they would be fully double their value, so far as it is
visible. A million and a half for a mine whose mill has
had to stop for the want of payable ore, which has been
gutted of all its known rich pockets, and which has spent
many thousands in prospecting, so far without success.
The co. has £200,000 invested in Consols, and. perhaps
another £lOO,OOO in realisable assets, whien is all that is
left of the over half-a-million got last year by the sale of
reserved shares at £l2 each. If the “ bears ” would
only leave them alone they would soon fall to £l.
Perseverance fell 10s. during the week, and recovered ss.
of it. lvanhoes are £lO, and were down to £9 15s.—a big
drop, in face of the fact that they managed to scrape up
another 8000 oz. for the month. The market begins to
talk, and another fall seems certain. L. V. Consols, £ll
10s., and allowing for ex-div. it is a fall of over £2 per
share, which means over half-a-million sterling ! What
gambling playthings some of these Hannan’s mines are,
to be worth half-a-million less one week than another!
But this sort of thing can’t go on for ever. Already
several —Great Boulder and Perseverance to wit—are sub-
ject to no such transformations, and sooner or later all
the others must arrive at their real position. Great Boul-
ders, 325. 6d. —a 6d. fall for the week only. Northern
Associateds, £2 7s. 6d. They reached 51s. one day in
Adelaide and 495. in London. Stated that both Hewitson
and Prof. Liveing have examined and sampled the mine,
and the rise in these shares started the day after their
report was believed to have been cabled to London.
South Kalgurlis, 91s. 3d. The mill has told the truth
about this mine. Good as it is, it is not as great as was
expected, and present output cannot pay anything like a
fair mining-div. on present price ; hence hope is centred
in duplication of plant.

Lake View Consols.—July production. Oxi-
dised slimes, 3072 tons, 12240 z. Sulphide ore, 3027 tons,
36390 z. Shipped to smelters, 71 tons, 22230 z. Total,
70860 z. Value presumably about £26,000. Working

Great Boulder. —July output, 3318 tons milled
for 32120 z. ; 9050 tons tailings and slimes cyanided for
55490 z. ; 480 tons sulphides put through the new sulphide-
mill for 6140 z. ; total, 93750 z. The sulphide-plant com-
menced with a small tonnage averaging an oz. and. a half,
and though tonnage will be greatly increased nextlnonth
it may be taken as a certainty that there will be ns fall-
ing-off in the average, which is certain to be kept up for a
long time and'may possibly improve. Manager Hamilton
makes few mistakes, and it is unlikely that he is going to
make the biggest of his life with the G.B. sulphide-plant
after the warnings he has had from other capable but less-
careful managers.
• » * *
Golden Horseshoe : 6202 tons crushed, 5888 oz.;
3264 tons tailings cyanided, 24970 z. ; 3760 tons slimes,
6530 z. and 5860 z. from concentrates. Month’s total pro-
duction, 10,6230 z. This mine maintains the record of the
field, but its depth, developments, area, nryd want of sul-
phide plant do not warrant its being looked for- rd to as
the biggest producer for long. It has by far the greatest
market valuation on the field, which is absurd. The yarn
about amalgamation with the Ivanhoe is re-started.
What basis is proposed? One has nearly double the
market valuation of the other. Golden Horseshoe a few
weeks ago touched £13—£3,900,000 ! and they have been
much higher! lvanhoes, £lo—£2/00,000. Amalgama-
tion on equal basis would be reasonable, and the position
of the mines makes it expedient, but an amalgamation
on market valuation would be monstrous ; for, allowing
all the bad things which may fairly be said lately with re-
gard to Ivanhoe, it has much more than half the value of
the Golden Horseshoe. It has a much better and bigger
record, is developed to a greater depth, is equally well if
not better equipped, and, taking all things together, is
probably as good a mine.
* * * *
Ivanhoe, W.A. Says Sydney D.T. : “The
Ivanhoe treated 6699 tons for 8086 oz.” It didn’t. And
this form of newspaper yarn about the big mines at
Hannan’s is very common. The gold got from milling
this tonnage was 45260 z., showing a remarkable falling-off
in the average value of the ore. 3867 tons tailings
cyanided 15290 z. ; 4032 tons slime's gave 12440 z. ; 34 tons
sulphides, 950 z. ; and 56 tons concentrates, 6890 z. Thus,
though mill tonnage considerably increased, it doesn’t
nearly come up to the quantity of slimes and tailings
treated, showing that they are still working on accumu-
lated, but now nearly used-up, tailings-heaps—a fact
somehow never mentioned in any of the papers. The
current month’s yield will probably tell a tale.
» » * *
Kalgurli Gold Mines. Ore treated for July : 946
tons sulphides and 554 tons of oxidised ore—for 24150 z.
An extraction of 91 per cent. The ores mixed in treat-
ment, therefore different values are not got at. This
mine seems to have more payable oxidised ore above 100 ft.
level than it has payable sulphide ores in all the levels
below, and there are four or five of them. It seems to be
a large pipe of ore shaped like a funnel with the small end
downwards. Writer thought he was in a great mine when
above the 100 ft. level, but the lower he descended the less
he thought of it. A good mine, but at the price (£6) not
good enough.

mine said to be opening so well in the lower levels as to
warrant the duplication of the plant, which is now pro-
ceeding, but will take five or six months to complete.

There must be a good profit in this. A remarkable fea-

mill in a month. Is there any other in Australia doing it?

* * • ♦
Brown Hill: Gold for July, 64360 z., made up
by crushing oxidised ore and a shipment of rich sulphides.
The oxidised zone is rapidly being exhausted, and the
sulphide zone is not big enough to warrant the present
price of shares. The co. is doing its utmost to develop
other bodies in the sulphide zone —so far, without success.
The Brown Hill people don’t care about any outsider going
down their mine, which is being elaborately engineered
in the London market.
• * * *
Hannan’s Reward : 330 tons, 2950 z .; princi-
pally worked by tributors. Has a large formation, which
would pay with a big plant, but has no hope of doing so
with the present 20-head rattle-trap.
* * * *
The Bendigo Mining Managers’ Association, it
is printed, has been asked if it can select 3000 miners for
the ltand and Rhodesia, and it is intimated that alto-
gether 10,000 miners and mechanics will be wanted, at big
wages. Miners, 20s. a day on the Rand, 255. in Rhodesia.
Mechanics, carpenters and fitters, 30s. per day. First-
class certificated engine-drivers 40s, and second-class 30s.
per day : while a few mining-storekeepers are wanted at
£3O per month. The miners are to be recognised mem-
bers of the Union, must be under 45 years of age ; pas-
sage-money to S’Africa to be refunded out of wages, and
an agreement for 12 months. It seems almost too
glittering to be true ; already some cold water is being
poured on it. The idea may be to cause a big exodus of
mining labor “on its own ” from Australia. Writer sin-
cerely hopes that it will prove true to the extent of
bringing about a substantial ncrease in the miners’ wages
at Bendigo, which have been the lowest in Australia for
many years ; yet the work is the hardest, for the mines
are the deepest, and therefore the worst ventilated.
* * * •
In the advertising columns of this issue appears
the prospectus of the Demon Creek Gold Dredging Co.
(No Liability). The co. is formed to dredge 101 acres,
which have been already secured on the banks and bed of
Demon Creek (Tenterfield district, N.S.W.), and 24 acres,
lease of which has been applied for. Capital is £24,000,
in 48,000 10s shares. The vendors (who take their pay-
ment wholly in shares) receive 10,000 of these fully paid
up ; 16,1 00 are offered for present subscription, and 16,000
reserved-for-f-utnre issue, shareholders having first offer.
Some very good reports are published, various experts
testifying to the high value of the property. Certainly
the fact that the vendors are to have one-third of the co.
merely in consideration of the transfer of their lease looks
a trifle steep, but the demands of most vendors
are, unfortunately, even more precipitous than that.

Society
Sydney parson Boyce wants to have British
names substituted for the native ones given to the
Federal electorates of N.S.W. The rev. gentleman
would, no doubt, have delighted in Whitechapel,
Blackfriars, Tooley, Grubb, Snooks, and such-like
designations. By the way, the nomenclature of
Sydney and thereabouts is lamentably and con-
fusingly unoriginal. Thus we have Newtown,
Waverley, Paddington, Glebe, Ashfield, Peter-
sham, Burwood, Granville, Liverpool, Surrey
Hills, Waterloo, Alexandria, and St. Peters. We
are tired of a system the ultimate of which is to
call every town London, every street Victoria-
street, every rivqx the Thames, and every person
Smith. May the Lord have mercy on us miser-
able, unimaginative, grovelling Cockneys !
• * * *
ft is indeed “ sickenin ” to read the tele-
grams published in the Sydney dailies from various
country N.S.W. towns re the Breelong murderers.
Singleton was in the “wildest state of terror”
over the “near approach” of the Governors;
Maitland was merely in the “ wildest state of
excitement.” Yet to get there the aboriginals
■'■'ould have to cross the range, pass down Goulburn
River, and thence along the Hunter —aridiculously
improbable thing. Verily excitement is cheap in
Australia. The climax was a wire from Armidale
to E. News stating that “the Armidale police
were on the alert for the black murderers ” !
* * * •
Sydney D.T.’s correspondent “on the
death-trail” of the Breelong-blacks—a Scotchman,
apparently—is lost in amazement at the riding
prowess of the denizens. People there think
nothing of riding 15 miles straightaway—pro-
digious ! And when Q. Inspector Galbraith’s
trackers got through 60 miles in 12 hours, they did
it “with awe-inspiring calmness,” and the corres-
pondence was cross-headed “A Record Ride.” It
evidently pains this correspondent very much
when he sits down.
* * * •
The expedition of well-intentioned Mr.
Sandeman’s bloodhounds to catch the Breelong
murderers was a quaint performance—like the
King of France’s with 20,000 men, who marched up
a hill and then came down again. The spectacle
of the dogs being carried over rough country by
perspiring devotees on a trail a week old or no
trail at all would have made the slave-drivers of
“ Uncle Tom’s Cabin ” split their sides. And one
of the dogs is a veteran of at least 14 or 15 years’
bloodhounding. His troubles about the Breelong
blacks !

Sample of Breelong panic-news—a wire to
S.M. Herald
WEE WAA, Wednesday.
Two men met the coach from Narrabri to Walgett at 3
o’clock this morning, and asked how far it was to Pilliga.
It was too dark for the coachman to see whether the men
were black or white.
• • • •
The naval contingent departed very
quietly from Sydney. No special trains, no half-
holiday, no suspension of business. The men were
taken per back-street to the wharf, and most of
them were very sober indeed. An exception was a
warrior with a phenomenally hard face, who
waved his gun and loudly and repeatedly ’led
“ I am God ! ”

Ed. Bulletin.—
If the electric-tram
controllers supplied
their motor-cars with
practicable sand-
pipes many of the ac-
cidents could be
avoided. The way
the present pipes
curve causes them to
get stuffed with mud
each time the car re-
verses. Straight pipes
which will let the
sand drop in front of
the wheels are re-
quired. At present
the sand - gear does
not work, and the
wheels skid a few
yards after the brakes
are applied. Jack
Shat, Brisbane.

T’other wet
day in Sydney a
bus-horse fell on
the slippery
blocks, within a
stone’s-throw of
the terminus,
and the passen-
gers alighted—
dll but one, a
daintily - garbed
young woman,
who retained her seat with evident determi-
nation to exact her full penn’orth of convey-
ance. A crowd quickly assembled, and. the
charmer inside was the butt of derisive
tongues. She never turned a hair, but kept
on jerking the ’bus-bell and asking the
astonished Jehu to either return her fare or
hurry along to the terminus. It was a
quarter of an hour before the horse was on
his legs and the tenacious damsel was able
to achieve her remaining 50yds. of vehicula-
tion.
* • * *
Melbourne Age and Argus were the
only first-class papers in Australia that made
mourning columns for the Duke of Edin-
burgh. The Argus gave Humbert a little
black also.
* * * *
During the last seven years N.S.W.
sent more appeals to the Privy Council
than all the other provinces of Australasia
put together. The N.S.W. record is 66.
Vic. sent 20, Queensland 12, W.A. 5, S.A.
1, M.L. 12, Tas. 1. Yet the only province
with a Judge of its own on the P.C. is
S.A., with one case in seven years.
* * * •
The director of Adelaide Zoo wonders
when Fowell Buxton, Bart., the ex-Gover-
nor, who has now been in England for nearly
two years, intends to give some sign that
he has not forgotten his promise to present
a stallion zebra to the Gardens, which
already possesses a couple of mares. It is
suggested that, because of the fragility of
his promises on this and other matters, the
late vice-regality was spoken of as his Egg-
shellency.
* * * *
“ Boja ” : Be Melb. Age editor
Windsor’s contempt for news (“Scribe,”
B. 21/7/’00). Small ■ wonder, considering
that the average daily paper menu of news
comes under one of three headings—Faked,
Distorted, or Parochial. In one Melb.
journal, now happily defunct, it used to
be the custom of the cable-code interpreter
to inquire of the printer, before “making-
up,” as to shortage of news. Being in-
formed, he would make good said short-
age ; and that evening pious house-
nolders would read with horror that the Khan of
Onehunga had taken unto himself 24 new wives.
News! Nothing would be called new in the
columns of a daily, save the truth !
• * • •
Sydney’s Mayor Harris discovered the
other day that an important minute tabled by
him was disfigured with quotations from a comic
opera. The old men voted to bowdlerise the docu-
ment, and the merry Mayor’s fine humour was
accordingly lost to print.
NEATER AND LESS HARMFUL .
PATCHWELL (bishop): “I hear, Mr. Sapless, that you no longer make use
of the word hell.”
SAPLESS (curate): “No, sir, the ladies prefer Cinerarium, and the
Sunday scholars can't use it as a swear-word.”
Dr. Armstrong, the Sydney plague-expert,
after inspecting the three cases of alleged plague
in Adelaide Hospital, declares that none of the
patients are afflicted with that disease ! Which
is a very nasty jar for the hospital doctors. Dr.
Armstrong is the first medico unconnected with
the institution who has been allowed to see the
patients. But this sort of thing makes the public
very uncertain as to whether the average doctor
really knows much about the plague.
* * * *
Townsville’s first plague-patient, a child,
was quarantined—he and his family and the
boarders within their gates —out on that sand-ridge
on the town common. Ditto, next case, that of a
night-watchman ; and ditto the third, a parson ;
but a canon, and a curate, and a maid-servant,
who lived in that infected parsonage, just stopped
where they were, and went on eating and drinking
and saying their prayers as usual. Then came
the big-hotel case, and the 50 odd contacts
were just fumigated and let slide. Much explana-
tion wanted somewhere.
* * * *
Not Sydney alone, but every Australian
city, should get its crematorium ready for the
burning of the human dead. Evidence is ample
that the disturbance of a grave years after a plague
victim has been buried awakes the deadly bacilli.
The many reasons heretofore urging the erection
of crematories are powerfully supplemented by
what is known of the rejuvenating powers of the
bubonic germs. It is all very well to say that as
far as one State or another is concerned the persons
who died from the plague were interred in special
places and with special disinfecting precautions.
There is no absolutely certain evidence of this.
Many persons may have—must have—died from
the plague without affording the external signs
which denote plague. That is admitted. And
just as Devonshire-street (Sydney) cemetery now
is about to be opened up for railway purposes,
the graves of these bubonic dead may be disturbed
in years to come. Crematories are an imperative
demand.
HOW THEY LOOK AT IT NOW.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When the scare was raging madly.
And the cry was “ Make us clean ! ”
When the contacts grumbled sadly
At their state of quarantine,
When inoculators zealous
Formed conclusions rather vague,
Was it proven—can you tell us?—-
That we really had the Plague ?
Was it Plague, or was it Typhus,
Born of terror, more or loss ?
Was it Paragraphogyphus ?
(Meaning measles of the Press)
Was it spread by lurid chatter
In the tramway-cars, or how ?
What the dickens does it matter
If we haven’t got it now ?

The N.S.W. clergy and certain of the
borough-councils are co-operating in a move to
legislatively put down “ football, tennis, cricket,
or other similar games within municipal boun-
daries on the Sabbath day.” Is it intended that a
man may not play tennis in his own enclosed
ground on Sunday ? Also, does it mean that the
quiet and fashionable game of—haw !—golf is to
be put down ? The Bulletin would dearly love
to see the parsons fight the golfers.
* * * •
After a slight rest, the famous N.S.W.
M'Sharry case comes up again. This time
the case is in re Gent. One’s bill of costs
and other tough technicalities. M‘Sharry
has been, probably unwittingly, the best
friend Sydney lawyers have ever had. Bruce
Smith’s address lasted 36 days, and the evi-
dence filled 24 vols. of 500 pages each. There were
colossal preliminary expenses, side expenses and
after expenses, but the mere hearing of the arbi-
tration, without costs, stood the Govt, over
£30,000. The hearing began in July, 1896, and
lasted until June, 1898. One witness received
£8,245 18s. 4d. as expenses, and several drew from
£2OO upwards.
* * * *
Extract from letter of a member of the
Vic. Mounted Kifles, at Bloemfontein :
There was a time when our men were swearing at our
inferior outfits and bad saddlery, but probably no volley
of oaths could exceed in profanity the outcries dis-
charged against the authorities for not procuring our
speedy return to Australia, when invalided, as some of us
have been kept waiting for upwards of two months with-
out a ship.
* * . * »
These are, indeed, the halcyon days of
the daily press. With the2so men of the N.S.W.
Naval Contingent for China were sent two
“ assistant-p&y masters ” at £5 ss. per week each,
who, in addition to their official duties, real or
mythical, represent Sydney morning newspapers
as war-correspondents. The Ministry defends
this job on the ground that “it is a proper thing
to have these men with the troops, so as to get
reliable information as to what is going on.”
It is certainly proper to get reliable
information —but what sort of reliable in-
formation are you likely to get from correspond-
ents with a duty divided between the £5 ss. they
get from the Treasury of this bleeding country
and the screw they draw from the newspapers that
never bleed more than they can help ?
• * • *
Only one medical man has gone with the
N.S.W. Naval Contingent, and it would surely
have been better to send two surgeons than two
assistant-paymasters—to help pay 250 men
(monthly at the rate of Is. 6d. a day, the balance
being drawable in Sydney.) The Government
have taken excellent care that no independent
newspaper had a chance of being independently
represented in China on similar terms. If Lyne
fired Reid from office for an improper payment to
Jawbone Neild, what should be done to Lyne and
See over this disgraceful piece of business.
* * * *
A wise South Australian J.P., who is also
a medical man, lately committed a boy aged 14
for trial for criminally assaulting a married woman
old enough to be his grandmother. What is more,
he demanded heavy bail, and the lad was long
locked up awaiting trial. At the assizes, the jury
stopped the case immediately after the evidence
for the prosecution, and expressed indignation at
the action of the police in respect to the child.

* * * *
“ ltan ” : I live in a N.S.W. town where
weekly markets are held. Saw the carcases of 60
pigs hanging there for sale one day recently. Was
told that they had been slaughtered by the
farmers, who held no licenses, and the inspector
did not make any examination. When it is
remembered that condemned cattle are fed to
pigs (the pig is, perhaps, more prone to tuber-
culosis than any other animal), what is the differ-
ence between disseminating tuberculosis and
cancer through pork and beef ? And also is it not
just as necessary to inspect the dead meat of a
farmer as that of a butcher ?

pair.” Badly wanted, a shops poultry-inspector.

“Weary”: Considering the state of the
awful N.S.W. superannuation fund, it
would be interesting to* know how Govt, intends
to find money to pay pensions with in the near
future. I know of one country school, closed for
lack of sufficient attendance, where the teacher
(well over pension-age) is allowed to occupy the
residence and collect full pay for six months—for
doing nothing—instead of being granted instant
retirement as applied for.

Neild was court-martialled, or something, for
“writing to the papahs.” Now, to all appear-
ance, General French, his superior officer, has
done the same thing, and when John Norton
asked Guerilla Minister See what he meant to do
aboift ft, he was told to “give notice,” which
meant that See hadn’t made up his alleged mmd.
The whole business is intensely foolish. French’s
letter to the London Times was an indiscretion.
He must have known that if one of his own
HIM : “ Well, I’ll own up I was wrong.”
HER: “That won’t do; you must own up that I was right.”
amateur officers had done the same thing, he
would have been heavily sat upon, and he himself
is, or should be, just as much subject to discipline
as the last-joined recruit. If “ writing to the
press ” on military matters is forbidden to mili-
tary persons in general, it is forbidden to the
Commandant, and if General French didn’t know

* ♦ * *
A sorry sight was the inarch through the
streets of Adelaide of the military invalids from
S’Africa. Only about 30 were able to land, the
remainder being unfit to leave the Persic. The
men marched to the accompaniment of an occa-
sional cheer ; at the Stock Exchange there was a
chilly silence where but a few months before there
had been vociferous Jingoism. The reaction has
set in with a vengeance—the public is already
tired of its new toy.
* * *
The so-called “ Patriotic funds ” are now
petering out in N.S.W., though an effort is to be
made to bring the main fund up to £50,000. The
huge mistake the Bushmen’s Fund was is slowh
being brought home to the subscribers. Of the
£36,500 raised to send away this corps, not a
penny remains; as of course the principal was
used up at the start. The trustees of the fund
aver that the only people they can succour are
dependent relatives of the slain —not of the
partially used-up soldiers.
Disaster has followed the officers of the
“Australian Horse,” as it was represented in
S’Africa. Of the five lieutenants, M’Kellar and
Ebsworth have been killed; Dowling was in-
valided home, and Wilkinson, after a miraculous
escape, was made prisoner. Vaughan alone has
escaped mishap.
• * • *
Long before there was any siege of Mafeking
The Bulletin, in reference to the nauseating
repetition of variations of certain cabled press-
puffs of the Baden-Powell family, remarked that
the Baden-Powells were indefatigable self-
advertisers. This is from a letter received from
an Imperial Bushman
When the siege of Mafeking is truly written it will show
what a gigantic fake the whole thing was. Starvation !
why they were all as fat as whales when the Boers cleared
away. The men who did suffer were Pluiner’s lot (which
fits in with a speculation ventilated in these columns
manv weeks ago.—Ed. B.). Poor devils, they seem to
have' had a real bad time of it; what with fever and insuffi-
cient and bad food. The Buluwayo people look upon the
affair as a joke, and say that they suffered infinitely more
than anyone in Mafeking. Not that the Buluwayoites
suffered any real hardship, but they missed a lot of the
luxuries of life, and prices were high. As an instance
of the lies sent out from Mafeking about their privations
and living on boiled horse-hide, &e., why a thousand
sheep which were there throughout the siege
have now arrived here, to say nothing of train-load
after train-load of provisions stored there for merchants
here before the war broke out. Fancy the garrison when
they knew, as they must have done, that Plumer’s men
were in such straits, taking a waggon-load of their food
just to keep up the farce ! It has enraged the people up
here terribly, and it’s hardly safe to mention the name of
Mafeking heroes locally. I have not read whether the
beleaguered moved out in rags to greet their deliverers,
but one chap who marched in with the relievers told me
that the Mafeking people hardly knew that anything had
happened, and the only human beings that met them
were a few Kaffirs. The whole thing seems to have been
a display of advertising mountebankism unequalled since
Cagliostro departed for other shades.
By the way, up to now only one of those chargers
for Baden-Powell has been selected. Better wait
to see whether any more gas escapes from the
bladder before sending along the other moke.
Evidence daily accumulates to show
that the Mafeking garrison were good self-
advertisers. We used to hear a lot about the
people living on mule-chops, hair-oil, starch, and
so on. Yet Chaplain Green, of the Australian
Bushmen, writes from Bulawayo to S.M. Herald :
The pressure in regard to food supply has been greatly
relieved by the arrival of immense quantities of stores
from no stranger a place than Mafeking. There was food
enough in that town for 12 months, and the merchants
have sent their surplus stock here to get, whilst they can,
high prices for it.
Then a Jamberoo man writes to say that he
“brought his dog out of it all —sleek and fat.”
Now, a siege that a dog comes out of, fat and
well, can’t have been, dietetically, such a very
“tight place.” The Bulletin much fears that
Australia has been fooling itself most hideously
over Baden-Powell.

Dear Bulletin. — Re “ R.E.’s ” par. as to
“A” (N.S.W.) Battery. The true reason why
that redoubtable unit never got to the front was
not because it was in any way inefficient, although
it certainly was not the battery when it at last
got away from N.S.W. that it was in Oct., 1899.
Col. Smith would insist on being given his
.ocal colonelcy—his right rank is a Major in
the It. A. —and, of course, he thereby set against
himself and his command the whole river of
jealousy which runs through the British army
from the Commander-in-Chief down. It was un-
likely that all the majors senior to him—let
alone the Lieut.-Colonels and Colonels—were
quietly going to let their junior get up to the
front—where, if he struck luck and sufficient
Boers, he miebt have got a whole brigade of
artillery put under him. Not much ! Therefore
the other chaps worked it so that “A ’Battery
should wander off afar to Western Gnqualand
to chase the cursory rebel and curse discon-
tentedly through miles of sand and months of
disappointment. These be no vain imaginings but
solid facts.— Khaki.
» * » *
Brutal are the conditions under which
many hotel and restaurant employes toil in Syd-
ney. At a meeting last week a restaurant-hand
complained that he had been working 120 hours a
week. “My working-hours were from 4 a.m. to 12
p.m.” The Dago, in particular, is a most cruel
employer.
* * * •
There have been some savage doings, in-
cluding cold-blooded murder, or something very
like it, in Pitt-street, Sydney, near Tattersall’s,
lately. Will the authorities please see that a few
picked policemen, who don’t drink, are put on
special duty in this region, and that the brutal
toughs who regularly congregate and spit on the
footpath there are systematically moved on—or
locked up ! Otherwise, decent citizens will have
to carry guns.
* * * *
Among the poorer classes “lodger” and
“ co-respondent ” are too often merely synonymous
terms—it would be the same among the richer
classes, only they aren’t driven to take in lodgers
—and in view of the devastating moral character
of the lodger, D. Thomson, M.L. A., is opposed to
him having a municipal vote, as provided under
Lyne’s Bill for the better government of Sydney.
Which might be logical if depriving the lodger of
his vote would make him less of a co-respondent—
but it won’t. Nor will depriving him of his
municipal vote make him give up the sinful pro-
fession of being a lodger. If a man is mashed on
a nice-looking landlady whose husband is out a
great deal, he won’t leave her and start a house of
his own merely for the satisfaction of being able
to vote for Alderman Thingumbob once in two
years or so.
* * * *
The measure before Vic. Assembly, aim-
ing to make young Australians moral by packing
them off to bed at 9 p.m., is supported by all
pietists and pretentious humbugs, and Excellency
Madden believes with the rest that virtue is not
an internal impulse, but an external influence,
and wants the youngsters yarded in. It is an un-
speakable outrage to force youngsters indoors
before 9 o’clock on summer nights in a place like
Melbourne. After red-hot summer days the
kiddies in the suburbs do not begin to exist
till nightfall, and then they run riot in the streets,
houses being oven-like and uninhabitable, and
there being no place else for them to riot in. The
man who would pack those children into the hell-
houses built for the workers before 9 o’clock, or 10
either, on close summer nights, for the better care
of their morals, would be a hog of the most obtuse
’ mild.

A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, August 14, 1900.
My Dear Myke.—
I had a peep at Dolly Vane’s bridal garments at
a leading Dressmaker’s Studio last week.
“ Studio,” by the way, is an elastic epithet, gra-
dually going to comprehend any four walls
wherein a woman does anything for her living,
from nail-shaving to hair-dressing and millinery.
In this case, though, there were so many pretty
things to be seen that we will let the term pass.
Dolly’s wedding frock is a turquoise cloth, with
white tulle vest, huge tulle bow at the neck, and
heavy white silk appliqiie on the revers and skirt.
More bridal, and to my mind prettier, is a lovely,
creamy race dress, of Honiton lace, made as sim-
ply as possible, with transparent sleeves and yoke,
and just a few big choux of creamy chiffon on the
corsage. Then there is a sweet little silk frock of
apple-green glace, sprig patterned, the Russian
blouse pouched back and front over a wide pink
folded panne belt, and a lot of fine black lace
about the hips and on the blouse. All these
frocks are made to sweep, and looking at them
uninhabited, as it were, they strike you as being
twice as long as their wearer-to-be. Like most
very small women, the bride cannot have too long
a train following her.
A black embroidered net stands for dinner-
dress. Little accordeon-pleated lace flounces run
round the bottom of the skirt, a jangling black,
sequined front makes an apron effect on the skirt-
and more black sequins form a pretence at a
zouave. A dinner blouse of black chiffon, ever
so finely tucked, has a berthe of real lace, and Gig
puffed sleeves caught in with black velvet and
paste buckles. A lovely opera mantle is of
strawberry-tinted silk, embroidered in different
shades of the same fruit. Another strawberry-
pink garment is a frock of the new Spring fabric
—fine silk voile —made with a big collar of Irish
guipure over pink chiffon, and a high, black velvet
waistband. And I musn’t forget an Empire tea-
gown of blue liberty satin, with a soft blue fichu
fastening at the left under one big, dark-red rose.
The wearer of all these and many other pretty
frocks I can’t remember, farewelled the public on
Friday at Her Majesty’s. An extra heavy house
gathered to see the last of the sparkling little
contralto, who is energetic in declaring she is
giving up the stage for ever and ever. They al-
ways say that, of course. Sometimes they keep to
it. Flowers poured down as Dancing Sunbeam
made her farewell curtseys. The strong reliable
voice that never went out of tune will be grehtly
missed. It has been the only feminine larynx in
the whole of this Opera Co. that has never
deviated from its legitimate key.
In a libel application last week I see that four
of our very wisest men went to sea in a boivl-
otherwise, tried to arrive at the whichuess of the
why of the “Social Column.”
The benign Chief Justice thought that some-
times very great unhappiness was caused through
the so-called social columns. Mr. Justice Cohen
said his view was that tiiey were published partly
to satisfy the idle curiosity of some people, and
partly to gratify the personal vanity of others.
Mr. J ustico • Owen believed that they were re-
quired by a certain section of the community.
Mr. Pilcher declared that they were written en-
tirely for the delectation of the ladies. And the
wise Mr. Justice Owen observed that the ladies
form one-half of the community.
Acting on all that unutterably unimpeachable
legal advice, I am going to abandou myself to a
perfect whirl of drapery this week. Then, if any
fiery male eye happens to chance along tins
column, I shall refer him, for the defence, to the
verdict of the Full Court as set out about half-an-
iuch back —in effect, that this is no place for men.
Billee Barlow’s frocks are next on the list. She
sings at the Tivoli nightly now. and considering
that there isn’t much of any one frock it is astonish-
ing what big effects she strikes. By the wav,
some of her songs are absolutely odious. All her
cM’c-ness and magnetism cannot eradicate the
dismal blue pervading at least three of her four
“ turns.
“Mashing the Band” is a much-gagged song
that Billee aims point-blank at one after the other
of the orchestra. “Gussie, my dearest,” she
cries, running along to the dark fat man at the
end who looks frightfully uncomfortable, and con-
tinues to become more and more so as the song
grows warmer. Billee sings these songs in a
Heavenly little costume, all shining and gold. The
short skirt and low bodice are built of huge gold
sequin i. The sleeves are of Breton net, creamy
and transparent, and black stockings and snappy
black satin shoes show up vividly. The warbler
wears heavy, corn-colored hair, and looks delight-
fully pretty, singing. Especially as a housemaid
in abbreviated sky-blue silk, with a frilly white
suggestion of an apron, and a tall, sky-blue bow
in her hair. Her last change shows her in black
tights with a black satin cloak lined with white
satin that’s sprinkled all over with little black se-
quins. The bodice is smothered in paste and real
sparkles, and that tiara in the hair is even more
wonderful, as paste, than as the genuine diamond.
I looked in at “ Hamlet ” on Friday and found
Rose Dampier frocking Ophelia in pale-blue dress-
ing-gown-cashmere, with a white fur edging. The
house was crowded, upstairs and down. A
highly intellectual—l might Almost say intelligent
—dress-circle muttered the text under its breath
as the play wound on. Sometimes a discrepancy
arose. The woman in spectacles next to me was
whispering familiar words while Rose was
strangely declaiming from the stage how “Hib-
self the pribrodse path of dalliadce treads ” ; and
how “ t’is id by bebbory locked.”
Luckily, Dampier’s rich, full, satisfying tones
still make up for family deficiencies ; but, con-
sidering Rose’s eccentric enunciation, I think that
Alfred is hardly justified in billing this as “A
Hcicrenlial Production of Hamlet.”
The employes of the Hotel Australia had a
merry dance, last Friday, at Leigh House. At
least, it grew merry after a while, though at first
the dancers seemed to take their steps a trifle
solemnly, as if this was real, this was earnest,
this was. The couples danced looking down, with
their heads close together, and their mouths very
solemn. I sat in the gallery with a friend, and we
had an excellent view of the hop, and learned at
least one profitable lesson—that Society doesn’t
know how to dance. Society can dress. Society
can undress. Society can give a ball. Society
can triumph with lights and decorations, and
supper and war-paint. But dance—oh no. Society
cannot dance. You must go to a ball like
this to see what real dancing is. Pretty house-
maids and chambermaids wore their ball-skirts
just off the ground. Unencumbered by trains
and frills, their feet twinkled and turned?
and tripped and glided, and they whirled
round like feather-weights, never losing an
opportunity to be on the move, as no society
woman, with a ton of jet train dragging round her
feet, can do nowadays. Julia, the comely house-
maid, was belle of the ball in black, and Harry,
the hall-porter, treated us with great kindness,
plied us with oysters, and begged us not to make
ourselves strangers. By the way, the “ Australia ”
won’t know itself presently. The big vestibule
and the sixpenny drinker will be done away with,
a grill-room sweeping them out of the place.
Something novel in the way of frivol will be the
Grand Masked and Advertisement Ball, to come
off in the Town Hall on Sept. li. It is ages since
we had a masked ball here. People seem to think
there’s something loaded about a mask, but this
hop is in aid of the Children’s Hospital, and has
for secretaries Miss End it h Walker and Miss
Martin, so that I can recommend the most timorous
to tak-e tickets.

. A meeting of bewildered Society women was
hastily summoned last Friday by Mrs. O’Kelly
—Madame L’Eclair. Three-fourteen-one was
the text of the discourse. Madame herself, who
undoubtedly worked very hard over the affair,
took the stage, and seemed to be saying that a
Society Committee was an awful frost, and she
only gave away two tickets herself, and that cer-
tain distinguished people who attended sent
never a contribution ; but perhaps I didn’t hear
aright. Afterwards, I asked for definite infor-
mation, and was handed the sheet above. There
was a distressing vagueness about that meeting.
Every now and then a pretty Society girl would
exclaim “ How dreadful! ” and one declared
that it ought to be put in the papers. I believe
“ it” was put in the papers, but what differ-
ence does that make to three-pounds-fourteen-and-
a-penny ?
If the explanation of that munificent benefit is
that Society takes tickets, comes along, and for-
gets to forward its cash, the sooner other people
run these charities the better—for our sick and
wounded sojers. That may be one explanation.
But there is something on the other side that my
feeble pen sticks at, and refuses to pass. Look
across at Expenditure. Who told who to pay
eleven pounds something for decorating the V.R.
box ? Govt. House never assumes that a box at a
charity show is a room in Buckingham Palace.
A few shillingsworth of flowers and ribbons to
tickle the V.R. nose is generally considered
sufficient attention. Cab-hire and cartage at
twelve guineas sounds strong, considering the
thing was being done for Charity. It may be a
novel idea, but I have a theory that taking cabs
never yet helped to swell a Charity balance-sheet.
Evidently the day of the dear good woman who
wore her feet to the ground tramping round to do
things for others is over and done. And then —
but perhaps it would be wiser, and kinder, and
less trouble, to stop right here.
Speaking of charities, His Ex. has delighted the
heart of St. Vincent’s Ball committee with a
cheque for £25, and a friendly letter, assuring
them that he would attend their hop only that he
cannot go to any dances while in mourning for his
brother.
Another V.R. item is that a new secretary has
arrived at Govt. House. It seems a little late in
the day. The Beauchamp regime ends next
January. But, presumably, there is going to be
much extra work at Federation time, when the
Hopetouns arrive as guests of Lord Beauchamp,
and another arm will probably be needed to fight
back the onrush from Woolloomooloo and Darling
Point. The newcomer is Mr. Percival Landon.
He has been out in Africa since the beginning of
the war, and has just got over malaria. An awful
rumor says that he writes for the papers—the
English papers—and that he is very persistent in
saying what he means.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“ Melba’s attempt to check the rush of Aus-
tralian warblers to Europe is pretty well a protest
against the tax which it imposes on her private
purse, although she doesn't put the case in that
way. The reckless emigrants—mostly girls, and
some of them rather old girls—get stranded in
London, and appeal to the wealthy ‘star’ for aid.
She lias grown rather tired of being a bank who
advances coin on the security of an unmarketable
voice. That fact can be seen sticking out from her
recent letter to the Melbourne dailies, wherein she
give- a nasty jar to a little crowd of ex-Australian
scribblers : ‘ Certain contributors to the Australian
press on this side of the water send glowing ac-
counts of the success of such-and-such a one at a
concert or musical soiree, and so mislead those
wao have a budding singer or a musical prodigy
in their family as to the ease with which the great
musical world is conquered. To my own know-
ledge, such indiscriminate puffery has been ap-
plied to unfortunate aspirants, whose present posi-
tion is all but hopeless, and whose future in
musical art has no possibilities.’
“Poor Melba! She might save money, prob-
ably, by giving a handsome cheque to an Anglo-
Australiau Fund for the re-shipment of some ten
or a dozen hopeless voices. I can mention one
Australian girl who managed to get into Mar-
chesi’s academy and was'none the better for a
course of training. She hadn’t a voice to be
trained. As for the damsels who have left this
country with very little money to spend on the
cultivation of merely second-rate voices, their
name is Numerous, and their chances of getting
taken up on ‘spec’ by a competent teacher are
Nil.
“ By the way, I am assured, on good authority,
that the wife of that popular politician is not a
medium, nor has she been a seanceiste or a
patroness of the spook movement since ever so
long ago. Her husband, too, was a mere sower of
wild spiritualistic oats in his younger days,
though, according to what Mr. Max bomebody
would call the ‘ Conventional Fictions of Modern
Australian Journalism,’he remains just as young
and trustful as ever. The fiction sticks to his
name—even in The Bulletin —because he doesn’t
think he could easily shake it off if he tried, and
so lie doesn’t try. Another politician has been
more lucky. Nobody brings blushes to the expan-
sive cheek of Mr. Trenwith by reminding him
that he first took to oratory at the Spiritualists’
Lyceum, where the brethren and sistern used to
meet together for manifestations on Sunday
nights, like Early Christians in ancient Rome.

led to a break which the Naicest members of

patch.
“The news has just reached a broken-hearted
swain that the girl to whom he had been engaged
for two years, and who went to England to see her
relatives prior to entering the married state, has
been and gone and entered it with an Englishman.
She met and vanquished the other fellow very
soon after her arrival, it seems, and now the poor
deserted one is preparing for his funeral to-
morrow. He wears the willow in the most public
way, and takes sympathy on a tablespoon as often
as it comes along.
“ A notable engagement is that of handsome
brunette Miss May Nunn (E. Melb.), daughter of
one of the Buckley-and-Nunn firm. The happy
man is dark, actor-like Mr. Barton (son of Mr. G.
B. Barton and nephew of Mr. Edmund Barton)
step-father and custodian of little Kathleen
Purves M icpherson, the infant heiress, whose
lucrative guardianship from time to time occupies
the attention of the Courts. Kathleen’s money
came from her father, the late Macpherson, whose
charming widow married Mr. Barton and willed
to him the care of her daughter by her first
marriage. Mrs. Purves (wife of J.L.) and various
other family connections have tried unsuccessfully
to get the thickly-gilt orphan legally transferred
to the keeping of the Macpherson clan, but the
powers-that-be elect to leave her with her little
half-brother Barton and the latter’s father, who,
after all, is the guardian chosen for her by her
dead mother. Miss Nunn’s elder sister is the wife
of one of the landed Wes ern District Johnsons,
whose mother, Mrs. Nicholas, died recently at
Toorak.
“Miss Edwards, the poor girl who died under
chloroform at the dentist’s t’other day, was the
only daughter of the superintendent of the Port
Darwin pearling-fleet. She had just come to
Victoria from the far North for a year’s cool
weather, and had braced herself up to go through
with the prescribed large dental operation and
banish toothache at the very outset of her jaunt.

too far around, for an idea for costumes for the

two chums of his wrote, suggesting that the
girls should go as an advertisement for So-and-so’s
food, representing before taking and after. The
artist has felt like a consignment of frozen meat

sters.

“At the Princess’s, a smart though not huge
crowd saw the “ Man of Forty ” for the first time
on August 11. The Madden girls in white
muslin frocks chastened with mourning touches,
and black crests on their glossy heads, were in a
box with their married sister. The belle of the
family-group posed a pretty gloveless hand on the
edge of the box all evening, which reminds me
that another extremely trim pair of hands in the
d.c. that night were the unobtrusive property of
Mr. Murray Smith. Thick, nicely-brushed silver
hair is another string to the bow of said well-
groomed veteran —whom Hawthorn is to know no
more as its representative. Another theatre party
included Janet Lady Clarke in a chic octoroon
toilette, i.e., seven parts white to one black.
“The ‘Man of Forty’ is a queer little play.
Looks as if the author had contrived small
commonplace incidents, remarks, etc., meant to
lead up to big episodes, and then, after putting in
the asterisks, had forgotten to supply the foot-
notes. That lady-journalist, for instance. She’s
an aimless jabberwock, with no more tinker in the
plot than Mr. Noah Lovell has a double rfile.
As a saint, he might just as well not have been
born, though in the part of his own erring brother
lie earns his vittles. There’s a vacancy for a
sinner, and he fills the bill. One of the many
sayings which end in blind alleys is Lovell’s
announcement of his engagement to ‘a widow
with one son.’ His fiancee never crops up again,
so who cares whether she’s ‘a widow with one
son,’a divorcee with 13 daughters.
Yours affectionately,
GOULI-GOUIL.
A vote of the ratepayers of Brisbane is to
be taken on the 23rd to decide whether the
Municipal Council shall control the electric-light
and power supply of the city, or whether the
splendidly-valuable electric privileges shall be
given away to a syndicate for a long term of
years. Now, it is a well-known fact, based on
British experience, that the electric-lighting
business can be done by . the corporation with
great profit to the city, and at a cheaper rate
than a private co. can do it at, seeing that the
co. wants big dividends for its shareholders and
the corporation doesn’t. That is—provided the
corporation is decently honest and has some
grains of sense. So the only possible reason for
voting against municipal ownership would be
that the Brisbane corporation had neither sense
nor honesty ; and even that wouldn’t be a reason
for throwing away a splendid source of profit. It
would be only a reason for getting a better corpor-
ation.

villain : “ If I were a man I would chastise the scoundrel
who said a word against my husband.” And nobody
laughed.

The double of Col. Madley, S.A. Commis-
sioner of Police, can be seen any day in charge of
a cab at Redfern Railway Station, Sydney. From
his stumpy legs right up to his broad, illuminating
smile, he is perfect Madley.

Premier Seddon never was a digger. An old mate
of Seddon’s, who worked with him on the Whaimea
(5.1.), says that Dick was first digger, then store-
keeper, and lastly publican at Teramakau—only
giving up digging when he took the pub.
The office of Deputy Postmaster-General
of the Commonwealth hangs evenly so far as pre-
sent influences go between the Deputy of Vic. and
him of N.S.W. The N.S.W. Deputy has longer
and wider experience and draws the bigger salary,
but Vic.’s Deputy is the younger and more vigor-
ous man.
Rabbi Davis, whose birthday is this week,
has been 38 years connected with Sydney
Synagogue. He is 72 now and was 31 when he
arrived. He taught Hebrew in London for many
years, and was Jewish pastor in Jamaica ere
setting out for Australia. He is kindly, earnest,
and in the lore of his church profound.
A wayback paper, in the course of an
article in high praise of The O’Sullivan, the other
day spoke of him as “the Minister for Words.”
The editor wrote “Works,” but the typesetter
probably knew his former fellow-craftsman best.
Also, Col.-Secretary See is the N.S.W. Minister
for Offence, and Perry is the Minister for Public
Destruction.
Why doesn’t the N.S.W. Minister for
Offence, who is so fond of public spouting, get a
“ghost” to provide him with something reasonably
attractive to say ? The platitudinous stodgery of
Sir John See (that is to be) is as pitiable as it is
intolerable. When did Mr. John See ever say, in
all his politicil and public life, anything that was
not well worth forgetting inside 2secs. ?
