Several notabilities who are booked by
public opinion for seats in the House of Repre-
sentatives may decide not to stand for election.
It may, for instance, occur to men in the position
of G. H. Reid that, after all, the bone is better
than the shadow. Mr. Reid’s bar-practice is now
a very substantial bone, his family has been grow-
ing larger, and he is not becoming younger. And
there are others in like case.
Lord Tennyson, Governor of S.A., was
born just one day before Lord Ivintore, a previous
Governor of the same province. The former dates
from 11 Aug. and the latter from 12 Aug., 1852.
Tennyson still dodges speeches whenever he can.
He declined to go and bid formal farewell to the
Protector’s men because the demonstration was
held on the day the Duke of Edinburgh was
buried —and because he would have to talk.
Gov. Gormanston took his last view of
Sydney last week. Not the best or the worst of
Tasmania’s potentates. He retires now on a
£2OOO a year pension, together with the proceeds
of a well-nursed Irish estate. His private
reminiscences are mostly of the army. He was
on terms of personal friendship with most of the
front army men now engaged in S‘Africa, but
betrays very little of fashionable Anti-Boer
rabidness.
Weiss, Australian champion billiardist,
has a brother (popularly known as “The
Colonel ”) in S’Africa, playing devil’s-pool with
the other Roberts against Old Kruger. He
reckons Oom Paul got away with a long lead,
making an effective use of cannon, and potting the
red very neatly ; but Roberts, by good all-round
strokes, is now even, and expects to win by potting
the Boer champion on the right hand top corner
pocket. Kitchener is holding the jigger.
“ Reform Club” writes :
Visited Watson’s Bay t’other day and had a look at
“Glovelly,'” the erstwhile residence of old Premier Jack
Robertson. What a gloomy spectacle ! The place is now
practically fenceless, and the outbuildings are mere
ruins. Had there been a camera at hand I’d have snap-
shotted some of the remains of the fence, and got them
into print as a warning: “Young man, go into politics
and serve your country instead of severely minding j our
own personal business. Then, just after you die, this is
the kind of fence that people will see round your man-
sion.”
S.A. Premier Holder’s great ambition
is to keep his Ministry alive until invited
to join the first Federal Government. If he
lasts until Xmas, Holder will have put up a
record of eight years of office out of 13 years
of Parliamentary service. Originally a State

when Burra elected him top of the poll in ’B7. He
is just over 50 years of age, and never smoked or
drank anything stronger than W.C.T. U. tea in
all his life.
The N.S.W. lunatic Creswell has been
handed over to his advocate, Edward Priestman,
for conveyance to England in Order to prove
his identity as the “lost Sir Roger.” He
has about as much chance of proving his iden-
tity as any other of the thirty-odd claimants
who have turned up since the famous trial; but
be is one lunatic less for N.S.W. taxpayers to
support—which is something. Priestman, who
seems an honest enthusiast, enters into a bond for
his good treatment.
Mr. Richard Jones, M.L.C. (N.S.W.),
one of Reid’s appointees, trips to Europe as
“Hon. Richard Jones.” Mr. Jones, who is an
active business man and director of numerous
companies, is son of the only man named J ones
ever invited to form a N.S.W. Govt. The Jones
Ministry did not come into being, however, the
Premier-designate advising His Excellency to
send for John Robertson. That elder Jones was
a printer, who started various papers and won
wealth as part-proprietor of Maitland Mercury.
The present Jones is a Maitland native.
Mainly about one J. Langdon Bonython :
J. L. Bonython, knight by the grace of God and King-
ston, owner of S.A. Advertiser and offshoots, a man with
a little body and a big hat, expects to sit in the Federal
Senate. This perky and careful little man is supposed to
have a fortune of six figures. Joined S.A. Advertiser,
then a struggling sheet, about 1870 as a junior reporter,
and ten years later became a proprietor. Later on
bought out everyone else and is now sole owner of a busi-
ness" worth over <£20,000 a year. The Bonythons are an
old Oornish family with big ideas, and the present J.L.’s
great ambition is to re-purchase the ancient family estate
and spend his old age as Baron Bonj thon of Carclew,
Cornwall. Alleged, by the way, that the Advertiser is a
democratic paper.
Of the participants in the trial and death
of O’Farrell, who wounded the lately-deceased
Duke of Edinburgh at Clontarf in ’OB, Judge
Cheeke, who presided, is dead ; James
Martin, Attorney - General, and R. M.
Isaacs, Solicitor-General, who prosecuted,
are dead ; Aspinall and Dailey, who de-
fended, are dead ; William Manning and
Inspector-General M’Lerie, two chief wit-
nesses, are dead ; Archbishop Polding and
Archdeacon M’Eneroe, who attended
O’Farrell in prison, - are dead ; Parkes,
who made political capital with subsequent
degradation out of the episode, is dead ;
and hangman Bull and his assistant, Elliott,
are dead. And now the Duke is dead
himself. O’Farrell was the last man
hanged on the old step-ladder gallows in
Dariinghurst gaol. John Hay, also dead,
was the means of saving him from being
killed by the mob at Clontarf.
Anglican Archdeacon Hales, who recently
died at Launceston (Tas.), came to Victoria with
Bishop Perry in Jan., ’4B. There were also of the
party Rev. Daniel Newham, Dean Macartney, of
Melbourne, Canon Handheld, of St. Peter’s,
Eastern Hill, and Charles Cock, the barrister
(bishop’s secretary). Mr. Handheld, the last
of the six, has just died, aged 71. Hale's
was 78 when he died ; Bishop Perry 84, and Dean
Macartney 94. Hales was in charge of Hei ielbeig
parish in the middle of the century. Handheld,
Hales, and Macartney were Irish, the other three
English.

When Mr. F. Wilkinson, Sydney D.T.’s
S’African war-correspondent, left Sydney, he was
as mild a little man as ever drank weak tea or
caught a butterfly in a net. Now he has got ’em
all on. Just listen :
The Boers retired in a canter. The Lancers picked up a
few stragglers on their sticks ; then, for some reason un-
accountable to us, they calmly wheeled into fours about,
and retired in a solid mass out in the open, to the place
from which they had started.
The Lancers picked up a few Boers on their sticks.
In a word, the Lancers stabbed a few fleeing
farmers in the back and through the stomach.
How soldiering does improve a mild little man !
Judge Tnglis Clark, of Hobart, was
a great friend and admirer of Henry
Parkes in the old statesman’s lifetime.
Once, when dining at Hampden Villa,
a small boy of five or six entered the
drawing-room and looked inquiringly
at the visitor. “ Hullo,” said the
Judge, “whose boy are you?” The
child drew himself up, and, instead
of giving the expected reply of
“Father’s” or “Mother’s,” answered,
proudly, “ I belong to the Federal
Party. Do you ? ” Mr. Clark did.
“0.P.”: Touching The Bul-
LEJJn’s remark that knighthood knocks
at the door of physically-big men in
N.S.W., it is noticeable that big men
occupy most of the front-rank public
positions all over the world. No doubt
the big man’s quiet self-confidence and
comparative immunity from personal
violence has something to do with his
starting on the sea of public strife,
where nerve and avoirdupois amount to
so much and give a constant advantage
over smaller competitors. Physical im-
pregnability seems as necessary in poli-
tics as in the prize-ring, and the man
with large ambitions and small “pre-
sence ” will confine himself, if he be
wise, to a professional or other quiet
walk of life.
Lady Randolph Churchill, who married a
“ mere boy ” t’other day, is said to be almost as
fresh and charming as in the days when she prac-
tised the arts of Primrose Leaguery on her first
husband’s constituents. She used to tell a spicy
story of her visit to the boss “ rebel ” of the elec-
torate — a hairy, scowling blacksmith. He warn’t
going to vote for no bloomin’ lord who didn’t do
no work for his livin’. “But I assure you that
Lord Randolph is the most industrious of men.
Every morning of his life he is up at 7, or earlier,”
said the perfumed vision in the ravishing costume.
“’Ovv do you know what time he gets up?”
growled the blacksmith. “I am his wife,” an-
swered the vision with a killing smile. The
horny-handed elector put down his hammer,
scratched his head, and spoke straight from the
shoulder. “ You’re his wife ! Well, all I can say
is if I was ’im I’d stop in bed' all day. Anyhow,
I’ll vote for your man.”
Some Hiyigmama :
After “Jack” Spooner, the Sydney Evening News war-
correspondent, who lately died of enteric fever in
S’Africa, had “ done ” the Deeming execution, he met
Hangman Jones, by appointment, at the Melb. G.P.O.
steps, at 11 a.m., for an interview. Writer accompanied
Spooner. Jones was that morning as cheerful and
enthusiastic as though lie had come from a matinee
instead of a neck-breaking. “ Where shall we have a
drink, Jones?” asked Spooner. “Oh! just here,” and
the man of hemp pointed to the Elizabeth-street hostelry,
close by. Spooner led towards the private bar. “in
here,” directed Jones, bolting into the 3d. bar. “ What’ll
it be ? ” inquired Spooner. “ A long beer —nothing like a
long beer —a long drop after a short one. Ha ! didn’t I give
him his length to a nicety ? ” and Jones grinned fiendishly.
The long beer disappeared at a gulp, and Jones was gayer
and more enthusiastic than ever. Then he produced
a neat, well-preserved pocket-book almost full of notes
about his executions, and in the open bar he read
them out one after another, giving the names of his vic-
tims, weights, dates, heights, and “ how he died ” —al
written in a careful, distinct style. Having read out the
note in each case, he contributed comments in a quick,
quaint, graphic review, wherein he epitomised the per-
sonality and characteristics of the particular doomed
under discussion. Spooner dexterously pencilled down
the points, and meanwhile the barman' plied Jones with
pints of beer. In an hour-or more the gruesome list was
exhausted. “ Now, if you want any more, 3ee me when
you return to Melbourne Th re’s no knowing when I’m
on the job.” Spooner made the most of the matter in
many columns of gallows-gossip. As for Jones—well, he
declined to hang the woman Thwaites, and, fearing his
occupation was gone, he tlrew a razor across his own
abnormally thick throat..
The Irish - Australian Catholics have
erected a handsome Celtic cross over the grave of
that good old Radical, Francis Longmore, at St.
Kilda (Melb.) cemetery. Longmore, who was net
a Catholic (nor a very ardent Home Ruler, for
that matter), put his name to the famous Grattan
address years ago, and was recognised on that
account particularly as a sterling Irish Nationalist.
Born in the North of Ireland, his tendencies to
Orangeism (if he had any) were nipped in
the bud. As a young boy he felt the iron
hand of the landlord at his heart-strings.
His father and brothers had devoted many
months to draining and filling in a morass,
with the result that when the property
was thus improved the “ rint ” went up, and
father .Longrnore refusing to pay the in-
crease, he and his family were summarily
thrown out, and had to camp in a ditch by
the roadside. This story of his own life
served Francis Longrnore in many platform
speeches on Australian land questions,
speeches which did much to stimulate the
cause of Democracy in Victoria when Graham
Berry was at its head. By the way, David
Syme, of the Age, offered a liberal sub-
scription to the Longrnore memorial, but as
it was a purely Irish Catholic expression of
sentiment, Mr. Symo’s offer was gratefully
declined by the committee.
“G. 8.” : Recently-recorded demise of W.
O. Hodgkinson stirs memories of the time when
he, with others, started Rockhampton News.
Present writer was editor, and had a good time
while it lasted. But Hodgkinson, though cute as
they make ’em, had not learnt the secret of
catering. Went in for literary criticism ; wrote
slashing satirical leaders, but didn’t attend to
paragraphic gossip, and ignored “small-beer”
chronicles. Made the paper good enough for »
small minority, who didn’t advertise, and who
read the News at their pub. or borrowed the
paper from next door. W.O.H. was a smart
man, and as full of energy as a high-pressure
steam-engine. Always had a dozen things on
hand, and was never so weary as when lie rested.
And he had a heart of such broad sympathies and
generous promptings as to render nugatory all his
hard work thereby. He could acquire, but
couldn’t hold. Some other fellow always wanted
—and got it.

Early Prejudice Against Cricket.
In 1748, it was decided that cricket was not an
illegal game under the well known Statute, 9
Anne, cap. 19, the -Court of the King’s bench
holding “ that it was a very manly game, not bad
in itself, but only in the ill-use of it by betting
more than ten pounds on ; but that was' bad and
against the law.” In these early times even, the
pastime was followed by all classes, and Frederick
Prince of Wales, died in 1751 from internal
injuries caused by a blow from a cricket ball
whilst playing at Cliefden House. Nevertheless,
this commingling of aristocrats and plebeians on
the cricket sward was viewed with apprehension,
and repeatedly discountenanced by writers of the
day. Games were played for large stakes.
Ground proprietors and tavern keepers farmed
and advertised matches, the results whereof were
not always above suspicion. The old artillery
ground at Finsbury appears to have been the
earliest scene of action of this class of matches.
But the true birthplace of the game in its present
state was no cockney enclosure, but the broad
open down of the southern counties of England,
and more especially in the great hop-drinking
districts. The large hop fairs, notably that of
Weyhill, were the rendezvous for all comers
from the southern counties, and it is probable that
the great county matches were arranged on these
occasions. The first record of a match preserved
is between Kent and All England, which, judging
from an advertisement in the General Adver-
tiser of the day, was played on August 4, 1746,
at the Artillery Ground, the score being kept in
the modern fashion.—[From the article on
“Cricket,” by H. F. Wilkinson, in the “Encyclo-
paedia Britannica.”]

“Scotty”: These Chinese and niggers
beat us in disregard of death and in numbers, two
great factors in war. I have seen a Hindoo
groom, after being bitten by a snake, refuse to go
to the doctor until he had received his pay, which
was due that morning. He died in front of the
orderly-room. Again, here’s “John’s” estimate
of death. A white man took arsenic, when half-
drunk, in a woolshed, and at the inquest the
Chow-cook deposed : “He tellee me, ‘Me takee
pisen.” Isay, ‘Where you gettem pisen?’ He
say, ‘Along in woolshed.’ ‘Oh !’ me say, ‘in
woolshed —welly good.’ Then me shuttem doah.”
By the coroner, ‘ ‘ Did you see or hear anything
else?” “Oh, yes. Hea’lem yell; hea’lem gloan
—welly bad! an’ kickum doah. Bymebye me
look out. Pooh fella dead ; close up doah.” Such
men are good food for powder. W ounding them
i 3 useless.

Aboriginal murderers figure comparatively
lightly in N.S.N. criminal calendar. No case
like the Governors’ appears on the records. An in-
dividual black killed a white for one reason or
another, but rarely killed two. No reference is
made to the early fights, which were in reality
battles to keep the country to themselves. The
worst, most brutal, bloodthirsty and systematic
murderers N.S.W. has seen were whites. Take
the Jew Boy’s gang, or take John Lynch —mad —
who on his own showing murdered eight persons ;
or Nioholls and Lester, or Butler, who was also
no doubt mad, judging by the pauoity of his osten-
sible motives and the severity of his epileptio fits
after arrest—about which nothing was said at the
trial. It is possibly the white blood in
the senior Governor wmch has carried him beyond
the true aboriginal murder-sphere.

“ P. A.” on parti-colored Marriage :
Kate Jewell, a young woman of wealth and position in
London, who loved and married a Gape Colony stable-boy
who was masquerading as “Prince” Lo Bengula at an
Earl’s Court show, was quickly disillusioned on the sub-
ject of mixed marriages. Several parsons had refused to
tie the knot, but one of them was eventually compelled
to do so, there being no legal bar. The pair went to
Capetown, and two months later the lady had the
“ prince ” up for biting pieces out of her lily-white neck.
The beautiful Princess Chimay, a millionaire’s daughter,
had a similar experience with her black Tsigane husband,
the renowned “ Jimsy.” Women don’t read the papers,
and may be excused for not knowing these things and

Matabele: The missionaries’ connection
with the present trouble in China recalls one or
two things. At the Cape and in Natal “ Mission
Kaffirs ” are rarely employed as house-boys, be-
cause (as a farmer’s wife once explained to me),
“ while a raw Kaffir may be trusted, at least while
his rawness lasts, a mission boy is always a thief
and a liar.” The one Christian doctrine the
Kaffir convert thoroughly grasps is, “ I’m as good
as the white man ” —which he isn’t; for the rest,
he is a Christian so long as there is anything to be
got by it, as food, blankets, &c., and a pagan
again the moment a little war or rebellion places
Christianity at a discount.

* * * *
Harry Stockdale writes apropos of that
recent bogging of wild horses in the snow near
Kiandra (N.S.vV.): —
It is not often that otherwise almost unyardable horses
are thus caught. Horses, unlike cattle, will not face a
storm and travel out of it. They turn their tails to snow
and wind, and thus at times become hemmed in. Then,
as the drifting snow becomes deeper and deeper, they
keep huddled together, the constant stamping of their
feet beating the snow hard down, leaving a wall of un-
trodden snow all round them, and thus, as it were,
“ oorraling ” themselves. Some of the wild horses pas-
turing in the valleys of the snow-olad hills around Kiandra
are of (very good stamp, being the descendants of two
thoroughbreds that escaped many years ago. The local
young fellows, on their snow-shoes, “located” the self-
corraled steeds, went home for their own horses and a
few quiet “decoys,” and returning to the snow enclosure,
roused the prisoners about until they were well winded -
which, owing to the hunger and exposure they had under-
gone, did not take long. Then the men dug a narrow
path out through the drifted snow, and took in the decoys
to give the others a lead out. Then a couple of riders
went in advance, the decoys followed in Indian file, and
in their tracks came the wild horses without any particu-
lar trouble to their captors, and were safely yarded up.
There were in all about 00 head, about 20 of which were
claimed, and the remaining animals, after a little feeding
and handling, were despatched to Melbourne, where they
fetched an average of between £6 and £7 perhheathsd—ths
total cheque being for over £260.

“ Yarrum ’’: It’s well enough known that
the “water-iguana” can climb up a
tree, but few are aware that he can’t
:limb down. He goes up as long as
there is anything to go on ; and'
then —flop into the water. I was
once up an oak-tree after a forked
stick for a catapult ; and,
coming down along a limb, I
met a spacious “goanner”on
the up-joumey. Tried to shoo
him back, but he wouldn’t
budge. It was then I learnt
that they can’t climb back.
I was straddled on the
limb, and tried to vault
over him, but came down
on his back, and we both
> fell into the river. When
I got out he was up on
the limb again, looking down
at me.

* * * •
Out where the rainfall is slightest the
losses through evaporation are heaviest. Broken
Hill Water Supply Co.’s figures show
that, while it supplies about 190,000,000
gal. a year to the mines and other custom-
ers, it loses by evaporation, &c., an average
of 11,000,000,000. Thus it loses between
five and six times more than it sells. Only once
or twice has the co.’s supply been lower, by the
way, than at present, despite the fairly good
season in the Barrier district.
“Gil Guy”: “Scotty” (8., 28/7/’00)
may be right about the horseman being a bad
cavalryman, but he is making a big mistake when
he sets the real bushman—the kangaroo-er, brum-
bv-runner, etc.—below the groom, bullocky, etc.
Has “Scotty” ever camped with a kangarooer,
and tasted emu-egg “flipper,” or tail-soup, or
grilled pigeon ? Has Scotty ever found the groom
or generally-useless who could grill a split wild-
duck on a stirrup-iron ? And the bullocky ! Ye
gods 1 The man of salt cow, sod, and bullocky’s
joy ! The generous, well-educated bullocky !
And “ they don’t read.” Why I never yet met a
dog-trapper who didn’t ask for a bundle of news-
papers along with his rations. Also, “they never
nave anything to say.” Why, half the “ Aborigi-
nalities,” if they were traced to their source,
would be found to come from the camps of drovers,
shooters, musterers, etc. I pass, but ask Will
Ogilvie and “The Banjo” who are the boys to
camp with —the drovers or the bullockys ?
They were perjuring about the fertility of
soils, and a haggard-looking man said from the
corner: “I was once travellm’ through Riverina
in a spring-cart. I had a lot o’ nails with me an’
some o’ them dropped out. When I came back
that way again, d nme if there wasn’t a fine
crop of crowbars.”

A staggerer from London Express, 26th
June:
HUMAN NESTLINGS.
In the bushmen of Australia we find, perhaps, the
lowest order of men that are known. They are so primi-
tive that they do not know enough to build even the
simplest forms of huts for shelter.
The nearest they could approach to it is to gather a lot
of twigs and grass, and taking them into a thicket or
jungle thev build a nest for a home, much as does a
bird. The' nest is usually built large enough for the
family, and if the latter be very numerous, then the
nests'are of a very large size.
Into this place they all turn and snuggle and curl up
together like kittens. Sometimes the foliage will grow
together and form a sort of natural covering, but there is
never any attempt at constructing a protection from the
rain and storms, and it is a marvel how they endure
them.
Where there is a particularly good piece of jungle for
home sites it will be quickly appropriated for the purpose,
and sometimes hundreds of these nests will be found
together in the bush, as it is called.

“Wally”: One hears much about the
caprice of women, but a certain N.S.W. town once
heard a lot about the caprice of a man. He had
led his lady-love to the altar, and at the question,
“Do you take this woman, &c.,” vanished with
the unorthodox response— “No!— l’m d ——if I
do!” Years after, when man and incident were
almost forgotten, he returned and took the lady
to the altar again. This time they got through.

“Milky White”: “ N.Y.A.’s” 6ft,
goanna (B. 30/6/’00) doesn’t take much beating.
There is a stuffed one of that length in the bar of
a Bourke-street (Melb.) pub. Also, the 10 or llin.
through thickest part of “N.Y.A.’s” reptile
might have been bolted rabbit. At Pretty Pine
(N.S.W.), during early spring, and
another, as new-chums, were startled* at seeing a
big fellow basking on the side of a ring-barked
box. It disappeared down a hole when we threw
things at it, but we got the tools, chopped it out,
and slew it, with great fear and trembling.
“ Bah 1 ” said the boss, when we told him of the
(to us) strange monster —“only a goanna! Big
one, is it? Well, all the worse for you. There’s
five bob a foot fine for killing them up here. They
keep down the rabbits.” We computed the fine
with a two-foot rule, and found it exactly 31s. 6^d.
Despite its harmless appearance, star
or rolling-grass, as some people call it, can
upset regulated time-tables. On the line
between Cambooya and Wyreema, Q., star-
grass frequently stops trains on the up-grade
where it accumulates in cuttings. When
crushed it gives the rails a smooth, glassy
surface, and sand has to be freely used to get
a fresh grip.

“A.P.R.”: A good bushman rarely
repeats himself either in swearing or slang-
ing ; for instance, .the shearer terms the rouse-
about variously a “loppy,” “bluetongue,”
‘‘ wop-wop,” ‘ ‘ leather-neck, ” ‘ ‘ crocodile, ”
&c. By-the-way—“ when a chock-an-log sniffs
ole pot-an’-pan, he does a carrington ” means
merely that when a dingo scents man he
decamps.

“ Bushie ” on turkey-nests :
I much question a recent Bulletin correspondent’s
statement that he got 300 eggs out of a scrub-turkey’s
nest. I have personally examined dozens of nests, and
my biggest haul was 29 eggs from a nest fully 20ft. in
dia. and 4ft. high at apex. The birds almost invariably
build on top of an old nest, though I have seen absolutely
new nests of a fair size. I have also watched the turkeys
building—ls in one party ; tails turned towards centre
nest and debris flying with a vengeance. A sure sign of
newness of nest and almost certainty of eggs being there-
in is warmth to the hand when thrust some distance into
the heap. These birds always build at approach of wet
season so as to have the nest ready for the moisture to set
up the required “ heating ” ; but, if insufficient rain falls,
the turkey will not lay. How she manages to retain or
dispose of her eggs I know not, but it is absolute fact—no
rain, no eggs; good rain, plenty eggs. By the way, I
have seen an aboriginal, scratching out a nest, reject the
fresh eggs, open one with a fair-sized chick inside, take
this by the legs, open his “cavern,” and swallow the
chick iike an oyster 1

“Long-Un” on South Australian bush-
\nedieoes :
Having travelled the whole of the populated districts of
S.A., I believe that fully half the country doctors are dip-
somaniacs—the worst cases being generally pronounced
“ such clever men when they’re sober.” This failing .8
probably due to the sudden change from city life and
genial society to the deadly slowness of the avenge
country town. I know of a case in which a bush youth
whose head had been injured by a throw from horseback,
was taken down by his father to the nearest township,
where the local Sawbones drank neat schnapps. “ Bit
soft in the upper storey, is he ?” said the medicine-man.
“ Stand the young —— on his head—that’ll cure him
quicker’n medicine, and it’ll only cost you five bob and
two drinks 1 ” I once bad occasion to seek edi ice from
one of these pub.-haunting gentry. Here it is. “ Look
(oty) here ole man, alwaysh keep a (hie) hoddle of Jimmy
(shy) Hennessy alongshide you—an' (hie) you’ll never
die 1 ” Quite recently I saw in one of our first country
towns a young squatter and his sister who had driven in
20 miles to bring out the leading medical man to a dying
sister ; but as the medico had had a piece of good fortune
only three months previously, he had not had time to
sober-up. The dying girl wanted him and no other, so
the brother drove him around all day in his tra", vainly
trying to sober him. In the evening a trap dathed past
the hotel followed by another. Someone murmured,
“Thank God, sober at last!” But it was the other
doctor—the first medico was still dead to the world.

Re horse'slipping girth, Ac.
(Harry Stockdale, B. 28/7/’00). The bones com-
posing the horse’s withers are at least 3in. higher
than the remainder of the dorsal vertebrae, and
the projecting elbows are in same line with
withers. Taking this into account, in conjunction
with the always semi-circular or upright-keeled
form of the sternum, to which are jointed the true
ribs, it seems impossible for girth to be misplaced
in manner stated. And what has belly to do with
girth? The explanation given by “Bathurst”
(same date) I cannot accept. What has the
“ slipping back ” of saddle and girth to do with
it ? The horse I saw sent rider, &c., fully 3ft.
high and syds. forward —and there was no en-
tanglement of girth in horse’s forelegs, either.

“Pelican”: How is this for bees not
stinging aboriginals? Jack Hamley, bringing
cattle from the Gulf to Gladstone, had with him a
Gulf nigger, who knew naught of bees save the
harmless native variety. Spotting an English
bees-nest overhanging the road, Hamley directed
his black retainer’s attention to it. “ That fella
bee belonga my country, Billy.” “Budgeree
fella?” inquired Billy, scrutinising the tree.

The export of frozen rabbits from Maori-
land increases rapidly. From Port Chalmers
alone, during June last, 22,693 crates of rabbits,
representing a money-value of £17,019, were
shipped.
* • • *
“Alia" : “A.E.C.’s" method of briar
eradication (B. 7/28/’00) is all right. Have often
seen it practised in New England, where big
areas, mostly on the fertile alluvial flats and along
water frontages, are covered by this pest. But
the - trouble always is root-recoveries. A local
squatter, however, goes one better. After clear-
ing by the chain-and-bullock method he runs goats
amongst the sheep placed on the land. Sheep or
cattle will not touch the root shoots, but goats eat
them down in preference to grass; and as the
goats are of Angora breed they help to pay the
bill, too.

N.S.W. Govt. Brewery in Parramatta, in
1804, sold beer of its own brewing at 4d. per quart,
and instead of cash it willingly took hoop-iron,
casks or grain.

Dear Bulletin.— Re natural craving of
animals for salt. Rats will gnaw harness, saddles,
&c. on account of the saline matter of the horse’s
sweat. It has been proved that if salt is left ac-
cessible close at hand, the rats will leave the
harness alone.—Rooster.

‘ Cecil P. ” : lie car-
nivorous plants (B. 26/5/’00),
in Kew Gardens, London,
is to be seen a South
American plant which is
regularly fed with chopped
meat. The absolute line
between vegetable and
animal cannot be drawn,
and the possession, there-
fore, by certain species of
attributes of both king-
doms is only what might
be expected, and is in strict
accordance with the uni-
versal law of evolution.
There was a stage when
the more highly developed
vegetable began to assume
the functions of simple
animal life, which, in turn
became more and more
complex until the highest
organisation was reached.
Another disgusted N.S.W. pedagogue ex-
patiates :
Dear Bulletin.— “ Bush Teacher ” (B. 2/6 ’00) gives a
few facts as to what a country kid-whacker has to put up
with. Let me supplement: The “ 3A” men are entitled
to a salary of £206 a year, including residence, to get
which, however —besides passing an examination—they
have to get three good reports from the Inspector ; and,
to secure these, they mostly have to get out of the best
bed and let said Inspector have it, and get the Mrs. to
shine his boots and feed him up on prime tucker and
whisky—mostly whisky, and afterwards to brighten his
memory about twice a year by sending him a good fat
present. That’s the way the 3A men—about 2 per cent,
of them !—get what they are entitled to ; the other 93
generally stick at £ll3 to £l5O a year because they can't
afford to do the needful, as above, and because they are
generally placed in charge of schools where the rain comes
down, and the rivers get up, and the kids have to plant
corn and spuds and mind the baby, and keep the pig- out
of the barley—thus keeping the attendance so low tlfat
getting a good report and a rise is hopelessly out of the
question. Then the way they have to live ! I wonder
where the lucky beggar is placed who wrote the par in
The Bulletin about teachers’ games of tennis, &c. Here’s
the kind of spot I struck—and not 100 miles from Sydney
either. I put in 4J y'ears there, and was always sure Of
having at least four yarns in the week- two to the mail-
man and two to a couple of sleeper-getters. I boarded
for 10 months, but found that I was doing too well on
oatmeal-and-water for breakfast, bread and jam for
dinner, and bread and “ salt-horse ” for tea, so I slept on
the ceiling of the school the remainder of my time, and
cooked under a few sheets of iron. Oh, it’s'a splendid
profession, this kid-whacking business.

It is alleged that several of the names
given to the N.S.W. Federal electorates denote
indecencies. This is not improbable. Aborigines
name rivers and ridges from fancied resemblances
to parts of their own bodies. Queensland Govt,
adopted aboriginal names for new townships some
years ago, but suddenly halted. There are towns
there now that no one dares translate into English ;
while the Maoriland geography contains more in-
decencies than a standard Slang Dictionary.

The gentle Chow. An ex-Sydney scribe
writes from China to Sydney E. News :
Yesterday I saw a man who is being strangled to death
in the Chinese city adjoining the settlement. The fellow
had been tried at the Mixed Court here for cutting two
men’s eyes out with a knife, and he had several murders
against him, as well as other charges. We thought the
Chinese Govt, would behead him ; but they condemned
him to slow strangulation. He stands in a thing like I
have drawn, right in the busiest street of the city.
Europeans give him a couple of dollars to hold up his
head while they photograph him. He only went in yes-
terday, and was eating and talking, smilingly, with the
people around. They reckon he'll be choked on the fifth
day, when all the stones are taken down from under his
feet. These stones are removed one by one, day by day,
so that at last he is hanged, after having been gradually
strangled. He may live for four or five days, but he’s in
the thing night and day. Horrible, isn’t it? But his
crime was horrible, too.
Proposed in Britain to discourage the
despatch of missionaries of either sex to China by
refusing to extend them even nominal protection,
and letting it be quite understood that in the
future any Chow who amuses himself any dull
afternoon by pouring boiling lead over a mission-
ary will not be called to account by an admiral
with a fleet behind him. To refuse armed protec-
tion to missionaries is logical; they go to preach
what is essentially a gospel of peace and non-
resistance and forgiveness of injuries, and to do
that backed up by troops, fleets, and cannon is
pure burlesque.

Writes a Victorian, settled in the
Transvaal:—
The man who first hoisted the Union Jack at Johannes-
berg was a Kaffir. It was this way. A number of
Uitlanders met at the office of one of their number to
privately celebrate Lord Roberts’ occupation of Bloem-
fontein. A big Union Jack was displayed at one end of
the room. After champagne had flowed freely, it was
inanely suggested that the flag ought to have some, too,
so it was forthwith drenched with the expensive tipple.
Next morning the Kaffir “ boy,” who came to put things
straight, noticed the forlorn condition of the bunting, and
prosaically hung it out to dry, over the balcony. Boer
feeling was very bitter just then, and if the proprietor
hadn’t turned up in time to take in the flag there would
have been great trouble.
Wake Up!
You are an energetic man—an ambitious one.
But it is hard to rise in tlie morning. You feel
that you could take just one more nap, and one
more and still more.
Your head is heavy.
You dislike to get up.
Your mouth tastes badly.
Your breakfast does not suit.
You are excitable. When you go to work you
don’t feel like it. You fight the feeling all day,
and a couple of hours before office closing time
you turn in and work like a hero. Next morning
the same thing over again.
That isn’t laziness —it’s illness, real serious ill-
ness. Later it will develop into something even
worse—liver trouble or kidney disease follow in
the wake of just such a condition. There is a
remedy that will put you on your feet, a specific
for kidney and liver troubles, Warner’s Safe Cure.
It will wake up your liver, give you an appetite,
allow you to ensure needed regular rest and sleep
at night, invigorate the system, and dispel all
symptoms of liver disease.
Mr. F. A. Burt, of Bourke, N.S.W., writes:
“ I have much pleasure in informing you that just
about twelve months ago I suffered from a pain in

DE. BOSS’S N.S.W. BOYS’ ANTI-SMOKING BILL.
'—There may be a difficulty in determining
a Quid’s age, because in this country some
children of 16 are very large for their
age. In Committee I will be prepared
to amend the first clause by insert-
ing words to the effect that it
should apply to a child actually
or apparently of a certain
age.
Dr. Roes : I have only one son, and I am proud
to say that although 46 years of age, he has not
smoked tobacco, cigars, or cigarettes, or chewed
tohaoco, or taken snuff.
That boy will have to be watched.
Depend on it he will yet be caught behind
the woodshed taking a sly whiff.
Mr. J. C. L. Fitzpatrick (Rylsfcone): It only provides against persons
under the age of 16 smoking in public streets, public thoroughfares, public
places, and public Those is no provision which will debar
any youth under that age from hiring a boat in Woolloomooloo Bay and
going out on the harbor and smoking there until he is black in the face.

The 3rd clause provides for children who may re
quire medical treatment, for instance, for asthma. I
would not like to pass a provision which would prevent
a child from smoking
a cigar for medicinal
purposes.
Suppose such a boy is caught smoking
a cigarette, and no birth-certificate can
be forthcoming. Suppose the boy, while
looking 13|. years of age, is 17 i years
of age, how is the bench to decide,
such a case ?
Dr. Itoss (Molong) rose to move :
That this Bill bo now read the second time.
7"* .. , Its object is to prohibit, as far as possible,
juveniles under a certain age from- using tobacco, cigars, and cigarettes. It .will be
a disgrace to New South Wales, the mother colony, if this measure does not become
law, but it will be no disgrace to me. T only seek to protect the children who are
igrowing up amongst us, so that they may not go to premature graves.
Mr. G. H. Reid : I never tried to smoke but once
in my life and then I was sick for a week afterwards.
Mr. Sleath : I would suggest to the hon. and learned member
for Molong, that instead of inserting in the Bill an age-limit, he
should insert a size-limit. Let it be
provided, for example, that no
unless he were under the height
of 5 feet 3| inches, should be
allowed to smoke cigar-
ettes. There would
be no difficulty
in the matter.

when females are suffering from

plaints they sometimes resort to
smoking. -i. J )

imagine a case of this kind: A

swallow a bull’s eye.

POLITICAL
POINTS
They now call Lyne’s the “ round-robin ”
Ministry. The Premier will risk nothing before a
big majority of members has been openly sounded.
Suggested in print that Edmund Barton
should succeed “Willie Governor” —taking over
the rags of vice-regality, the paper-bag emptied
of its apples.
There is just a chance that the Royal
Commission now enquiring into various smellful
matters of Q. Works department may prove the
“happy despatch ” of the Boodlewraith.
Several highly-placed Q. politicians do not
intend standing for the Federal Parliament. For
the present, at least, you see, the railways will
still be controlled by the local Legislatures.
The Sydney City Railway is likely to cost
B. R. Wise his seat at Ashfield, and will dis-
count his prospects for the Federal Parliament
should Parochialism be a factor in Commonwealth
; politics.
The Q. Govt, uses the old local electoral
names for proposed Federal electorates up North.
No imagination is available, all that commodity
being used to extol the virtues of various syndi-
cate-railways now on the board.
The facts in connection with the resigna-
tion of N.S.W. Govt. Whip Carroll, anti his
sudden return to the Ministerial fold, would
supply material for a political comedy. The press-
inspired “ facts” are leagues from the truth.
The latest attempt of Major Steward,
M.H.R., to enact that Maoriland Ministers shall
be elected by Parliament was only defeated
by 33 votes to 24, though the vast weight of the
Seddon Govt, was thrown against the measure.
The Elective Ministry is coming right enough.
The Holder Ministry (S.A.) is generally
supposed to be only hanging on to office by a
majority of two or three, and at latest the
Solomon-Rounsevell opposition crowd was busy
tempting weak Ministerialists with offers of seats
in a new Govt, as the price of their desertion.
The opinion gaining favor among a certain
section of Federalists is that the State Governors
shall be local men, but shall be nominated by the
Gov.-General. What is gained by this latter
condition ? Why should they not be elected by
the people, as in the IJ.Si, or appointed by the
Executive Council ?
Concerning the Bill drafted for presenta-
tion to Q. Parliament prohibiting the continuance
of trials for capital offences after.fi p.m. (8., July
28), The Bulletin is informed that the Bill is
mainly due to A. H. Barlow, who was greatly
influenced to take the matter up by The Bul-
letin’s remarks on Judge Gibson’s conduct at the
Ensor trial at Broken Hill.
TLEIN-AIR
PAINTERS.
STLASH : “ Going out
painting this afternoon?”
SMUDGE : “ Yes, Pm going to have a smack at
that sunrise I started yesterday.”
Ex-Speaker Abbott, whose health unfits
him for the Chair of N.S.W. Parliament, and,
generally, for ordinary Parliamentary work, an-
nounces his intention to stand for the Federal
Senate.' He is also in favor of a member sitting
in both Commonwealth and State Parliaments,
and will probably hold a seat in each, if allowed.
J. P. Abbott has for years held prominent posts
in N.S.W. politics for some of the reasons which
keep the Sick Man of Europe to the fore, but
the new wave of national life is certain to engulf
many pretentious careers.
The insistence with which the Rhodes-
Chamberlain gang sticks to its plan of getting the
Australian troopers to join the S’African Con-
stabulary emphasises a recent remark here made
that, by t ;e strange whirligig of time, Australia,
originally peopled by convicts, had come to export
policemen. Our Great National Atonement!
Next sensation in N.S.W. politics : A re-
solution imposing ad valorem, duties. In the
Jennings period the Ministry was composed of
fieetradeand Protectionist elements, much as the
present is, but the freetrade members, including
Want, boldly declared ad valorem duties did not
touch the fiscal issue. A convenient gospel now.
The Maoriland M.P’s. who voted for the
right of free speech and against the dismissal of
HANSAitn-chief Grey for daring to protest against
the Boer war are—Atkinson, Collins, Ell, J.
Hutcheson, Lang, Lawry, McLacldan, Pirani,
G. VV. ltussell, W. R. Russell. Some of them are
Jingoes, but they are honest and manly Jingoes.
That a member of the Executive should
be professionally concerned in the defence of a
prisoner arraigned at Criminal Sessions is a
scandal and cannot lie justified an any grounds.
You can’t logically act as Judge of Appeal (at the
Executive table) after having acted as counsel or
attorney for the defence at the court. Executive
and judicial capacities must be remorselessly
divorced.
Is the Australian assimilating himself
rather to the Latin than to the Teuton in his
increasing liability to panic ? Lyne’s Sydney
Harbor Frontages Resumption Bill is still hung
up in N.S.W. Leg. Council, and no one is worry-
ing, but if the plague were still with us that Bill
would have been rushed through all its stages in
one night, and a wild-eyed person would have
roused up Gov. Beauchamp in the small hours for
his sign-manual thereto.
Seven members of N S.W, Assembly,
including John Norton, voted against the proposal
to adjourn because of the death of that German
princeling, the Duke of Saxe-Coburg and Gotha.
“Anyhow” commented John, “seven’s a respect-
able number. Look at the seven sages, the seven
churches of Asia, the—” “and the Seven Deadly
Sins ” chipped in a voice from the other side. Also,
weren’t seven devils cast out of some gentlemen
in the New Testament ?
Ed. Bulletin, —I ask you, who have
always supported purely secular State education,
to let me invite some of the rev. gentlemen who
are now so loudly advocating Bible-reading in
public schools; to show the justice of their scheme.
Why should Jewish parents (and Catholic ones)
who are taxed to support the State school, be
mulcted for the purpose of subsidising the teaching
of Protestantism—for that is what Bible-reading
amounts to.— A. Collins Woinarski.
What if an Imperial Ministry subsidised
sections of the London daily press by giving
to its war - correspondents semi - sinecures
with substantial pay out of the public Trea-
sury, and providing them with every facility
for doing presswork ? Certainly the Govt, would
fall, in the first instance, and, in the second, the
public would not believe a word such subsidised
scribes would write. If old man Parkes were in
the N.S.W. Parliament now he would pull down
the roof on the heads of the Ministers, and the
very press to whose families and business interests
they now pander at public expense will, when
they have milked them, rend them in pieces.
Something quite new in the way of de-
fence proposals has been put before N.S. W. mili-
tary people. The suggestion is that the
■j way-back stations which can
muster a dozen willing
horseman shall be the
centre of “rifle units,”
Govt, providing rifles
and ammunition on the
same terms as rifle-clubs
get them, the station-
owners or managers being
responsible for the rifles. But
in connection with any such
movement there ought to be
tacked on to the conditions one that
there should be no call for service
other than to repel a foreign in-
vader. ,
The Industrial Arbitration
Bill (N.S.W.) is being fought
almost as unfairly as the
Federal Bill was. Here, for
instance, is one leading em-
ployers’ association’s protest :
We hold that the measure is
inopportune at the present time,
inasmuch as conciliation and arbi-
tration for the pi evention and settle-
ment of industrial disputes are speci-
fically reserved in the Federal Constitu-
tion tor the Federal Parliament.
But, as a matter of fact, the Federal
Parliament is only empowered to legislate with
regard to disputes affecting two or more States ;
so that all the miners or all the carpenters or all
the engine-drivers of N.S.W. might be on strike,
and the law which the Federal Parliament is en-
titled to make couldn’t touch the matter. Besides
there is no specific reservation of power to the
Federal Parliament, anyhow.
The stolidly parochial Brisbane Tele-
graph, probably the most consistently ignorant
and mendacious city paper in Australasia, put up
a record by hinting, in its issue of 2nd Aug., that
the Westralian Federal \ -e must be a wicki d
Federationist swindle, because out of “ 7<>,7oG ma'e
adults,” of whom probably only 51,138 were avail-
able for voting purposes, you couldn't get a vote of
70,000, or so, as shown in the polling returns. The
Get that women have a vote in Westralia is evi-
dently not known to Brisbane Telegraph yet.
That paper is only sufficiently up-to-date to know
that Queen Anne is seriously ill, and fears are
entertained that the old lady is breaking up. The
same Telegraph came out recently with a rather
dull anecdote about the “ King of Venice” —who-
ever he might have been. Probably he was
brother to the Due d’Moses.
Rhodes’ bribe to Australian bushmen to
settle in Rhodesia:
The conditions are— to Australians of good character,
with a knowledge of cattle and sheep—3ooo acres of land
to be purchased at lOd. an acre, payments to extend over
a number of years, with 500 head of cattle, and £25 per
year for keep. In return settlers are to give half the in-
crease of cattle and to attend military drill every quarter.
“ Pukaki ’’ : Almost certain that M.L. will
raise a big loan before long. Ministers keep on
half-heartedly denying it, but the majority of
their followers seem determined to force their
hand. Amusing to note how, whenever the
subject crops up, each mem-
ber rises and deprecates
the idea, and then goes
on to say that, if a loan
is raised, his district
ought to get its whack
of the
boodle.

about the S’African

is to the effect that portion

It seems that when Tommy
could get nothing better,
he used to extract the
strings of cordite from his
cartridges and eat them.
Cordite looks like gelatine,
and is made from nitro-
glycerine, and a hungry
man can get up quite a
respectable drunk on a •
couple of cartridges. On
several occasions, it is
alleged, a good part of
the aggregate T. At-
kins had got so tight
on part of his am-

he cou Id n’t

good pur-

rest. Query.
Did any of those men who
were allegedly killed by ex-
plosive bullets simply fall
down and explode their con-
tents by the shock ?
Westralia leads the way
in some things, and one of them
is the new idea of issuinjg monthly
a Drief, abstract of the province’s statistics.
Half the advantage of statistics consists in
having them constantly kept up to date.
By this method the principal figures relating
to Westralian affairs will never be more than
six weeks in arrear. In the other provinces
they are mostly 12 months behind. This time
W.A. comes pretty close to leading the world.
There is a serious split in the ranks of
Sydney Italians over King Humbert’s death.
The Royalist Italians are comparatively few;
most of the fruit-vendors and ice-cream men (who
form the bulk of the local community) are Social-
ists or Anarchists who are devoid of sympathy
with the Italian Royal house.
The influence of the Sydney dailies upon
the Lyne Govt, seems to be exerted on a “ scratch-
me-scratch-thee ” principle. The fact that two
Telegraph reporters had been transferred to
N.S.W. Agent-General’s office in London was
recently noted ; the son of D.T. manager Wynne,
reporter with the Chinese contingent, was sent
away as salaried paymaster ; and it now tran-
spires that D.T. managing-director Carey was re-
cently appointed a trustee of N.S.W. Govt.
Savings Bank. Also, isn’t “ Major” Carey to be
chairman of the Harbor-Resumption Board ? The
Government organ—yes, by Jingo !
The Bulletin has been tabooed from the
Maoriland M.L. Parliamentary Library. Yet
when Premier Seddon—he ruled then and he
rules now—visited Australia, he called in quite
spontaneously at The Bulletin office to thank
tiiis paper for all it had done in the cause of
democracy, The Bulletin, which never runs
after any politician, took it as a compliment, but
didn't mention the fact. Nor did it men-
tion that more than once it has received
from him enthusiastic cablegrams, thanking
it for the help its leading articles had given to
him at the elections'. It somehow can’t help
mentioning these things now. The Bulletin
will continue to help Premier Seddon all it knows
in his democratic and cosmopolitan capacity. In
his capacity as a Jingo and a bigoted Tyke it will
continue to jump upon him with all its feet.
The new Sydney Freetrade paper, Our
Country, covers nearly a page with a kind of
reply to The Bulletin’s article of 4th August,
“The Foreign-Shoddyists of N.S.W.” And in
all that spacious rejoinder it doesn’t once touch
on the matter which almost wholly occupied the
article it is replyingto. Said matter wasthatduring
the recent freetrade period, 1895-09 (it wasn’t
really freetrade, but still it was the only period
when N.S.W. came reasonably close to freetrade),
N.S.W. exacted from its people £1,529,413 less in
Customs revenue than Victoria did, and made it
up by charging to loans £1,017,237 more of
the ordinary un-reproductive works properly
payable out of revenue than Victoria did.
This is according to the figures of N.S.W.’s
own Statistician Coghlan, who divides the loan
expenditure between “works yielding direct
revenue ” and other works. Therefore, the alleged
“ lifting of the burdens off the people ” was a mere •
shifting them on to posterity in the shape of loans.
And the great Freetrade period in N.S.W., as
compared with the corresponding Protectionist
period in Victoria, was simply one in which Free-
trade appealed to the people by pointing to ita-
own beauties as a policy that could reduce taxa-
tion and yet keep the country solvent—and care-
THE DANGERS OF TEMPERANCE.
CHORLRY : “ But , my deah fellah, at your age
you ought to be working. What’s wrong

Tasmania’s House of Assembly, being
invited to again renew the concession of the Great
Western Land Grant Kail way Co.—that unspeak-
ably insolent concern which has been going to
build a railway for Heaven knows how many
years and which never got started—had a sudden
access of common sense and energy, and dropped
the whole swindle overboard by a majority of
two. And after this experience, if Tasmania
listens to any more oily gentlemen in the
land-grant rail way business it deserves the
inevitable consequences. It was the vote of
R. C. Patterson, of Hobart, that settled the ques-
tion. The division was 18 and 16 on Braddon’s
motion that the Bill be shelved. If Patterson
had voted with the syndicate the numbers would
have been even, and Speaker Nick Brown, on the
skirt of whose district (Cumberland) the railway
would run, daren’t vote against the line even if he
were so inclined. A queer thing about the divi-
sion was that Gaffney, a West Coast member,
went against the Bill, but Gaffney owns the
Palace Hotel at Strahan, the port of the West,
and where the harbour works are being begun.
Patterson has apparently ruined his chances of
re-election for Hobart, but he has done a good
thing for Tasmania at large. The night the Bill
was so justly squelched, an indignation meeting
was held at the Town Hall, when R.C.P. was
called “ traitor” and “falsepretender,” and there
were cries of “turn him out!” It was resolved
to send round for signature a requisition calling
on him to resign. Director Rymill went over
from Adelaide to barrack for the Bill, and ad-
dressed a public meeting in Hobart the morning
of the day it was thrown out, and tried to explain
that Patterson was against the impudent land-
grab from motives of personal enmity. The Tas.
Northern members voted in a solid block against
the Bill. They held a caucus and agreed not to
discuss it, and Braddon was the only one of them
that made a speech. When the first syndicate
got its Bill late in ’96, Braddon, who was then
Premier, sat up all night and successfully resisted
the Northern stonewall.

BRISBANE AND NEWCASTLE.
Colonel Parrott, in a recent letter from
S’Africa, says that “ there are only 18 left (includ-
ing two officers) out of the 110 members of the
Australian Horse that left N.S.W. in the Surrey.
Killed, wounded, prisoners, sick and missing,
account for the balance.”
Letters from the Ist Australian Bushmen
(Airey’s.lot) give a terrible account of things. The
country a fever-nest —60 per cent, of the men down
with sickness. The whole thing a hideous bungle
—nothing but incompetence and mismanagement.
The bulk of the Imperial officers placed in com-
mand no good whatever ; among them lots of
persons who would make perfect stage-idiots,
like Tweenwayes in “The Amazons.” “Here,
for instance,” writes The Bulletin’s informant,
“are a few local Johnny gems : —One distinguished
officah, travelling on the road, turned to his com-
panion, while cleaning his teeth, and said in a
languid voice, ‘l’ve not half finished cleaning
my teeth, dear boy, and I feel quite exhausted.’
Another actually bawled out at the club, ‘ Haw,
I’ve sent a cable home for toothpicks—l, haw—
cawn’t get any heah that I like.’ Another said to
his servant, referring to a tin of boulli-beef:
‘ Haw, M , just taste that and tell me what
you think; and if it isn’t too beastly I’ll try it.’
Talk about ‘trying it on the dog!’ You can
imagine what the exponents of such idiotic affec-
tations are capable of.”
A solicitor at Grafton (N.S.W.) was,
t’other day, applying for the postponement of a
case owing to certain accidental injuries which
one of the parties had met with. The Bench
asked for a medical certificate. The solicitor had
not. procured that, as no medico was treating the
patient; but, instead, he produced a blood-
stained sock worn by the suitor when the casualty
occurred. Case adjourned !

Mr. LENNON BYRNETON, of 88 Oothara-road, Kew,
(Vic.), says :
“ I ani 72 years of age, and for a long time my life was
despaired of. My disease was a complication of troubles,
viz., Liver and Kidney Complaints. Sometimes I was so
ill I could not sleep or take any food, and the pains were
so bad that the doctors said that there was nothing for
me but an ‘operation.’ My friends in Williamstown
heard of the wonderful cures brought about by the Great
Remedy, VITADATIO, and urged me to call upon Mr.
Palmer, Agent for VITADATIO, and get his advice about
my case. I did so, and he assured me that VITADATIO
would cure me, and I am delighted to say, after taking
six large bottles, I am once again restored to good health,
and I feel as if I shall never be able to say enough in favor
of the Great Remedy. Anyone who would like a personal
interview with me can do so at the above address.
“ Yours sincerely, LENNON BYRNETON.”

A Melbourne weekly announced in a re-
cent issue that a much-advertised local poster-
artist had completed a carved door subject—a
nude female figure with graceful sweeping draperies.
The draped nude is good.
An application by the Dental Board of
Victoria for recognition of its diplomas in Great
Britain has been refused by the General Medical
Council of “down under” [vide Lancet, June 2).
The British Dental Education and Exam. Com-
mittee explained to the learned Council that the
present regulations for exams., by Vic. Dental
Board were not quite O.K. At same meeting the
Council decided to enter the name of E. L. Old-
field on their Colonial Dentists’ Register, in
special consideration of his services to Dental
Surgery in Vic. where he has been giving fits to
aforesaid Dental Board for many years. So the
indefatigable Oldfield scores again—a remarkable
instance of; how a popular amateur tenor can
manage to stick to his professional last. The
Dental College and Hospital of Vic., founded by
him in 1895, promises to outlive its toughest
opponents.
Queensland Govt, also does a little in the
way of repurchasing large estates for closer settle-
ment, and its wonderful Q.N. Bank is generally
moaning op the Treasury door -mat for the
State to take over for subdivision some of
the big properties with which it was struck
in the boom-time. The latest trouble is over a
great southern squatting estate, which Govt, can’t
see it's way to buy at the bank’s price, and which
the bank can’t sell for less without loss. In
view of the fact that there is a. constant and
genuine demand for small farms in Queensland,
N.S.W., and Victoria, it is really remarkable that
the reconswindled banks which have been stuck
with huge properties don’t go in themselves for
subdividing the more suitable of them, making
roads through them, and selling them as small
farms on easy terms, with, possibly, a township
and public-house in the centre. Maoriland’s ex-
perience shows that the business can be done pro-
fitably.

* -A- BIRD’S KTOTE.
This is the season the birds tune up their little flutes and prepare those wonderful
melodies that soon, through dale and leafy bower, proclaim the dawn of Spring.
Australia boasts a wonderful variety of birds. They are mostly found in the bush,
but still there are lots in the towns. In the streets we meet black birds and red
birds, while one does not always have to go that far to strike lyres and jackasses.
These latter amuse us. They are such splendid imitators. They will imitate any-
thing from an opera to a bicycle advt. Still, we like them, because they make us
laugh, and that does us good. Then we remember that the RED BIRDS are the
pride of Australia, and get our bicycle out and have a run through the forest
glades. THE WOLD BICYCLE CO. LTD., 64 Kingr Street, Sydney. Tel. 3160.
SPORTING NOTIONS.
Twas only fitting that Mo. should have a good
time at Tatts. meeting (Kandwick) on Saturday, but
results were better than good from his stand-point. He
got away with a huge haul and hasn’t felt so contented
with things in general for a long time. Tramway Handi-
cap (6 fur.) opened the ball and looked a moral for
Haulette, Gunnedah or Reviver, but Bange(ls and 20 to l
against) got home easily a length and a half ahead of
Dozen in 1.14 J. Same nag, over same distance previous
Monday (starting at 7 to 4 on) was donkey-licked by
Gallinain 1.16 J ; she carried 141bs. less (7.8) then. Daring
(name your own price) scored in Aug. Stakes and Mo.
didn’t have to pay out a cent. Dandy (even money) got
home in Hampden Stakes like the great racehorse he is ;
but well-backed The Chief, Hautboy and Schimmel
(last two promising Derby colts) saved Ikey’s book
and left him with a margin on the right
side. Triem, who would have annexed Aug. Stakes but
for being badly blocked- thrice, lined up 2nd favorite (5
to 1 against) in Welter Handicap, and just pipped abso-
lutely friendless Underhand by a short head. Last event
(Spring Handicap) was the biggest staggerer of all for
punters. Fielder’s St. Clare and Brown’s Alix divided
first favoritism (3 to 1), Johansen was next (6 to 1), and
a heap of money went on each. Promise and Carberra
(atlo’s) all carried big commissions, but not one of them
finished among first four. Winner turned up in Metal —
Edna aged gelding Blue Metal, with 20 to 1 chances (Tur-
bine and Little Joe) second and third. Writer watched
on course and made enquiries at Sydney Tatt.’s rooms
that night, but couldn’t see or hear of a Mo. who had
written Blue Metal’s name. Blue Metal only recently re-
sumed work after a long spell, hence his being totally
neglected. Marvellous how a slice of the sweep makes
’em all “ cut.”

• * * *
That most respectable grey horse, Chelsea,
owned by Col. Campbell (the gentleman who stands be-
hind Waxellency Madden’s chair at “functions” and
otherwise swells the triumph of the vice-regal train) won
the four-mile Steeplechase at Caulfield, the price—10 to 1
—being as respectable as all the other surroundings of
the victory. Chelsea didn’t “ land a coup for the gallant
OoloneV’or anything of that sort. He just carried the
modest top-weight of 11.10 which had been earned by
good honest performances in public, and he won because
the others, bar Mysore, were commoners. Mysore “ ran
off ” once agajp. A few weeks ago, when he happened to
run straight, friend Mysore beat Chelsea at Caulfield, and
one of these days he may repeat the offence, but mean-
while the good grey ranks as Australia’s champion ’chaser.
He is quite a young one (5 years) at the game, and a safe
jumper (although he fell in Flemington Grand National),
and the public hope he will not get too hardly treated by
the handicapper in future.
♦ * • •
Two years in succession Caulfield G.N. Steeple-
chase has gone to a grey, with top weight on its back,
Domino last year and Chelsea this. Again the question :
why is it that the colour rarely comes to the front on the
flat, but gets more than an average proportion of success
in jumping races? Because, with the exceptions that
prove the rule, it is slow but sure. One of the exceptions,
Reaper, won a 7-furlong race at Caulfield on Saturday
and may be heard of in the Cups anon.

Although at a very outside price, it is quite
possible that the smart mare Felicity (7.4) will beat many
of the cracks in the approaching Epsom.

The much-talked-of Melb. Cup candidate
Lancaster is to cross the border and go for either Epsom
or Metrop., in which he is handicapped respectively at
7.12 and 7.9. If he has any Cup pretensions he should
surely account for either of these events.

Sale of famous St. Albans’ stud in Melb. on
Friday drew a big crowd ; 21 of 25 catalogued were sub-
mitted and all sold. Brilliant little Carbine-Oratava,
(imp.) mare, La Carabine drew most attention. Bidding
started at £5OO, but S’Rupert Clarke at once bid 800 gs.
and finally got her at 1200 gs. Bobadil brought 500 gs—
. half what was expected. Kinglike (with the en-
dorsement of recent good performances at Randwiek)
went for 900 gs. Barragunda (half-brother to Bill of Port-
land) fetched 6)ogs. from Sydney trainer Earnshaw. Mo.
Murray’s bid (575g5.) secured Debenture, but writer has
ah idea it was for “ Teddy ’’ Knight, owner of sensational
Mora, in-and-out Blue Cap and others. Purchaser
(Scobie) of Kinglike also got the Trenton-VVater Lily geld-
ing Revenue for 725g5.
• * * *
The dispersal of the late W. R. Wilson’s horses
in training promises to make the Spring handicaps more
interesting than if La Carabine and Co. had all remained
in one stable. Leslie Macdonald (Wilson’s manager)
bought some of the expensive lots, but it doesn’t follow
that he got the pick of the basket, considering that the
Sf. Albans people were very apt to make mistakes.
Rumor fell to Phil. Heywood, Headland was a bargain
to H. Bellamy, and Ike Earnshaw bought the promising
half-brother to Wallace. And nobody knows how much
these horses may be improved, or otherwise, by a change
cf air.
* » • •
The V.R.C. did only a fair thing in cancelling,
after a lapse of years, the disqualification of S’Rupert
Clarke “asif it had never been incurred.” There was
little excuse for the Club’s hasty sentence. As a shipper
of horses to India, the guileleis Rupert Clarke bought
through his agent a racing pony for export. Previously
it had been entered for races at unregistered meetings,
and the vendor, who retained the animal in his charge
after the sale had been made, ran it out for one of its
engagements. The V.R.C. committee disqualified the
new owner without asking him for an explanation of his
offence against Club law. S’Rupert felt aggrieved, as any
straight-going sport would have felt under the circs., and
the mere official removal of the disqualification didn’t
quite satisfy him. So he cut off his nose to spite his face,
as it were, by withdrawing his colors from the turf in
Victoria until the V.R.C. practically asked him for “ the
favor of his continued support.” And inasmuch as any
decent sort of horse-owner is worth running after nowa-
days, the recapture of this well-gilded specimen was a
solemn duty.

* * • B
W. P. Crick, who is a candidate for A.J.C.
committee, issues a circular to the members stating the
reforms he intends to advocate 1. That all enquiries be
open to the public, so that no man may be condemned in
Star Chamber fashion. 2. Better representation on the
committee of the large racing centres in the country. 3.
That the tramway be carried on to the course. 4. The
abolition of the shilling turnstile for members. 6. The
appointment of a refreshment committee to approve of
the caterer’s supplies. 6. The right of the committee to
enquire into any case of late scratching which is attended
with suspicious surroundings. Mr. Crick adds that the
supremacy of the parent club can only be maintained by
keeping pace with the times. Also he promises that if
elected to the committee many palpable cases of pulling
now “ not seen ” will be seen by him.
• • • •
Racing flourished once in Broken Hill, thanks
to the good start given it by the brothers Wilson, W. R.
and S. R._; but it has been sick for years. Now it has
livened again, and a couple of meetings held in July were
the most successful for ten years or so. There are two
clubs, which work together, and each holds a meeting, the
one a week after the other; but most likely for the
future the two will be crowded into one week. The
Broken Hill boxing boom is, by the way, dead ; the pace
was made too warm, and too many flew to live on the
game.

In a 13.2 race at a Sydney pony-course, recently,
Wray, the winner, carried 8.8, and The Weed 8.13.
The next time these two ponies came together
Wray carried 9st. and The Weed Bst., the two dif-
ferences—totalling 191 b.—being a stupendous drop in
favor of The Weed, who, of course, won. Writer, with a
host of other hungry punters, despite funny rumors,
rushed Ikey, heedless whether the thing was “ dead or
alive."

* * * *
“Old Timer”: Dooley-Doherty farce for Aus-
tralian heavy weight pug. premiership ended Saturday
night, and won’t be forgotten soon. Writer didn’t like it
from the beginning. The thing was conducted too loosely
to take. No articles were signed, no money up—nothing
that usually gives a big match proper endorsement. It
smacked of “gate ” all through. Date originall v fixed (Mon-
day last week) saw only a £4ohouse, which didri’tsuit. Post-
ponement till Friday was extended a day, and on Satur-
day night about 600 people and a big array of empty
benches greeted the champ. (Doherty) and his challenger.
Both kicked at the “ gate ” again, but the generous offer
of Manager Matthews to forego his share (25 per cent.)—
though already £3O out of pocket—ultimately brought
matters to a head, and the thing came on. Dooley was
nervously anxious from the jump and seemed afraid to
do anything. Both stood up as if engaged in a spar pure
and simple. Doherty took a lot of liberties and left him-
self open time and again, hut the erstwhile clever Dooley
seemed blind and oblivious to every one of these chances.
Writer sized-up the situation, and from indications in the
third round remarked to a friend that there wouldn’t be
a fourth—and there wasn’t. By-and-bye Dooley came
over to a baulk, and, as he did, Doherty crossed with the
right, and while Dooley wobbled, gave him another—both
high up and not over-heavy. The old’un hesitated
momentarily—as if looking for a soft patch in the boards
—then fell and remained listless till the referee counted
* 1 9—l0 —out I**
* • • •
Atkinson seemed to try hard to lose his
fight with Jack M’Gowan at Melb. Vic. Hall on Saturday
by a foul. Though a stone the heavier, he was completely
outclassed by the little actor pug, who held his own in
14 rounds of rough and tumble and finally left Atkinson’s
seconds to sky the towel. For a man of his weight 9.4
M’Gowan is a very powerful hitter and in the third
round fairly lifted his opponent off his feet with a knock-
down swing on the jaw. The incessant clinching, how-
ever, spoiled the fight, as it does so many, and in this
respect Mick Dunn is a model of pugilistic virtue. As
soon as he closes with a man, his arms are out and any
hostile movement of his adversary would mean a manifest
foul. Why can’t the rest do likewise ? Nine-tenths
of the Australian fights would be impossible under the
English fashion of the standing outside the ring.
“ X.”: Despite all the talk Tim Hegarty
wasn’t “ on ” for a match with Australian light-weight
pug champion Peter Murphy, when they met at Melb.
Sportsman office last week. Hegarty wanted Murphy to
come to 9.7 (which was impossible), for £lOO a-side, or
would take 100 to 50 and give Peter a margin of 7 lbs,
more. As Murphy didn’t fancv laying 2 to lon himself
there was no deal, but Jack M’Gowan filled the breach
and arranged preliminaries with Murphy fora “go” at
the light weight limit (10st.). Jack is good and game,
but he has a big contract on, for Peter’s a lot taller,
longer in the reach, quicker, and more skilful. Still, the
S. Melbourne miniature giant may wear his man .down or
get the opening for that knock-out punch he keeps handy
right through a fight. If M’Gowan comes out atop a
match ’tween him and Oribb would be a “ cert,” and what
a house ’twould draw!

“Old Timer”: Monday’s cables told that red-
headed Maoriland blacksmith, Bob Fitzsimmons knocked
“ Akron Giant ” Gus Ruhlin,(6ft. 3in. high and over 16st.)
out in 6 rounds at New York previous Friday. In view
of Ruhlin’s decisive smashing up of Sailor Sharkey
recently this must have staggered Yankeedom, especially
as the giant was heralded a sure thing against world’s
champion Jeffries. Fitz. and Ruhlin had a match
arranged before, but a severe bite from his pet lion com-
pelled Fitz. to forfeit close on 3000 dols. Sharkey filled
the breach. Yankee writers wrote columns on the
Australian’s “luck in dodging the big fellow.” ’Twas
certainly luck, but not in the sense they meant. Whipping
Sharkey brought Ruhlin to the same plane as Jeffries and
consequently made him a much bigger “ gate ” attraction
than he would have been otherwise, and perhaps an
“ odds-on ” favorite. Same day as this engagement was
fixed, Fitz. signed to meet Sharkey Aug. 25. Though the
verdict went against him, as the result of a palpable
swindle, Bob whipped Sharkey convincingly at ’Frisco a
while back, and there’s no reason to imagine that he
cannot repeat the dose this time—particularly when it’s
remembered that Ruhlin never gave Sharkey a hope from
the word go in their battle last June. Jeffries will now
have to meet the man from these parts again and writer’s
tip is that latter will surely win.
* » * *
Latest U.S. accounts indicate that at long last
the erstwhile Sydney Rocks phenomenon “Griffo” has
reached the end of his tether. On July 10 he met Negro
Gans (a third-rater) at Baltimore. He punched freely and
seemed his old self awhile, but broken-up nature wouldn’t
stand the racket despite the fact (so ’tis said) of his
having trained strictly. The black bided his time and
when Griffo began to fail knocked him out—in the Bth
round.
* • * *
“Old-Timer”: Death, at Wagga recently, of
Mr. John Dougharty, formerly of the big Vic. firm of
Messrs. J. G. Dougharty, Son and Parker, recalls the hal-
cyon days of Australian boxing, when Melbourne A.O. and
Sydney Amateur Gymnastic Club boomed. “ Jack ”
Dougharty was the life, soul and substance of the Vic.
body, and assisted it to the extent of hundreds. He stood
to most of the big pug. guns of his time, and dozens of
others who were not in the bruising game but lived by it
in different ways. When Melbourne A.C. offered £3OOO
for Jackson and Slavin ’twas understood that “ Jack ”
Dougharty had promised the best part (if not all) of it.
He was to have backed Slavin against J. L. Sullivan, but
something went wrong when thfe proper moment arrived,
and the match never materialised. At the time
grizzly Joe Goddard signed to fight Peter Jackson
in Sydney Amateur Gymnastic Club for a purse
of £ll5O, and Goddard backed down, latter stated
that Mr. Dougharty gave him £350 (value of loser’s end of
aforementioned sum) not to come over; this owing to
jealousy between the two clubs. Dougharty afterwards
contradicted the statement, and writer believed him,
because wild horses wouldn’t have dragged Joe into the
ring with Peter then. It was “Jack” Dougharty who,
as timekeeper, saved Goddard from being counted out in
the fourth round of his fight in Melbourne with Mick
Dooley. Joe was on the floor nearly 20sec., and the man
with the watch, instead of telling off the allotted lOsec.,
rushed over to Dooley’s corner ostensibly intent upon
cautioning him for an imaginary infraction of the rules (a
matter that didn’t come within the timekeeper’s scope),
while intense excitement prevailed. Meanwhile Goddard
pulled himself together and got up and won.
* * * *
Australian billiard-premier Fred Weiss has
challenged Roberts for £lOOO a side if latter will concede
7000 in 21,000 (bonzoline balls) or 6100 (ivories). Roberts
isn’t “ taking any ” till he cables “ down under.” Weiss
probably sizes up his chance by the fact that a few years
back, when he (Weiss) was at ’Ome, Roberts gave' him
6500 in 21,000 and match ended in a draw that favored
neither. Roberts (during present visit) has already met
Weiss twice in games of 14,001 and 7000 up and given him
4000 and 2000 or a total of 6000 in 21,000, which is 1000
less than Weiss wants now.
• • « ■
Cabled that Shoalhaven (N.S.W.) Jim Wray
and Hunter River (N.S.W.) Geo. Towns have at last
signed to scull for the English premiership on the
Thames, September 10. As much ink, gas, and news
space has been wasted over this affair as over any six
matches ever previously made. Wrangle as to which
course (Thames or Tyne) lasted years (three nearly); then
came the barney whether boats should be moored stem
or stern. Up to date of last mail leaving each refused to
budge—though at most the advantage wouldn’t have
meant 6 ft. One affects the stump and t’other the old
style of shell. Towns looks to have the better chance,
but there’s not much to choose between them on Aus-
tralian form. In ’95, at Clyde (N.S.W.) regatta, Wray
was only just beaten by Neilson in a severe tussle over
two miles. A year later Towns—-at Raymond Terrace
(N.S.W.) —beat Neilsen after they’d had it all up for two
miles, at which point Neilsen led. Towns and Wray (com-
paratively new at he game then) met on the Parramatta
eightyears ago. Wray conceded 6 sec. over the champion
course, and Towns won so easily as to make it appear
that he might have done so had both started together.

Big crowds and splendid weather for Saturday’s
Sydney football. Most notable occurrence in first grade
competition was South’s defeat of North at Sydney Cricket
Ground by 11 to 9 ; majority was obtained in last 2min.
of an exciting finish per medium of a “ try.” Worth men-
tioning that Evers (for South), within lOmin. of time,
dropped a goal from right in front of posts, and imme-
diately afterwards dropped another from the extreme
wing—outside 25. Though Glebe scored 14 points to
Eastern Suburbs’ 8, the defeated side played the sounder
game from end to end, and only succumbed through a
coupleof mistakes by one of their backs, which errors Glebe
were quick to make the most of. Newtown and Western
Suburbs put up a ragged, unattractive contest, which,
beyond being even, had no merit. Westerns won bv sto
nil. 4000 people saw ’Varsity account for Balmain by 17
to 10, thanks to former’s brilliant back play.

Port Melbourne F.C., on the initiative of its
secretary, Julius Sinn, has “ dispensed with the services ”
of two of its leading players, suspected of not doing their
utmost in the recent match against W’mstown. Sinn
further proposes to press the charges before the Associa-
tion. The men claim that they have been more sinned
against than sinning.
* • «
“Jim Crow”: In the recent inter-State la-
crosse match 18-st. goal-keeper Ayres of S.A. was a
tower of strength to his side. As he stood between
the posts the goal appeared to his opponents almost as in-
accessible as the blind pocket in billiards, the ball
often cannoning off his adamantine rotundity as if he
were pneumatically inflated, and thus completing the rout
of the Vic. forwards. Victoria should take the hint, and
get a goal-keeper of heroic girth, like—say, George
Robertson, of Carlton.
