In the Grand Prix du Centenaire (Paris),
pigeon -contest recently 14 survived (he 9th round, and 3
the 15th—a Spanish marquis, Donald Mackintosh, and
Murphy (N.Y.). All killed up to and including 20th bird.
Yankee missed his 21st. Now- ’twas a match ’tween
Don and Donald. Nines were laid on the gun-every time
Mackintosh shot. Soon a beauty beat the marquis badly,
and left Mac. to kill, which he did with first barrel.
Winner’s prize was 5000 fr. The Australian had a high
time in the series of matches ; he won £5OO (prize-money
alone) in two weeks, and yvhacked the Grand Prix du
L'Exposition (30,000 fr.) with a Yankee, a Belgian, and a
Frenchman.
* * * •
The cabled knocking-out of huge Yankee Gus.
Ruhlin by comparatively little Bob Fitzsimmons—who
might not have scaled 12st. —recalls the deeds of Tom
Sayers, and sets one thinking whether men of his day
were really not as good as those of present period, as is
often asserted. Writer is sweet on old-timers, and could
adduce cogent argument in favor of their end, as the
result of extensive reading, but the thing is too big for
Bulletin space. In view of happenings abroad, present
position of Peter Jackson (a younger man than Fitz-
simmons) is sad. Nobody who knows much of the two
will derfy that the famous black, anything like well, would
account for Bob. When Jackson was in his heyday, Fitz.,
though challenging the world, always barred Peter.
Once, in the States, when chatting about their successes
over a social bottle, the Maorilander remarked that there
wasn’t another man in America who used his right hand
as well as did the white and black Australians—which is
probably the secret of those long lists of triumphs credited
to both, and accounts for Fitz. settling Ruhlin so sum-
marily.
* * * *
Cyclists should note that the Dunlop Tyre
guarantee of twelve months on roadster Dunlop tyres
applies only to Australian-made tyres. Cyclists can
easily distinguish the tyre, as it is embossed on the out-
side “ Australian Hand-made.” Publicity is given to the
matter owing to several cyclists having purchased cycles
fitted with foreign-made Dunlops, and then finding, to
their chagrin, that the tyres were not covered by the
Australasian Dunlop Co.’s liberal guarantee.*

Champion Roberts bit off more than he could
chew at Sydney Tatts. on Saturday night. He gave
“ Billy ” Lyons (runner-up to “ Paddy ” Bourke in recent
Tatts. tourney), 350 in 500. Game went to Lyons by 58.
Subsequently, same start was again conceded another
Tatts. crack (Ross), and latter won with 119 in hand.
Lyons tackled the big fellow again on same conditions,
but went under, 37 to the bad. Roberts ran up a 284
break this time. Lyons’ best break was 42, and Ross’ 51
(unfinished). Against Ross, Roberts’ highest compilations
were 162 and 95.

Harry Stockdale writes : —“ The Federated
Australian Grand National Steeplechase Handicap of
1500 sovs ; Ist horse, 1000 sovs. ; 2nd, 300 ; 3rd, 125 ; 4th,
75. Distance, 3 times round the (new) course—about 4
miles. A Gold uup of the value of 100 sovs. to be pre-
sented to the winner by .” Such a race would be
worthy of the mother-province, and of the splendid new
Randwick course, and would “fetch” both the public
and horse-owners from all parts. Let N.S. Wales rise to
the occasion and take her proper position in the racing
world. Hitherto her biggest steeple-prizes have not
even reached the value of second-class events.
* * * *
Electorate football, adopted in Adelaide this
season, has increased gate-receipts 50 per cent., besides
producing 1 six strong teams instead of three. The minor
premiership was won by N. Adelaide, which was bottom
last season, and Port Adelaide, one of the old-time strong
Clubs, was absolutely last. Apparently, the district
system, in every sport, has always proved the best. In
Adelaide electorate cricket has produced three new
grounds in three years.

May not be generally known that champion
heavyweight thumper Bill Doherty comes from a fighting
family. He has five brothers, all in the game. Bill is the
eldest (32); then come Austin (30), “Joe” (28), “Ned”
(26), “Charlie” (24), and “Josh” (18). Latter promises
to be bigger and better than any of the others when he
matures. The family lived at Footscray (Vic.), and Bill
for long was one of the “ Footscray eight ’’—t hose worthy
successors of the whilom doughty “ Footscray mud-pun-
ters” who used to take their seats in a calm matter-of-
fact sort of way, lay their pipes alongside of them, and,
without any preparation at all, settle the best that could
be brought in opposition.

When it is time to stop you are just warmed up
for a good day’s work. But it is too late, and the
next morning it is the same thing over again.
That is not laziness, it is sickness —real, serious
sickness—and later will develop into something

Messrs. David Blyth and Co., of Sydney
and Melbourne, agents for the Vinolia Co., Ltd.,
inform us that their principals have given £10,000
to the Transvaal war fund for widows and
orphans. This sum represents a half-penny on
every tablet of Vinolia soap sold during the first
six months of the war.
“ Malle ” on a phase of Chow commerce :
Went from Swatow, China, to Singapore, once, with
800 coolies. The method of the trade is : Ship is char-
tered by Chinese, and, beyond navigating, the crew have
nothing to do with her; she is filled with coolies, who
have been induced by promises of much gold, and, on
arrival at destination, they are sold to the highest bidders
for two years, which they have to serve for food only, to
repay the philanthropists who provided their passage. Of
course, there is no slavery in the British Empire, and this
is only a fair deal for the risk the charterers run, as it
costs them fully ftdol. per head to land coolies, and they
seldom realise more than 150dol. per nob in the market.
On one occasion, owing to a typhoon and battened
hatches, out of a cargo of 800, only 270 pigtails survived the
seven-days’ voyage. The coolies were counted-in like bags
of rice by an oleaginous Chow at the hatchway, who banged
every tenth on the back and yelled “ Tal-lee ! ” When we
reached Singapore we were at once surrounded by sam-
pans, and a couple of clipping skiffs, manned by gigantic
Arabs, lay by to prevent escapes. One “ passenger ”
almost reached shore ere the watchers caught him, and
when brought back he was tortured in certain subtle un-
mentionable ways, but not seriously injured—he was
property. The unfortunate sampan John, his accomplice,
fared differently. The gratings, thwarts and oars were
thrown out of his boat, and its planks started from the
ribs. Then he was secured by the pigtail to a
stanchion, stripped and welted enthusiastically with
a hunk of firewood. The charterers, who had been
so soft and oily, became now perfect fiends, and
they were about to tear off the poor wretch’s nails
when a couple of us interfered with revolvers. They
maintained that as he’d tried to “stealee their man,”
they could deal with him as they choose, but a Smith-
and-Wesson is not a good thing' to argue with, so they
shoved him into his wrecked boat and let him rip. Since
then I have known that every Chinkey’s childlike gentle-
ness covers a devil incarnate.

OF THE
Demon Greek Gold Dredging Company,

(To be registered under Part 11. of the Companies Act, 1899.
CAPITAL—£24,OOO in 48,000 Shares of 10s. each,
of which
16,000 fully paid Shares will be allotted to the Vendors in full payment for the property.
16,000 Contributing- Shares are now offered for subscription, payable 2s. on application, 2s. on allotment,
and the balance in calls not exceeding Is. per Share, at intervals of not less than one month. The
whole of the subscribed capital will be - paid to the credit of the Company, less only brokerage and
flotation expenses.
16,000 Contributing Shares are reserved for future issue at the discretion of the Directors ; but in the first
instance are to be offered, pro rata and at par, for subscription to existing Shareholders.
Total 48,000 Shares.
Allotment will take place, and the Company will be registered, when 12,000 Contributing Shares are applied for.
The Vendors, being so satisfied with the prospects of this property, TAKE THE WHOLE OP THEIR INTEREST
IN SHARES AND RECEIVE NO CASH.

GBMERA.L DESCRIPTIOKT.
This Company is being formed to work by means of modern Gold Dredges 80 acres of the banks and bed of
Demon Creek, and two adjoining fiats of 12 acres each; which have been taken up by the Vendors. The Minister
for Mines has approved of the issue of Lease for the 80 acres. Leases of the two 12 acre blocks have been
applied for.
Demon Creek is situated in the Parishes of Maclean and Wunglebong, County of Clive, and is distant from
Tenterfield 21 miles. It flows into the Titnbarra River, a tributary of the Clarence River. It has an extensive
watershed ; and Messrs. Richardson and Baker report that there will always he an unfailing supply of water for
dredging purposes.
It is estimated by the three gentlemen who have reported upon this property (and whose reports are hereto
attached) that the wash is from 12 to 25 feet deep, being on a soft granite bottom. The flats secured are about 12
feet above the bed of the River, and the Experts believe they contain the old River bed. The bed of the Creek is
practically virgin ground, alluvial miners being unable to reach the wash owing to the strong' flow of water. That
portion of the Creek which has been worked has yielded excellent returns. The Vendors of this property obtained
when prospecting it three pennyweights of gold from 120 dishes taken from different parts. This was at the rate of
8) dwts. to the load or ton. The gold is of a coarse and shotty nature, and of excellent quality, and samples of it
may be inspected at the office of the Company’s Brokers. Mr. S. R. Baker, when prospecting for the purpose of
making his report, got an average of 1 dwt. to the load. The average obtained by Mr. C. E. Richardson, when
prospecting for his report, was 9) grs. per load.
It is intended immediately after the formation of the Company to make arrangements for the construction of a
Dredge to cost about £6OOO, capable of treating, in two shifts of 10 hours each, 12,000 loads per week.
The lowest return obtained by any of the Experts was 9.V grs. per load or ton, and was obtained from a depth
not exceeding 4J feet. The following will, on the basis of half that amount, viz., 4f grs. per ton, show the highly
remunerative character of the property. Four and three-quarter (4f) grains of gold are worth 9d. Thus each load
treated yields a return of 9d.—12,000 loads per week will, on this basis, return £450 per week. Deduct £lOO-for
Dredge and other expenses, the nett return will be £350 per week. Allowing for the unforseen expenses the time
the Dredge may have to be laid up for overhaul and repairs, expenses of management, &c., large and regular divi-
dends may be confidently anticipated on the proposed capital; while deeper workings may reasonably be expected
to yield higher returns.
Mr. Richardson, Mining and Dredging Engineer, has already chosen a site for the erection of the pontoon.
There is an abundance of suitable timber in the neighborhood for the construction of a pontoon, and for all other
purposes. v
It may be mentioned that the three Experts, whose Reports are hereto attached, hold no interest whatever in
this property, and, as far as the Vendors know, are not known to each other.
Agreements.

benefit of the said Section.
REPORTS.
Report by Mr. C. E. Richardson,
Consulting Dre dging Engineer ; late Manager Mataktaki and Manorburn Gold Dredging Companies, New Zealand
11 Vickery’s Chambers, 82 Pitt-street, Sydney,
November 30th, 1899.
The Secretary, Demon Creek Gold Dredging Syndicate, Sydney.
Dear Sir, —I have the honor to report having inspected your property on Demon Creek on November 23rd, 24thi
and 25th.
The claim is situated in the Parishes of Maclean and Wunglebong, about 20 miles from.Tenterfield, and comprises
the banks and bed of Demon Creek for about 2J miles, starting from a point about one mile above its junction with
the Timbarra or Rocky River.
Demon Creek and its feeders traverse the rich alluvial diggings of Timbarra and Poverty Point, Timbarra forming
the northern watershed, and the almost precipitous range on which is situated the Poverty Point diggings forming
the eastern watershed for about 16 miles.
The numerous feeders to Demon Creek and the bed of the Qreek, above your top pegs, have been worked exten-
sively, and must have been very rich in gold.
During tny inspection I saw a great amount of old workings immediately above your claim, and also on Mundy’s
Lookout Creek, at its junction with Demon Creek, and also immediately below your bottom pegs on the rocky bar
where the ground is shallow. Within your pegs l could see no signs of old workings, and it is said by the oldest in-
habitants of the neighborhood and also by the blacks that the ground proved too wet to work.
Your Claim may safely be looked 'upon as virgin ground. . ...
The Claim throughout its length is free from bars, and is in every way suitable dredging country.
The wash is extremely free and will wash well in a dredge screen, there being no clay.
Owing to the fact of your claim being virgin ground I was not able to get any evidence as to depth of wash. ,
After carefully observing the nature of the surrounding country, I estimate the depth will run from about 15 to
25 feet below water level, with a soft granite bottom.
The Demon Creek, though subject to occasional freshes, does not flood badly ._ In fact, a rise of 4 feet is of rare
occurrence. There will therefore be very little risk to a dredge working your claim. .
Although there is standing timber along the banks of the Creek in places, there are very few snags visible ; do
difficulties need be anticipated on that account.
There is a suitable site for building about i mile above the lower pegs of your claim. . , ,
There is abundance of water all the year round. At the time of my inspection there were fully 40 sluice neaas
running in the Creek. ......
At the present time the original gravel in the bed of the Creek is covered to a depth of from 2 to 6 feet of hgnt
drift gravel and sand, evidently due to the denudation of the softer granite formation of the higher ground, wmcn
has been extensively sluiced into the Creeks from the Timbarra and Poverty Point diggings during the past 30 .years.
It is said that the old blacks remember Demon Creek when it was a succession of gravelly beaches and waternoies,
with practically no drift sand. . , . , . . ...
The presence of this sand will not materially affect the dredging of your claim, but I found it an obstacle in tne
way of prospecting in the bed of the Creek, except where the rough gravel deposit could be reached near the surface.
A great number of dish prospects were washed throughout the length of your claim and in nearly every instance
gold was found. Prospects taken from the travelling drift sand also showed fine colors. • .
At a point about one mile from the lower pegs I was able to get down to the wash and to a depth of 4it. oin.
below the water. . , ... , _ , .
From two holes about 30ft. apart 1 and 1-12 cubic yards of wash-dirt and drift-gravel was put through a banjo.
After each lot of j-yard had been put through, the tailings were prospected. The loss in treatment proved to e
practically nil.
Total .. 11-12 cubic yards ..
10J grains.
The average of the four prospects taken is therefore 9'69 grains per cubic yard, which I consider extremely satis-
f&ctory.
As, with few exceptions, alluvial ground proves to be richer nearer the bottom than on the surface, there is every
reason to expect that m uch higher prospects could have been obtained had 1 been able to get down.
Owing to the favorable class of country, most of the claim could be worked without an elevator, but I would re-
commend that in putting on a machine, provision should be made for fitting an elevator when required.
I estimate that a suitable dredge with a nominal capacity of 100 cubic yards per hour could be put on your claim
at a total cost of under £6,000. -
Suitable timber for construction of pontoons can be had close at hand at, I understand, 10s. per 100 feet.
The cartage from Tenterfield to the claim, a d.stance of 38 miles by a fair country road, can, I understand, be
done for £2 per ton. ...... 4 _ _ ,
In conclusion, I have much pleasure in being able to express the opinion that your area on Demon Creek will
prove a highly payable dredging proposition. a E RICHARDSON.
Report by Mr. Harold D. A. Preston, New Zealand Dredging Miner.
Demon Creek, October 17th, 1899.
Tiib Secretary, Demon Creek Gold Dredging Syndicate, Sydney.
Dear Sir,— I have been employed in prospecting Demon Creek for dredging purposes for the last four weeks, and
have been requested by Mr. Wall to report to you. Although 1 have no desire to pose as a dreging expert, I may
claim to know some little about it, as I have spent the last eight years in Otago, N.Z., right in the midst of the dredg-
ing operations on the Molyneux River, Tuapeka, Waitahuna, and districts surrounding. In these places dredging, as
is well known, has been a most decided success, and reduced to a science. The Molyneux, of course, affords unusual
facilities for dredging, but at the same time presents such obstacles as high flats and rocky bottoms, which are not to
be met with on your property at Demon Creek, and which detract so much from successful dredging. On the other
hand, such districts as Tuapeka Flats, Waitahuna, and Glenore do not present the same difficulties in so marked a
degree ; in fact, they resemble in many details the Demon Creek property, and I am acquainted with several dredging
companies and parties in the abovementioned districts, which are obtaining excellent results, where the prospects per
dish and general facilities are far inferior to those of Demon Creek. In prospecting Demon Creek I have the honor to
report as follows_
I found the gold very regularly distributed throughout the property, and wherever any depth at all could be
reached, prospects were exceedingly good, improving with depth. .* •
At the bend of the Creek at No. 3 Flat I would estimate the dish prospects at not less than 5 dwts. per cubic yard,
and in the prospecting trenches of No. 1 flat (about 35 chains further down the Creek) the prospects are similar.
On the edge of No. 2 Flat we put down a paddock, and obtained equally good prospects, no dish being washed
which failed to show'in payable quantities. At the lower pegs near the Bar 1 washed prospects quite equal to J oz.
per ton, and should say that there is an immense quantity of gold all along the bottom of the main channel, which
has in no instance hitherto been touched. In most places I should estimate the wash to be fully 30 feet thick,
judging from the surrounding country. For J of a mile above No. 3 flat, I prospected the tributary gullies on the
right bank, in every instance obtaining satisfactory results, there still remaining the upper portion of the Creek and
tributaries to be examined. The presence of gold in payable quantities even in the upper wash, however, proves the
main creek to have both rich and constant feeders from the auriferous ground above.
The alluvial flats 1,2, 3 and others have formed the old bed of the Creek, and vary in height from 6to 18 feet
above water level; they are covered with a stripping of soft loam, and underneath a gravelly wash carrying gold in
payable quantities is met with.
The bed of the main Creek consists of fine gravel, and presents no obstacles to successful dredging with ordinary
bucket dredge, there being no boulders or sunken timber.
The gold is of a fine but shott.v nature, and appears to be of excellent quality.
Mr. Wall and I thought out the prospect of bottoming the deeper channels, &c., and, with him, I fear that-it is
an impossibility by any ordinary methods, owing to the constant flow.of water, which is a difficulty not to be over-
come under the circumstances, though its presence is, of course, an additional desideratum for dredging purposes, an
advantage it is scarcely necessary to refer to.
There is an abundant supply of water throughout all seasons of the year, and an unlimited supply of timber, suit-
able both for dredge building and working fuel.
In conclusion, I may state I feel confident that you have acquired a most suitable property, and one that affords
every facility for successful dredging operations.
I am, Sir, yours faithfully, HAROLD D. A. PRESTON.
Copies of the Prospectus, containing full reports and plan, can be obtained from the Brokers, RANDLE and
WILLIS, Martin Place, Sydney.
N.Z. Loan and Mercantile Agency Co. reports
that the wheat market drifts along to a very slow tune.
No change in prices has taken place and although fair clear-
ances have been effected to millers, those gentlemen are
now beginning to look sideways at the current prices.
London cables that no material' improvement' is likely in
the near future, and shippers decline to invest at figures
which would probably leave them with a loss. Present
quotations are : Prime milling, 2s. 9d. to 2s. lOd. ; good,
2s. Bd. to 2s. BJ,d. ; pinched, 2s. 6d. to 2s. 7d. ; chickwheat
(little offering), 2s. 3d. to 2s. sd. per bushel.

At Poverty Point.
Signor Steffani, the musical tutor of Florence Schmidt
interviewed on hisretum toSydney.said he “did not know,
whether Miss Schmidt was likely to persevere in her
musical ambition. For reasons which, have more relation
to morals than to music Miss Schmidt, as a high-prin-
cipled Australian girl, may find the surroundings of the
Continental operatic stage more repellant than attractive.
It is a test demanding great virtue as the support of
honest merit and high ideals. To phrase the matter less
bluntly would be affectation. The concert platform, par-
ticularly in England, is clean and wholesome. That is
why I think that Miss Schmidt will make it her choice.
Do not for a moment imagine that I am making a sweep-
ing charge against the operatic stage. Nothing of the
kind is in my thoughts. What I said just now with
respect to the dangers which, as a rule, beset the path of
a young woman, who, without rich or powerful friends,
seeks to succeed in the field of opera on the Continent,
was a passing reference to a condition of things the exist-
ence of which is unfortunately too well known, both inside
and outside the profession.” To put the matter
“ straight: ” a clever Australian girl, who went to Europe
to study, and has succeeded well, wrote to Tiie Bulletin long
ago that even if a woman was beautiful and sang like a
bird it was next to impossible for her to get a really good
show on the lyric stage unless she consented to become
the mistress of an entrepreneur.
The piquant Emily Soldene only returned from Sydney
to London in time to attend the death-bed of her .mother,
aged 89.
“ S.S.” : Surely the most extravagantly-eomie situation
ever devised by a playwright with serious intentions is
the last scene in “ The Orphan Heiress,” now running at
Melb. Royal. The heroine is to be destroyed. The
villain pays the owner of a vagrant menagerie to bear her
off, and hand her over to the mercies ora famished tiger.
The dreadful deed is perpetrated in a most casual manner
in the presence of disinterested spectators, but ere the
lady goes to the tiger Heaven opens behind the circus
cages, and gives the audience a glimpse of a feathered
angel dangling on a wire. This spectacle so encourages
the heroine that she goes gladly to the mock tiger (full
brother to the panto, donkey) but is rescued, just as the
stuffed monster is about to devour her, by the brown bear
of the next cage, said bear being the red-headed comic
man in disguise. How the comic man disposed of the real
bear and appropriated its hide is left to the imagination of
the audience. When a very small boy I saw sundry
Richardson-Shows at the old Eastern Fairs in Bendigo.
They were monuments of art and jewels of coherency
compared with this appalling fakement.
“Skull” : Are the legs of Man degenerating? A re-
view of the shanks of the male members of the Nance
O’Neil co. would lead impartial students to lose respect
for the human understanding. There is not a decent leg
in the regiment. Kingston’s have"a tendency to wobble,
and in certain attitudes they bend backwards in a de-
cided curve right up the shin-bone to the body, a charac-
teristic I once thought peculiar to John Leech’s footmen.
Plimmer’s legs markedly jerk outwards at the knee as he
walks, and stage-manager Barnard’s come together there
with a suddenness that suggests a danger of interlocking.
The smaller members of the co. display an amazing packet
of mixed pins, aud suggest the idea that the party respon-
sible for the selecting of the co. has a peculiar weakness
for bizarre legs.
Two scenic artists are hard at work for George
Musgrove. Phil Goatcher began operations a month ago
in Rickards’ dismantled Opera House, where there is
plenty of space but not much comfort now that the re-
build'ers are about. Coleman, the artist engaged by
Musgrove in London, arrived lately, and was granted
the use of half the painting-room at Melbourne Princess’s
until the retirement of Brough and Co., when the theatre
will come under Musgroveian management, brother Harry
taking charge of the commercial dept Seems that the
Princess’s is to have a month’s re-furnishing and “ doing
up ” before the new season starts.
Touching a par in last week’s “ Poverty Point,” The
Bulletin may as well mention that although the first
production of George Musgrove’s season at Melbourne
Princess’s is still a matter of mystery, more or less, it will
certainly not be “ The Scarlet Feather.”
At Melb. Philharmonic’s recent “ Damnation of Faust ”
an effect was produced which quite interfered with the
music. Just as Marguerite was _ overwhelmed with the
intense pathos of the “ King of Thule ” ballad, a number
of stout male choristers —the basses who sang the Devil’s
music—entered the choir smacking their lips and looking
happier. They had been out for a drink. Some were
still chewing coffee-beans, and one elderly chanter wiped
his lips quite frankly.
Little Fanny Powers appears to have lost some of her
vivacity and sweet young voice through stopping up too
late o’ nights. The only way of keeping a stage kiddie
fresh and natural, is to put her (or him) early to bed.
When are we going to have the advertising drama ?
The particular brand of champagne used in “Camille”
is puffed in the programmes, and pantomime comedians
have often made large sums by advertising gags. But
there is a fortune for the scene of, say, two women dis-
cussing their favorite shops—with real local names instead
of fictions.
Laura Roberts and her husband, young Charley Tay-
lor, have been with a prosperous little musical-comedy co.
through India, and hope to make more money on their
roundabout way back to Australia. Laura Roberts, it
seems, is “ the popular Australian Prima-Donna ” when
she gets to the East,
Dear Bulletin. lie the allegation by “ Edson ” that
Nance O’Neil has not been specially boomed in Melbourne
I will quote three instances of abnormal booming.
Previous to Nance’s arrival, each of the three Melbourne,
dailies published a long interview with J. C. Williamson,
and in each interview J.C.W. mentioned that his
immediate reason for taking Her Majesty’s Theatre, was
to introduce Nance to Melbourne.—Fax.


“ Bush Pedagogue ” writes :
The unwisdom and want of common sense characterise
tic of the parson in the mass is evidently responsible for
the brilliant plan evolved by the president (Archdeacon
Langley) of the Religious Education Commission. He
proposes the simultaneous instilling of religious truth
into the godly of the scholars, and military drill (as a
punishment, seemingly) for the infidel remainder, by pne
teacher (vide Age, 30/6/’00). He considers it quite prac-
ticable that while those willing are being religiously
instructed, those unwilling can be receiving military
drill. This, in one-roomed and one-teachered bush
schools, involves a queer combine. Just consider this
picture : Interior of bush school with nine children seated
in desks ; Outside, five ditto struggling with the intri-
cacies of “Right form.” Teacher standing in doorway
with service book in right hand. Teacher reads : “ Dearly
beloved brethren, the Scripture moveth us in sundry
places that we should confess ” “Squad—’shun! By
the right—Quick—march” “and acknowledge our

The total cost of Federation to N.S.W. —
fees to delegates, travelling expenses, referen-
dums, printing, &c.—from 1891 to Aug., 1899 was
£73,481.
Millionaire Tyson’s money has begun to
travel. Mrs. Dymock, a next of kin, has given
£lB,OOO for Rockview station, near Junee. She
has also purchased Paling’s warehouse, Sydney.
From Buenos Ayres comes the account of
a new meat-preserving process, said to supersede
both the freezing and “ preparation” methods. It
is the invention of a German, and a trial shipment
is about to be made to England, in which country
the patent rights have been bought up.
London Sphere obituarises Stephen Crane
and refers to him as being a great admirer of
Mark Twain’s works, “especially Bob Sawyer.”
Which is reminiscent of a Melb. professor who
publicly advised school-boys to read “Tom Jones”
and had to hurriedly rush letters into the papers
to explain that he meant “ Tom Brown.”
“S.” : I know of a case in which a man
who was travelling the bush and found the body
of a murdered man was detained by the local
police during the seven months which elapsed
before the murderer was traced and captured.
Yet, for the loss of all this time, as well as of a
billet he had been going to,‘he never received any
compensation. [Quite illegal, of course, but the
police may have “ bluffed ” an ignorant man.]
The time-limit on speeches in M.L. Par-
liament produces interesting results. “If I had
my way,” shouted George Fisher the other night,
banging his fist on something hard as he worked
himself up to fury and a great peroration over the
Libel Bill—“ if 1 had my way I ” “Time ! ”
said the Speaker. And now people are wondering
whether Fisher wanted to hang all editors or to
double their salaries out of the Consolidated
Revenue and give them each a free railway-pass
for life.
The amenities of N.S.W. railway system.
Writer lately travelled from Sydney to Mudgee
on a cold July night, a perfect “perisher.” At
Penrith he asked for a foot-warmer and was told
that none were to be had. At Mt. Victoria re-
peated ; again in vain. At Wallerawang, after 10
minutes interviewing of different officials he
obtained a promise from the station-master that
he should have one, and just before the train left
a porter thrust one into the carriage. It was stone-
cold. Two a.m., and the worst part of the journey
just commencing.
The Adelaide Labor paper—the Herald—
although it bitterly denounces sweating in fac-
tories, is trying to fill its columns by means of a
literary competition, under which weekly articles
500 words long are to be contributed regularly for
six months on the off-chance of winning a bicycle
(valued by the giver at £2O) at the end of the
I term. The best man will therefore be paid at the
rate of 15s. weekly, and the other poor devils will
I £ et nothing, although all will have to help in
setting the Labor policy before the country.
Far-Out (N.S.W.) selectors are trying to
arrange a plan to put before the Pastoral Commis-
sion. Many of them have agreed to urge that,
instead of the present maximum area of 10,240
acres open to a homestead lessee, the selector
should be entitled to take enough country to run
a given number of sheep—some suggest 6000, some
3000. And almost as a necessary consequence of
that they have agreed to urge further that one
selection should follow on after another—that is
to say, the selector should not be entitled to put
in his pegs in order to take in picked country,
leaving awkward slices of poor land between him
and his nearest neighbour.
The indecent small female—with the con-
nivance and under the protection of the Crown
has been at it again. Despite the evidence of
several witnesses to the effect that they had seen
her and accused “courting” in sundry places and
manifold ways, she still stuck to her simple little
tale that he had met her suddenly and taken
advantage of her in an open paddock in the town-
ship of Yarram (Gippsland), said paddock being
within coo-ee of the whole village. Judge
Holroyd told the jury that one side or the other
had undoubtedly committed perjury, and the
twelve promptly decided against the “ Crown.”
The perjured small girl went free, as the Crown’s
perjurer always does in Victoria. And so long as
the Crown protects its licensed perjurers it cannot
expect them to get the same credence as the
accused and his friends, who always risk a prose-
cution for false-swearing if the case goes against
them.
A story of Melbourne's great Mafeking
drunk. It was a promiscuous drunk, as every
student of ancient history knows. People drank
wildly with complete strangers, shook hands with
complete strangers, slapped them on the back, ex-
changed cards with them, and implored them to
call and dine four times a week, and be introduced
to their sister, and to regard themselves as belong-
|mg to the family. Some of them even offered to
I lend money to complete strangers. Afterwards in
I |he dim grey dawn, when Melbourne streets were
1 ttered with broken hats and umbrellas and cigar-
ash, and the city smelled like a cubic mile of
, tobacco-smoke and decayed mackintosh, the police
tore the more respectable-looking drunks off the
lamp-posts to which they were adhering, explored
their pockets for a card, gave it and the
drunk to a cabman, and bade him take
the wreck home and fight his relations
for the fare if he couldn’t get it otherwise.
Almost every wreck had a dozen cards in his
pocket—the cards of persons he had embraced
and foregathered with during the night—but the
policeman couldn’t know that. His business was to
clear the streets somehow. So it came about that
a young lady at Toorak, sitting up in the dim
morning for her lost and loyal brother, heard a
n ??> and when she opened the door saw a Form
with the rums of a bell-topper driven down over
its ears charge headlong into the hall and then
drop. She paid the fare, which was 10s. 6d and
remonstrated kindly with the ruin, and it ’said
Gr-r-r-r-rwong.” Then she forgave it, on the
ground of its loyalty, dragged it into a bedroom
and urged it to go to bed and rest. And the
iorm said Gr-r-r-r-rwing.” It was late in
the afternoon when another cab drove up, and
bometning with a black eye and a swelled head
crept out. This was the real brother He had
also been delivered at the wrong place, only he
had been less courteously received, and had done his
recovery on the doorstep The bedroom was
investigated, and the person of whom delivery had
been accepted in the early morning proved to be
1a Tk 011 ?, P ers ° n - • his fearfully muddy state
V l6 oc oul ' f )f drink that arose from him proved
that he was at least a fellow-adherent of the Good
, use, so they treated him kindly. The brother
when delivered at the wrong house, hadn’t been
treated kindly, but he had probably been left on a
German s premises, which would account for it.

The London “TIMES” Reprint of the Encyclopaedia Britannica
A. 1 ! 1 A. BA.iR.GrA.I3NT !
The popularity of the “ENCYCLOPAEDIA BRITANNIC A” is sufficiently shown by the fact
that in less than twenty months more than 20,000 copies of the “Times” Reprint of the “Encyclo-
fisedia have been sold in the United Kingdom alone, in addition to copies supplied by the “Times ” in
ndia, South Africa, and New Zealand, as well as to the United States, where this complete and
authentic Reprint of the great library is eagerly welcomed, notwithstanding that the country has,
owing to the faulty condition of the copyright laws, been flooded with thousands of copies of piratical
and mutilated editions.
The aggregate sale of the “Times” Reprint of the “Encyclopaedia” has been, at home and
abroad, over twenty-five thousand sets of twenty-five volumes each, a total of six hundred and twenty-
five thousand volumes. The figures in each case are of interest, not only because they show that the
“ Encyclopaedia ” is a general favorite, but also because they describe an unprecedented success in the
dissemination of really good literature, and go far to prove that the public taste is by no means so
debased as some alarmists believe it to be.
They are figures, too, so astonishing that they cannot readily be grasped.
Every copy of the “ Encyclopaedia ” contains twenty-two thousand pages, and this means that
over five hundred and fifty million pages have been printed and sold. Large figures of this sort pro-
duce upon the mind an effect which a contemporary foreign writer has aptly described as a “brain-
blur.” One cannot easily picture printed pages floating through the air and lying like snow upon the
ground, but a simple comparison will assist the imagination. Each of these pages is Uin. in depth by
Bfin. in width. Their aggregate superficies would therefore be, if the front and back of each leaf could
be split apart and all the pages spread out, no less than fifty-two .thousand nine hundred and thirty-
seven millions five hundred thousand square inches, or 367,621,527 square feet, or 8439 acres, or nearly
14 square miles. Conceived as one gigantic sheet of printed words, having its centre at a given point,
the whole would extend nearly two miles north, nearly two miles south, nearly two miles east, and
nearly two miles west. On this enormous sheet there would appear no less than 750,000,000 words,
for there are more than 30,000,000 words in each copy of the “Encyclopaedia.” Using another
familiar illustration, 550,000,000 pages, each llin. in depth, would, laid end to end, make a ribbon
more than 95,486 miles in length, reaching nearly four times round the earth.
Each copy of the “ ENCYCLOPAEDIA, BRITANNIC A,” packed in a box for delivery, weighs
about one hundred and ninety-six pounds. The whole would therefore weigh over two thousand tons,
as much as can be carried by a dozen ordinary goods trains.
No other book devised by human ingenuity has ever had a sale which can be compared to this, and
after the recapitulation of these figures the acceptability of the “ENCYCLOPAEDIA BRITAN-
NICA ” as a timely investment can hardly be questioned.
The One Work of its Kind in the World.
It is a unique characteristic of the “ENCYCLOPAEDIA BRITANNICA” that while it is
authoritative in the highest degree—a standard in every field —it has so little of the typical encyclo-
paedia atmosphere. That is to say, its articles are not mere compilations—they do not form a huge
structure of facts unsculptured and unadorned ; they are, the longer ones at least, rather a series of
concise and rapid monographs. They are the work of masters in every province, yet not mere masters
of uncouth details, but of form and exposition as well. Many of them, indeed, are among the finest
contributions to present-day literature.
These articles are delightful reading—they are more—they are a liberal education. This is not
saying that anyone can master so vast a story of fact—there is no one living who could do that. Nor
does the mere acquisition of facts constitute a liberal education. Something more is needed, and this
is why no dry-as-dust compilation will suffice. A fine appreciation of form, the habit of detecting
relations between widely-separated subjects, a mastery of broad outlines, the ability to seize the
central truth, come rather from a long association and familiarity with minds of the highest reach and
training.
It is precisely minds of this type to whom the present character of the “ENCYCLOPAEDIA
BRITANNICA ”is due. “ Those who have proved themselves to be at the summit in their particu-
lar departments ” were everywhere selected to expose and interpret for intelligent and educated people
their special spheres of knowledge. The result has been a degree of excellence which has never before
been attained in a work of scope so immense. Notwithstanding its high scholarly standard, the
“ Encyclopaedia ”is still literature in the strictest sense of the word. It may be taken volume by
volume, treatise by treatise, article by article, and read for the individual interest which each affords.
Because it embraces all departments of human learning, and evenly and impartially presents the
best results obtainable from each, the “ ENCYCLOPAEDIA BRITANNICA” constitutes within
itself, an ideal popular library. Certainly it is the foundation, the true beginning, of any extensive
collection of books. Not a thousand volumes, each chosen with an understanding of books which
perhaps not a dozen living men possess would it be possible to obtain anything like so admirable a
resume of all that a nineteenth century man or woman wishes to know.
An Admirable Investment.
The “ENCYCLOPEDIA BRITANNICA” is an admirable investment for three excellent
reasons. The three excellent reasons which combine to recommend the “ Encyclopaedia ” are these :
In the first place, it is the very best thing of its sort to purchase. The “ENCYCLOPEDIA
BPITANNICA ” has been recognised, ever since the first edition was published in 1771, as the very
best work of reference in the world. It is easy enough to find more costly books, to purchase a thou-
sand volumes instead of only twenty-five, but that thousand would be ill-chosen if the twenty-five
volumes of the “ ENCYCLOPEDIA BPITANNICA ” were not among them. It is not, indeed, too
much to say that no thousand other volumes contain so great a proportion of the whole sum of human
knowledge as do these five-and-twenty. Here, then, is one excellent reason why the “ Encyclopaedia”
is the very best thing of its sort.
In the second place, the “ ENCYCLOPEDIA BRITANNIC A” may, at the moment, be pur-
chased upon remarkably advantageous terms. The established publisher’s price for twenty-five volumes
in cloth binding is £37. The “ Times ” offers precisely the same work, unaltered and unabridged, for
seventeen monthly payments of one guinea each, or for £l7 in cash—less than half-price. This is an-
other excellent reason, for every one must wish that the pounds aud shillings one spends should do
their utmost in adding to the accumulated wealth of the investor.
In the third place, the purchase of the “ENCYCLOPEDIA BRITANNICA ” does not involve,
on the one hand, ready-money, nor yet, on the other hand, the incurring of a vague debt which the
debtor may be called upon to pay when it is least convenient for him to do so.
A preliminary payment of only one guinea in cash will secure the delivery of the volumes.
After the delivery has been made other payments of one guinea each become due at monthly intervals.
The purchaser of the “ Encyclopaedia ” who adopts this novel and convenient system of paying for
books knows precisely when his payments should be made, and knows, too, that each one of them is
so inconsiderable as to make hardly any strain upon a month’s income.
The Choice of a Binding'.
The Times Reprint of the “ ENCYCLOPEDIA BRITANNICA ” is offered to residents of New
South Wales in three different styles of binding. The cheapest is in a neat and useful green cloth—
the same in every respect as that which has been sold at the higher price. With care it will last for
years, and it is to be commended to any purchaser who has to choose between buying the
“ENCYCLOPEDIA BRITANNICA ”in cloth and not buying it at all; but, in the nature of
things, it is not the best possible form in which to clothe such massive volumes as those of the
“ENCYCLOPEDIA BRITANNICA.”
Such a book deserves a leather binding, and the pleasure of using the volumes and of seeing them
upon one’s bookshelves is greatly enhanced when they are worthily attired. The intrinsic utility of a
book depends.in sdfme measure upon its comeliness, and the half morocco and full morocco bindings are
each in its degree so handsome and so inexpensive in proportion to their durability and beauty, that
no one who can afford to dress the best of books in the best of bindings will hesitate to select the richer
form of the “ENCYCLOPEDIA BRITANNICA.”
The leather employed in the full morocco and half morocco binding is genuine goatskin, sumach
tanned. This leather, clear in colour, elastic and soft, yet firm and fine in grain and texture, “resists
wear and tear and the action of gas and heated air better than any other leather.”—“ Ency. Brit., vol.
xiv., p. 538.)
The full morocco binding is, technically considered, an exquisite production. The backs are
double-sewn on four bands, and the quires are hand-folded. The cover boards are pressed and lined
to make the binding solid, and are squared and hand-cut by guillotine. The books are entirely for-
warded by hand with hollow backs and fine silk headbands set solid.
For purchasers who elect to make serial payments for the work, the better bindings make no more
urgent a call upon the purse than do the cloth binding. The monthly payments are in any case the
same, and only a little more perseverance is called for in the case of the leather bindings than in the
case of the cloth binding.
Unabridged and Unaltered.
The Ninth Edition of the. “ ENCYCLOPEDIA BRITANNICA,” completed in 1889, which the
Times has reprinted, is universally recognised as the most complete and perfect of works of re-
ference. The limes ” Reprint is in every respect the same as the copies already sold at the higher
price ;it is what booksellers call a new impression, not a changed edition. There has been no con-
densation, no abridgment, no omission. Every word, every illustration, every map, every element of
value is preserved intact; there is not the most minute cheapening of the product to offset against the
remarkable diminution of the price.

tricky grab at would-be employees’ pockets, the

Ex-Premier George Reid, having been
utterly damned by the N.S.W. Finance Commit-
tee as a man whose alleged surpluses were deficits,
and whose figures were bogus and faked, and
having been exposed generally as no Australian
Treasurer was ever exposed before, remarks
brightly that the awful report has “triumphantly
vindicated ” him. This is the second time George
has been vindicated. When he had his first historic
“surplus” he appointed a committee of three
bank managers to investigate it and guarantee its
genuineness, and they’reported, after careful in-
spection, that they weren’t prepared to guarantee
anything of the kind.- And the Unspeakable
George laid this painful document on the Assem
bly table, and said with a yell of triumph that lie
was “completely vindicated.”
Most of the anti-Federalists are now con-
centrating their crocodile grief on the theory that,
as the Federal constituencies will necessarily be
large, somewhat like the Vic. Leg. Council con-
stituencies, they will therefore become, like the
Vic. Leg. Council constituencies, a preserve for
rich Tories only. Which is just as sober reason-
ing as if it was alleged that because the man who
represents one large constituency has gout, there-
fore all men who represent large constituencies
will have gout—in fact that large electorates cause
gout. Vic. Council is Tory because it is elected
by property-owners, it is unpaid, only rich land-
lords are eligible, and it can’t be dissolved. And,
seeing that the Federal Parliament will be free
from every one of these drawbacks there is prac-
tically no more resemblance than between Chim-
borazo and Reid’s deficit, both of which are large
but not otherwise similar.

Labor party backed up the Govt, in the servile
waste of public time. And it was pointed out
that on the death of ex-Governor Hercules .Robin-
son—whom all Australia did respect —no official
notice was taken by the Assembly.

A Nervous System out of
Balance.
ST. VITUS* DANCE.
A Final and Decisive Cure.
The Case of
MISS FRANCES M. E. RALPH.
A Long Tale of Suffering and Succour.
Men folk may be divided in opinion as to
the wisdom of “Our Boys” having been
sent to the Boer war, but amongst the fair
sex enthusiasm has no limit. The ladies,
full of heartfelt emotion, crowd the piers
and wharves to hid farewell to our depart-
ing sons and wave a last goodbye. When
our reporter called on Miss Frances M. E.
Ralph, of IS Prentice-street, Inkeiman-
street, St. Kilda, at her pretty cottage resi-
dence, she was hurrying to be off and say a
last adieu to the Victorian contingent, yet
she was pleased to relate the details of her
exceptional experience :
“ As a young girl,” said Miss Ralph, “ I
had a great deal of worry and anxiety—far
too much for one of my age ; and when,
after her illness, my mother died, the grief
caused by that event entirely upset me,
seeming to throw my nervous system quite
out of balance, and so causing me an amount
of suffering awful to experience or to con-
template. I was treated by two doctors.
They said my complaint was melancholia.
I always wanted to shut myself up—away
from everybody—in some dark room, where
I could brood over my sorrows in a half
dazed, morbid way. It seemed that in
some way my mind and brain was partly
benumbed and not moving or thinking with
my own will at all. There was a dull,
heavy pain across my scalp; my heart
would flutter as though it were bursting,
and feel as heavy in my chest as a stone.
My digestion was all awry, and when I ate
there would he a feeling of fulness all over
me. But often I did not eat at all—my
appetite was so capricious; also, I was
troubled with chronic constipation. My
desire to eat was so erratic that for days
together I would not touch a bite ; then, a
sudden hunger coming on, I would eat too
much, and suffer for it. My nervous system
was quite disorganised ; I trembled and
started at the least thing, jmy thoughts
wandering, and fancying all kinds of gloomy
impossibilities. I thought and said things
ihat were not quite sane, and I could not be
held accountable for all my actions at that
time. A peculiarity of my condition was
that I thought everyone about me as foolish
as myself. A pain used to take me right
across the chest, and at times it would settle
in the left side. My teeth decayed and in-
creased the symptoms of my indigestion,
for I could not properly masticate my food.
This induced me to have them extracted by
a dentist. One of my nervous fears was
that someone was to Beize hold
of me. My sleep at night was hideous ; I
was less rested than when awake, for, while
I never entirely lost consciousness, my mind
was a prey to the most fearful illusions.
Misery seemed to be in front of me, and I
to be hurried there without hope of repreive,
and driven by indomitable fate. In the
morning I would be seized with a kind of
fit—a sort of St. Vitus’ dance ;my body
would break into a nervous, twitching mo-
tion from head to foot, a trembling and
spasmodic action of the muscles that I was
quite unable to control. I got very yellow
in appearance, like one suffering from jaun-
dice, and my skin had a shining look. Far
from getting thin, my face had a puffed out,
bloated appearance. My eyesight got quite
misty, and when I stooped down would be
blurred, and standing up again I would feel
quite dizzy. My memory quite deserted
me. I could hardly call a thing to mind
that I wanted to remember—my past was a
blank. Wind collected in my stomach,
forming into a ball, and rising in my chest
with a stifling oppression, till it; finally
burst, the wind escaping, and for the mo
ment I was relieved. My mouth was never
clean tasted ; my tongue was dry and
furred. My ears used to hear a constant
singing sound that appeared to buzz in my
brain. I had pains in the back, between
the shoulders ; but the most terrible was
that across the loins. Often it would quite
cripple me, forcing me to keep in a bent
position, for it was impossible for me to
stand or sit straight, Black rings formed
round my eyes, and incurable pimples and
blotches broke out over my skin, so that
altogether I was a pitiable object, Miser-
able when alone, yet not wanting company;
my right mind paralysed, and with the con*
stant fear for myself, and others for me*
that I should have to end my short remain-
ing days in some asylum, under forcible
restraint. I was treated for my disorder in
all kinds of ways. One doctor ordered me
spinal baths —a cold, douche that ran fiom
my head all down my back. Another, after
vainly trying to do me good at home,
ordered me to the hospital. fieie, because
my complaint was so largely mental, they
did not confine me to bed, but sought to
keep me busily employed, which seemed at
the time a strange thing to me, for 1 was a
paying patient. Yet there was no doubt
good leason in what they did. But I was
so fractious, so discontented, always beg-
ging to go home, that finally they let me
go, for I think 1 fairly weaiied them out.
I came home no better in any way, and with
the most hopeless prosptct ahead. I re-
member that I was always worse at the
change in the seasons. Though lam natu-
rally active, and glad to he busy about my
household duties, I had now to let them all
go. Things were anyhow. I had neither
heart nor strength nor will to do anything.
I was at this pass when I came across a
little pamphlet about a medicine called
Clements Tonic ; and, reading it through
with the curiosity of a sick person hoping
for relief from her trouble, I came to have a
faint hope that Clements Tonic might do me
good. I purchased a bottle, but the first
apparent effect was to make me worse ; it
was as though a combat was going on
throughout my system of two opp< sing
elements. But later on, when I had taken
more Clements Tonic, my improvement was
marked. The blood seemed to be actually
jumping through my veins, electric with
’ revivifying life. Combined with Clements
Tonic I took Dr. Fletcher’s pills, and the
two acting together worked a marvellous
change. My digestive functions were first
soon quite restored, I lost the back pains
and chest pains, the fearful nervousness and
the accompanying melancholia. My face
cleared, my mind cleared, and all the hor-
rible cobwebs of my diseased imagination
were in time swept away. The morning
fits of trembling and nervous twitchings in
a very short time no longer troubled me,
and alter a full course of Clements Tonic I
regained perfectly sound health, aDd was
able to eat, sleep and work in a normal and
natural way. And I may add further, that
though this took place some years ago, I
have had no return of my old symptoms, so
that my cure was no temporary easement,
but final and decisive. A friend of mine has
derived almost equal benefit from Clements
Tonic ; and a near and dear relative, who
suffered from great mental strain, has foui d
in this remedy the only effectual relief. Bis
grief is that he did not know of the virtues
of Clements Tonic years ago, so that he
might have saved the heavy and fruitless
doctor’s hills.”
“lama reporter,” said the man with the
note-book, “ and live by my ‘ copy.’ I hope
you have no objection, Miss Ralph.”
“Nob the least in the w’orld ! More than
once it has been in my mind to write an
account of my case myself. Print and pub-
lish what I say, by all means; and if any
reader should doubt let them call or send to
me, and I shall be pleased to Confirm all I
have said and to answer any pertinent ques-
tions, I am that grateful for the benefits
derived.”
STATUTORY DECLARATION.
I, Frances M. E. Ralph, ofiB Prentice-st., Inkerman-
St., St. Kilda, in the Colony of Victoria, do solemnly and
sincerely declare that I have carefully read the
annexed document, consisting of four folios, and con-
secutively numbered from one to four, and that it
contains and is a true and faithful account of my
illness and cure by Clements Tonic, and also contains
my full permission to publish in any manner my
statements—which I give voluntarily, and without
receiving any payment; and I make this solemn
declaration conscientiously believing the same to be
true, and by virtue of the provisions of an Act of the
Parliament of Victoria rendering persons making a
False Declaration punishable for wilful and corrupt
perjury.
Declared at St. Kilda, in the Colony of Victoria,
his 30th day of January, one thousand nine hundred,
before me,
J. GARDINER, J.P.,
A Justice of the Peace in and for the Central Baili-
wick of the Colony of Victoria,

A “Bulletin” Story.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
This is a true Bulletin story, and as the'moral is
somewhat derogatory, I have hope of its seeing
print. The moral comes first, for a change, and
it strenuously advises you not to keep your back-
numbers about the house.
The stranger struck Abe’s selection when the
sun was straddling Bald Head. He dropped
his bundle by the front-door, sank down in the
shade, gasping, and lay still whilst the hens pro-
spected inquisitively amongst his rags, and Tige,
the cattle-dog, sniffed him over resentfully, and
the red calf chewed his boot.
Abe found him in this condition, and helped
him into the kitchen. He had tea with the family
that evening, and ate a great deal, and drank fou
pints. The feed made a new man of the wanderer,
and developed in him a vein of cheery optimism.
He talked right along till midnight. He knew
Abe’s family in Lancashire, and iiad heard of Mrs.
Abe’s people in London. He had been every-
where and everything, from university professor to
cook’s understudy on a guano-boat, and he knew
of all things on the earth, in the waters below,
and the heavens above. He smoked Abe’s tobacco
with beatific disregard of cost, and beguiled Mrs.
Abe, mysteriously, and much to her own after-
amazement, into bringing forth the precious
brandy from the medicine-chest—a kerosene-box
behind the bedroom door. He said his name had
been Andy Johns for this last month or so,- and
before the brandy was finished he distinctly
remembered meeting Mrs. Abe’s brother Tom at
Kimberley, and believed he had been mates with
a cousin of Abe’s in the West.
Abe made up a bed for the guest in the spare
room. When he returned to the kitchen after
bidding Mr. Johns good-night, he looked sheep-
ishly over at Mrs. Abe. Mrs. Abe made a
heroic effort to look as if she had no such thing as
a suspicion about her, and the two retired in an
unusually thoughtful mood.
Andy Johns was up bright and early in the
morning, and ate a big breakfast, and when Abe
was shuffling about preparatory to moving off to
his job at Oldham’s Johns said :
“So long, old man. Don’t you mind me; I’ll
be all right. I see you’ve got a big pile of old
Bulletins in my room. I’ll just go over them, if
you don’t mind.”
Abe gasped and walked away dubiously, pausing
once or twice before reaching the track as if to
turn back and move Mr. Johns out; but Abe was
a soft and rather diffident man, and nothing was
done that day to incommode the stranger within
their gate.
Mr. Johns spent the day amongst The Bulle-
tins, and in the evening was full of gay prattle
about The Bulletin and Bulletin writers, one of
the most conspicuous and gifted amongst whom, it
was hinted, was the speaker himself, who wrote over
an alias. The stranger declared that he ha d earned
a great deal as an author at one time, and was cer-
tain he could do as much again under the stimu-
lating influence of three meals a day, and in the
calm of those peaceful surroundings. Complete
quiet and generous feeling, he said, he had always
found absolutely essential to sustained intellectual
effort. He would begin contributing again pre-
sently, and the first cheque that came along—well,
he was not an ungrateful man. Did it occur to
either of them that he was the kind of man that
would be likely to forget a kindness? No, no ; those
who had shared their little with him would be
his chosen companions in prosperity. Meanwhile
it was necessary for him to familiarise himself
again with The Bulletin style, and he intended
continuing his study of those back-numbers next
day.
Mr. Johns spent the second day and the third day
over the back-numbers, and Abe, who had made
up his mind a dozen times to clear him out, and
had failed miserably in the face of the sunny smile
and bland manner of his guest, was developing a
morbid condition of self-contempt, and would sit
out between the chimneys brooding, whilst Johns
lolled comfortably indoors, poring over those old
Bulletins. On the fourth night Abe conceived a
plan. He stole into the spare room, seized
about a hundred papers from the bottom
of the pile, dropped them out of the
window, climbed out after them, and then
carried them away and hid them in a hollow tree,
returning to the kitchen with the air of a con-
victed thief. Before Sunday came, Abe made two
more shameful, surreptitious visits to the spare
room, and got away with nearly the whole of The
Bulletins, leaving only those Mr. Johns had
read. The visitor made no comment on the dis-
appearance of the papers, but devoted Sunday to
the composition of a poem, for which, he said, he
would receive ten pounds by return of post, and
Abe wasted his day of rest mooning about fret-
fully, trying to make up his mind to eject the
poet, and failing pitifully.
That evening Mr. Johns gave Abe an envelope
addressed to the editor of The Bulletin. The
envelope was supposed to contain the .ten-pound
poem, and Abe was expected to post it m the
township next morning.
“ We’ll have the cheque along in less’n a week,”
said Mr. Johns.
When the unwelcome stranger had retired for
the night, Abe took up the envelope, and glanced
dubiously over at Mrs. Abe. Mrs. Abe nodded
her head, and a large, hard thumb went through
the wrapper. There was no poem—nothing, in
fact, but a few sheets of old print.
That was the last straw. Abe rose to the occa-
sion, morally and physically. Quietly, but with a
steely glare in his eye and an ominous rigidity of
the jaw, he stole to the tool-box, armed him-
self with hammer and nails, and, going to the door
of the spare-room, began to nail it up. He drove
in about a pound of nails before he was satisfied,
and then paused for the remonstrance; but Mr.
Johns spoke never a word.
“You can leave by the window, mate—or stay
where you are, and be damned to you ! ” said Abe
through the keyhole ; after which he went to bed.
Next morning, the poet appeared at the kitchen
door, carrying his swag.
“I’m thinlcin’ about going,” he said. “No,
Mrs. Abe, I will not stop to breakfast. No, no—
thanks very much, all the same.”
He moved away, but returned presently, and
addressed them through the open window.
“ About our little account. You can take what
I owe you out of that Bulletin cheque, when it
comes, and send the rest on to me. Address
Menzies, Melbourne.”
al hat was the last they saw of hi nr.
SILAS SNELL.
When Li Hung Chang was in Glasgow, a
few years back, he asked one of the members of
the “Clyde Trust” (honorary position) what
salary he got, and was told none. ‘‘Then,’ asked
Li, pointing to a diamond ring and a gold watch-
chain, “ how did you get these'/ ”
Lizzie.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I saw Lizzie yesterday. I have been helping to
clean-up dirty Sydney, and in a Chinaman’s back-
yard in Wexford-street—l saw Lizzie! God !
how she has altered ! When she was my wife (I
suppose she is my wife still) she had laughing blue
eyes, and was as fair as a lily, and clean and tidy
always. Now, eyes like big black saucers ; yellow
as a sovereign ; dirty, stupid-looking, and her hair
about her face like the shredding stringy-bark.
And while I was there the pig-tailed Chow came
out and said to her: “ Wha’ fo’ you come heah ?
Go in ! ”
Ordered about by a Chinkee !
It was not long after I married Lizzie and took
her to my little farm that she cleared out and left
me. And—it all came of living in a gully.
There was no one for Lizzie to talk to; the
floods carried away my crops, and I was cranky
(everybody who lives in a gully gets cranky), and
so Lizzie left me. I sold-out and tried to find her,
but I couldn’t, and my money went, and I’ve been
humping my swag nearly ever since.
I wonder how Lizzie has spent her time ! I
wonder if she knew me ! I looked at her long
enough—but then she seemed stupid-like.
Well, now I’m revenged, and I should howl
with delight. And yet—and yet!—there’s some-
thing in my throat that—curse me if my eyes are
not wet !
Never mind—there’s a pub. near where they
sell o.p. rum—fierce, fiery o.p. rum—that’s the
thing to drive away thought!
Poor Lizzie !

A Braidwood (N.S. W.) railway deputation
explained, _ t’other day, that, in that thinly-popu-
lated district, there were over 50 residents whose
ages were 80 and upwards ; that a Braidwood boy
of 90 had ridden 30 miles a day with ease ; and
that an enthusiast of over 80 had walked 10 miles
to a railway meeting, and back, without incon-
venience. Braidwood is the sort of place where
doctors sW've and undertakers despair.
“ Alpar ” : A man police - courted in
N.S. W. for shooting a retriever dog, “in mistake
for a dingo,” called two men, one of whom valued
the dog at 55., and the other at 7s. 6d. Owner
stated that he would not have taken £5. He wis
allowed 6s. Now, I contend that an animal is
worth—not what somebody would give for him—
but what the owner will take. For instance, a
lady of my acquaintance drives a quiet old horse
she wouldn’t take £5 for, though a horse-expert
would probably value him at about ss. Now, if I
shot that horse “in mistake for a kangaroo,”
would it be just for me to part up only a crown ?
[The principle wouldn’t work. A. may possess—
say, a lock of hair —for which, though of abso-
lutely no intrinsic value, he would not take hun-
dreds of pounds ; but it is not to be supposed that
8., should he accidentally destroy this, is to be
therefore held liable for hundreds of pounds.
Besides, the man who swears when his dog has been
killed that “he wouldn’t have taken £5 for him”
is often the same man’who would have rushed at
an offer of 20s.]
When Paul Marfcinetti was at Sydney
Tivoli he created amusement by leading his valet
about the stage by the ear. The thing is as old as
the hills, but it always raises a roar. To appre-
ciate the real humour of the action it requires to
be seen in actual life. Writer saw it thus
for the first time last week. It was on William-
street (Sydney) tram, and the big, blue-faced,
testy gentleman with the aggressive jaw and pug.
shave had been compelled to draw the attention
of no less than four apparent gentlemen to the fact
that, after entering, they had left the front door
open, and that a bitterly cold draught was blowing
in on a young woman with an infant in her arms.
When the fifth men entered, left the door open,
and got into a corner whei;>the draught would not
reach him, the blue-faced man lost his temper.
He cursed loudly, seized the offender by the ear,
jerked him across the car and hissed at him :
“ Shut that door, you mangy whelp ; don’t yer see
the lydy’s in ther draught, you hog ! ” The
offender shut the door, and the blue-faced man sat
down. The offending passenger fell down. After
all, despite his prize-fighter’s shave and his lan-
guage tne blue-faced man was the one real gentle-
man in the car.
Private Inglis in a letter from the front
(published in Melbourne Age) :
My leg was broken and I lay in great pain for some
time. By-and-bye, a Boer, whose left hand had been
amputated, approached me and asked me if I wanted
anything. I asked for water. In response the man drew
from his pouch a cake, which, with water, he offered me.
I took both eagerly, and he then inquired whether I
would like some peaches, so I had a feed of peaches. The
sun was beating down fiercely upon me, and the Boer
essayed to move me to the shade of a clump of bush. I
had to request him to leave me where I was for fear my
injured leg might be irreparably damaged. He im-
mediately desisted, and walked some yards away to where
a heavy boulder lay. He managed to roll it up by my head,
and then for a solid three-quarters of an hour he sat on it
to keep the rays of the sun from me. Towards dusk the
British ambulance came along, and I stood a big chance
of being missed by them, so I showed my Boer friend how
to coo-ee. His effort was anything but a perfect imitation
but it had the desired effect, and I was lifted into the
waggon.
As a beautiful contrast to this read the sentiments
of the flippant Private Daly, of Queensland, who
describes how he found a wounded Boer on the
veldt and gave him a drink. “He,” continues
Daly,
implored us to take him to some farmhouse, which was
impossible for us, but we told him that if we could get
him help on the way we would send it. But, alas ! we
could get none. I felt sorry for poor old Dutchy. I often
wonder what became of him. Probably if some of those
vultures which hover over the veldt could speak they
might tell a doleful tale of his last hours of life.
Women and Marriage.
HINTS FOR CHOOSING A WIFE.
It is not always the prettiest women that are the most
popular with the sterner sex. You will often find that
one of the plainest girls, if she be good-natured, light-
hearted, and witty, will be most sought after by the men.
\\ hen it comes to marriage, a careful man takes pains
that the women of his choice are healthy and robust; he
considers it a duty he owes to himself and to posterity
that his companion in life should be as nearly perfect,
from a physical standpoint, as possible. Many a man’s
life has been ruined by marriage with a frail and delicate
woman. It is these sort of matches that find
marriage a failure. . Not that the woman is so
much to blame, as in many cases she does not
know of a remedy for her ills. A woman who is
run-down, nervous or irritable, is not a helpmeet to a
man in any way. If she suffers in this way, sooner or
later she will be taken with fainting-spells, dizziness, and
backaches, accompanied by a heavy’, crowded feeling in
the abdomen. There is no need to suffer from any of
these ailments when Bile Beans are close at hand. They
act gently (as all medicines for women should), and are,
therefore, to be recommended. Bile Beans put the liver
in a perfect condition, and prevent the ducts con-
necting the various digestive organs from becoming
stopped up. In this way they prevent biliousness.
When a cure, however, is required, they cause the
liver to secrete increased quantities of this fluid,
and thus flush the whole digestive organs and carry
off all waste matter from the system, clearing off at the
same time all surplus bile. Once tried (they are only
13Jd. per large box), they will be found beneficial, and
their use continued the system will be toned up, the
appetite returned, the circulation improved, and general
health restored. A healthy woman is always interesting;
she is happy, and her spirits are contagious. Good
health and good nature are inseparable ; secure the first
by’ using Bile Beans for Biliousness and the latter will take
care of itself.
Not a Lily Langtree.
If there is one thing more than another that Lily Lang-
tree is noticed for it is her complexion. Unfortunately
we see in the streets every day young ladies who are not
Lily Langtrees, who, in other words, have pimples on the
face and an unhealthy, pasty look. The dark look under
the eyes which accompanies this paleness is a sure sign of
an unhealthy body with amende conditions in the system.
The healthy girl has a fresh, rosy complexion which is
admired. Let us inquire into the reason of these very
opposite conditions. Healthy blood is absolutely essential
to a good complexion. Many girls with almost perfect
features are annoyed at finding that they are neither
pretty or attractive. They think their trouble is their
face, when it is really their liver. To have good blood
the liver must be in working order, the stomach able to
perform its part of the contract, and the person must be
free from all digestive trouble. Bile Beans are com-
pounded expressly to bring about these conditions, and
girls with a pasty white skin cannot do better
than try them. Once the organs of the body are
in good order and the functions of the body carried out
regularly, the skin will assume a beautiful color without
the aid of cosmetics. Bile Beans will have made the blood
pure, and the result will be shown on the surface. Their
action is gentle, there is no griping, purging, and no evil
after effects. Their formula is purely vegetable, and
su’h drastic ingredients as aloes, calomel, and mercury,
found in most purgatives,are emphatically and positively
not employed even in the smallest degree. Occasion may
come when you wish a medicine to thoroughly search out
and get to the root of a digestive ailment. If it does, re-
member Bile Beans are the people’s popular-priced pre-
paration, and they cure when all other remedies fail.
Those who are troubled with pimples should try’ a course
of Bile Beans, and the young lady taking them may be
assured that her complexion will at least be improved,
if not as fascinating as Lily Langtree.
