The Correction.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
There’s a rumpus, sweet and pretty,
Such a rare old row !
For the Mongol’s getting gritty
* In the North just now ;
And he says the frantic fellow,
In a crazy battle-bellow,
That his Land is for the Yellow,
For the pure-bred Chow !
And the Briton and the Prussian
Stand in blank amaze ;
And the Frenchman and the Russian
How they gasp and gaze !
And says Bull: “By all that’s clever,
Kaiser Billy—did you ever ? ”
And says Billy : No—l never !
Not in all my days ! ”
“ We must bring the Chow to reason,”
So the whole gang swear ;
“We mu t whale him for a season,
But we won’t say where ;
We must show him that our mission
Is to clarify his vision,
Or he'll go to pure perdition,
If he don’t take care.
“ We must clothe his pagan body
In our own sweet style ;
We must waste our lovely shoddy
On his carcase vile ;
And when war has made its exit
From his country—we’ll annex it,
That the rebel may not vex it
With his grievous guile.
“ For the Mongol in his blindness
Is a heathen blear ;
And he pays us for our kindness
In a way most queer;
In return for all our mercies,
Opium-wars and such reverses,
Why, he plugs us with his curses
And his Maxims dear !
“ So there’ll be a little friction
In the North just now ;
But we hope to bring conviction
To the rampant Chow ;
And, by blowing him asunder,
And dsliv’ring him to plunder,
We'll correct the blazing blunder
Of his pig-tailed fow ! ”
Q.
P.L.
Shimmy’s Ghost.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
We had the ballast in and everything snug, and at
about 7 o’clock were lopping down Sydney harbor.
There was just a suspicion of a land breeze, and
as we got abreast of Dawes Point the sails and
gear yawed and yapped in the wash of the ferry-
boats. The skipper—a sallow, hidebound ’Water
man— was at the wheel, while the crew—mate,
cook, and two A.B’s., all Russian I'inns sat play-
ing knifey on the hatch.
One ot the crew lounged across to the star-
board rail and hastily darted back exclaiming,
“Dere is Shimmy—he’s dere sitting by hitnselef
on der buoy.” Then he hastily swung himself
below and the rest of the men tumbled after him.
• • * *
I asked the skipper what was up.
“Oh, it’s nothing,” he replied in an uncon-
cerned sort of way, and all the time he peered
across at the buoy and fumbled among the spokes.
“These —*- flatheads gets up to all sorts of
ideas, and they’re all —— scared of Jimmy.
What, did you never hear tell about Jimmy the
Dane? The most curious things about Jimmy
was his hands—like noo claws on a growed-up
crab. But he was a rummy, natty sort of a chap,
and was a real terror for their doodl’em sweeps.
He used to go and fool round with them clair-
voyantes, and get advice about sweeps. Never got
nothing for years. But one day he comes scooting
acrost to where we lay at Pyrmont, and took all
the chaps out of every craft he could find above
water in the bay, and kep’ them blind drunk for a
week almost. Then he brings up with a rush, and
turns sober’s a judge and swears off everything—
says he has £9OO left and is going to buy a
cutter for pearling up in the North.
* * * *
“He used to go round in cabs all day with
these ’ere broker chaps, a probing and poking in
all the crafts in the harbor. Terrible feller to
pick about garboard-strakes with a penknife, was
limmy When he wasn’t looking for a vessel he
was getting shaved. ’Struth ! he used to reg’lar
haunt a little barber’s up there in the Rocks.
The barber, yer see, was a knowing chap
with horses, and Jimmy used to sit there
and get shaved two or three times a day. Ot
course, if a chap has a bit of ready and wants to get
scraped right bare to the frames, I s’pose it don t
matter, but this’ere barber had a wife, and she had
a sister—a big, tall, horse-faced sorter piece ; and
of course, knowing as Jimmy had a bit, what’s she
do but makes a shy at him ! I expect Jimmy
thought to himself how he’d be going away and
all that, and how he’d get lonesome and all that,
so damn me, if he don’t up and marry her !
There was a devil of a row at the church because
Jimmy didn’t believe in nothing, and got a-testing
of the joints of the altar with his penknife to see
if cobra was in ’em.
m * * *
‘The day after he got spliced Jimmy made up
his mind very sudden about a craft. A long,
narrow-gutted old hooker she was. He got a
chap—a reglar quiverin’ sort of old cove—l never
did see any cove quiver like he uster—to fix up
the cabin a bit and make everything snug. But
when Jimmy brings his missus down to see the
ship she turns up her nose and says she wouldn't
trust her life in a thing like that to go to Port
Darwin. So it was fixed up that the ole woman
she was to go north in the A.U.S.N., and Jimmy
was to follow with the hooker.
“Jimmy books her passage and takes all her
fixin’s aboard the steamer. Then he hurries away
to get his own clearance-papers. You know how
a cove feels when his wife has left him on his
own ? Well, Jimmy had been going unilaterally
straight, so he reckons jist to have one more
shave. The barber cove gets talking and talking,
and Jimmy gets making arrangements about
sweeps and y so g on till the Customs closing-time
slips by; and, it being Saturday, o£ c°ui« e Jimmy
was tied-up till'Monday. Them all ™To
what’s Jimmy do but invite the barber chap to
comedown aboard the craft and keep
panv over Sunday. ‘ There’s plenty of grub and
so on,’ says Jimmy. ’Tanyrate, s° o ’ i s cl c
time, down comes the barber. Jimmy got out the
whisky and they had a good time.
“In the morning it was low-tide, and about
half-a-dozen of them ’ere drownded dogs 3 was
a-sticking up their legs right alongside. Of
course, the barber chap, lie had a nnssu > ‘ .
wasn’t use to this ’ere ships bender busmess
Hadn’t no stummick, you see. He f r °™v
about the smell. The crew were aHaway s me-
where, so Jimmy says, “ If you can gimme a hand
to get a rag or two on her we 11 lop down the 1 ar-
bour a bit. They had an awful job, but at last
brings up in Rose Bay. .
“ They got in on the whisky again. The barber
chap gets worser all the time, and about o
in the afternoon begins to rave terrible. Jimmy
ups and tackles him, and at last gets him snooze
down in his bunk. Then he thought it good
enough to have a bit of a doze himself f eck - ,
“Something fumbling round his , chln Jl? k ®
Jimmy, and there was the barber chap wyth a
razor' trying to shave him. I think .l
tole yer how partial Jimmy was to have
a shave; but, as he afterwards tole me,
when you see a cove with eyes like two port-lig it.
and all afoaming at the mouth, it kind of klUs ? J
any hankerings after titivation. Jimmy . >
and as scared as a whale with a thresher insight,
trying to think what he could do. All the time
tlie barber chap was darting and diving and
dancing and prancing round, swooping the razor
about terrible. At last Jimmy thinks about
sweeps, and gets talking about era, and after a
while the barber starts babbling about somestift
’un, and how he’d lay 100 to 22-you know the
yabber. Jimmy wanted a drink terrible, sa at
last lie ventures to get up and reach for t •
But soon’s he turned the barber starts a-stroppmg
the razor on the standing-gear, and makes for hu
again worse than ever.
“Well, to make a long story short, Jimmy
spent two days there without a bite of food or a
wink of sleep, dodging the barber around the
galley and deck-fittings, and every time this ere
barber chap passes a bit of gear he stropped the
razor on it and started every strand.
“ One of the tug coves tells the quivering chap
that the ship was in Rose Bay. It seems Jimmy
hadn’t paid the quiverin’ cove for the nggin and
soon, and naturally the old feller wanted to see
Jimmy terrible bad. ’Tanyrate, he goes down m
a boat and rescues Jimmy. The quiyenn chap
cried like a infant when he saw how the barber
had ruined all the gear, and he rushed at the
barber and tied him tip —razor and all—in sucn a
—complicated knot that it’s a miracle
he ever got out of it.
. * * * *
“ Jimmy never seemed the same after that. He
loafed round for a couple of weeks. ihen he
seemed to make up his mind sudden like, and he
got me and the chaps together for a start.
“It was one of these ’ere dull, dirty sort of
mornin’s, with a scowlin’ sky and just a show ot
feather in the sou’-easter. Jimmy came aboard
looking awful—tole the mate his wife had bucked
on the trip at Brisbane some passenger had tole
her about women up in the Straits. He seemed
short and hasty-tempered. The hooker slipped
into irons right in the fairway. The cook—a big
six-foot-six fiathead—didn’t hear him callin for a
haul, or was sulky or .something.. 1 any rate,
Jimrnv rushed into the galley and tried to haul
the cook out. The galley was one of these little
deck cabooses about three-foot-six square, with
a red-hot stove in it. Somehow m the scuttle
the door gets closed ; and I promise yer the row
in that galley when first one and then the other
bumped against the stove, and spilled the boiling
water around, was like hell let loose, but all ot a
sudden the side gives way, and out both of them
rolls clutching each other’s throats and bleeding
and smoking •all over. Before we could tear em
apart, flop over the rail they goes, jabbing and tear-
ing at each other. We run into the wind again and
gets the dingy out. After a' long time, just in a
whirlpool of blood and bubbles, up comes Pete,
looking awful. Jimmy didn’t come up at all.
The police they picked him up with the drags three
days afterwards —hooked his hand and he comes
up with one arm raised like a parson.
• * * *
“ Jimmy—oh ! he sits there cos he likes to
watch the traffic, I suppose. Pete sailed in the
Bandicoot, come a mucker on the Buccra bar
rolled the masts out of her—came ashore as
flat’s a pancake.”
N.S.W.
W.H.C.
The red-tapey Hopson T. Walker, barris-
ter, who is retiring from the office of Chief Clerk
of Yic. Supreme Court, was the first person who
held that office, which was created by the Judica-
ture Act of ’B3. During his brief career as prac-
tising barrister Walker had one remarkable case.
Before trying his luck in Victoria he went
to Vancouver, and there became acquainted
with a member of the local Parliament named
Green. Years after Green and Walker met in
Melbourne, where Green got into an awful
mess, and had to seek the aid of his old Vancouver
chum. It was like this : One morning in the early
seventies an engineer, named Reynolds, who had
an office in chambers in Queen-street, found, on
his arrival, a parcel addressed to him lying on the
window-sill, close to his office door. He took it
up and proceeded to open it, when it exploded
and killed him. It was a box, and the fragments
were picked up and a portion of the superscrip-
tion found intact—one or two words. It became
known that Green had business relations with
Reynolds, and that the latter held some “ paper ”
of his. The late Supt. Winch had Green
arrested, and tried to prove that his hand-
writing was on the box by which Reynolds met his
death. Green was tried for murder. Walker
defended him, and became advocate and witness.
When the Crown case was closed and the defence
opened, Walker went from the bar into the box
and told the jury how he had known Green in
Vancouver, and that he was a respectable man
and'no murderer. The jury disagreed. Green
was tried again, and the jury disagreed again.
He was tried a third time and the jury acquitted
him. Walker has distinguished himself in another
way. Although an Anglican of most “Evan-
gelical” type, he was instrumental in making
legal in Vic. bequests for masses for their souls by
R. C. testators. A case of this sort came, before
Walker as chief clerk, and he refused to pass the
money, and referred the matter to the Court.
Queerly enough it came before Justice Hodges,
who is Chancellor of the local Anglican Diocese,
and Hodges held that the old English act against
such bequests was not in force in Victoria.
